* uaoie, July 26, 1901.
t Report to the Committee of the Distress Fund for
boutn African Women and Children, by Emily Hobhouse
London, 1901. ' ’
t I remarked, “But surely that report about eating
horseflesh .Wasn’t a deliberate lie ?—and when a garrison
.arets down to that things must be at a low ebb.” “ Oh
yes,” replied a trooper, “ we had a go at the horses ; but’
bless you, there were herds of cattle grazing on the flat
here all the time !” “Then why, in the name of all
thats foolish, did you do it?” I asked naturally.
Orders, ’ lie replied, with a charmingly expressive
shrug. As a in itter of fact, about the only article thev
really feit the want of badly was good flour, and that was
only towards the end.—Berth Morning Herald corres-
pondent at Mafeking, Oct., I960.
The whole of the Mafeking business was a “ frost ” I
was for iour months in the neighbourhood with Pluiiier’s
force, and Plumer was so anxious about the condition of the
besieged, he was so Certain that thev were starving that
he economised Ilia own provisions, and for months before
the relief we were on a biscuit a day and half-rations When
Mafeking was relieved it turned out that Baden-Powell
had provisions for a year, and there had been no want
Plumer was awfully mad. Be sent a few waggons of food
and stores into the town just for appearance sake, and
to make a show for the paper-men ; hut he was awfully
wild.—M.L. trooper in Loud. Morning Leader, quoted by
Sydney Telegraph, duly, 1901. •
(And Sydney noodles, on the faith of plainly-lying
tables, solemnly subscribed sev eral hundred pounds and
presented horses and a sword of honour (!) to the bogus
hero of the bogus siege—-which included “ the night sortie
from Mafeking, in which no single Boer was killed, though
the most blood-curdling details of hand-to-hand slaughter
were vouched for by the garrison.”—Lond. Timeu corres-
pondent, Sept. 14, 1900.)
* The poverty here is really appalling. You can buy a
woman for a tin of jam, and many good women have been
reduced to sell their virtue to feed their children. These
are Dutch women whose husbands are in Ceylon or else-
where.-Trooper with Brabant’s Horse, in Barrier Truth
(NIS.YV.), Maroh’ls,l9ol.

THE RED PAGE .
U.R.
PUBLIC NOTICE.
It is hereby notified for information
that unless the men at present on com-
mando belonging to families in the town
and district of Krugersdorp surrender
themselves, and hand in their arms to
the Imperial authorities by July 20,
the whole of their property will be
confiscated, AND "THEIR, FAMILIES
TURNED OUT DESTITUTE AND
HOMELESS.
By Order,
G. H. M. RITCHIE,
Captain K. HORSE,
Krugersdorp, Dist. Sup. Police.
July 9, 1900.
SO low has Britain sunk ! True ; the
notice was cancelled a week later, by
another and similar notice which
omits the last and worst clause. But
who doubts that the clause was enforced ?
During the year since passed the African war
has been essentially a war against women
and children. British soldiers have become
farm-burners, house-looters, home-wreckers;
and almost the whole non-combatant popu-
lation of the Transvaal and the Orange Free
State has been herded and hustled and
driven into guarded camps; with the result
that 14,561 men, 27,573 women, and 42,599
children were being fed by the British in
June*—fed miserably, but fed lest even the
British conscience be roused by the atrocity
of the war. This system of concentrating a
desperate population has now been estab-
lished for nearly a year; and its results
are deadly indeed. If the object were
the utter extermination of the Boer
race—man, woman, and child—a better
method could hardly have been devised with
any pretence of civilised conduct to disguise
its horrors. In some of the early camps
women and children died like flies. At
Bloemfontein camp the death-rate in the
early months of this year was at the rate of
250 per 1000 per annum, t (The normal
Australasian death-rate is 12 per 1000 per
annum.) Even in June, when the manage-
ment of the camps had possibly improved,
the official return admitted a death-rate of
95 per 1000 per annum. How could it be
less ? When Miss Hobhouse, under per-
mit from Milner and Kitchener, visited
the O.F.S. Cape Colony camps, she
found the women and children sleeping on
the ground, without proper clothes, without
suitable or sufficient food, without sufficient
fuel even for cooking; for the most part
huddled in tents, with sometimes as many as
10 or 12 persons in a tent; without proper
medical attendance; without milk enough
for even the youngest children. No wonder
she writes :
I oall this camp-system a wholesale cruelty. It
can never be wiped out of the memories of the
people. It presses hardest on the children. They
droop in the terrible heat, and with the insuffi-
cient, unsuitable food; whatever you do, what-
ever the authorities do, and they are, I believe,
doing their best with very limited means, it is all
only a miserable patch upon a great ill. Thousands,
physically unfit, are placed in conditions of life
which they have not strength to endure. In front
of them is blank ruin. There are cases, too, in
which whole families are severed and scattered
they don’t know where.
And the daily ration allowed when Miaa
Hobhouse saw the camps was this—
Meat, |lb. (with bone and fat)
Coffee, 2oz.
Wholemeal, fib.
Condensed milk, one-twelfth of tin.
Sugar, 2oz.
Salt, |oz.
No more. Of the quality of the rations,
often condemned by Miss Hobhouse, there
is a hint in the report by Dr. Johnson regard-
ing the Johannesburg ration (printed in
Loud. Daily JSiews last April), where he
declares samples of meal mouldy, and sugar
unfit for children, tending to cause diarrhoea.
In only one camp—Mafeking—could Miss
Hobhouse say a good word for the food—
The rations are better than in any other camp,
accidental!'>/ > long a story to dilate upon m
this short letter. £
From the contaminated source of their
-London cable-agency, itself dependent on
“news” doctored or invented by the
Rhodesian press-gang, and further revised
by a British military censor, the Australian
daily press received this message, printed
June 22 last— *
London, Friday.—There are now twelve Boer
rerugee, or concentration camps, besides nine for
natives, in the Orange River Colony, requiring
weekly importations of 88 tons of food-stuffs.
, " e average mortality is at the rate of 116 per
thousand per annum. The high rate is largely a
result of Boer disregard for sanitation, and the
jioer women s neglect of their children.
No more despicable lie was ever uttbred or
printed. It was a lie on the face of it. Left
to themselves, the Boers are notoriously one
of the healthiest and longest-lived races on
earth. But when thousands of women and
children are driven from their homes, wire-
fenced in collections of crowded tents, left
to sleep on the ground without proper food,
without sufficient fuel, without adequate
nursing or medical provision and convenience,
what can you expect but wholesale disease
and death ? And to say that Boer women
neglect their children ! —why, a hundred
testimonies prove family affection a chief
Boer characteristic. Let Miss Hobhouse, as
an eye-witness, tell what is meant by “ Boer
disregard of sanitation.” She writes, on
June 1, 1900, from Bloemfontein, capital of
the 0.F.5., where the camp was dumped
down two miles from the town on the bare
veldt-
£ reat blunder was made by bringing an extra
people into Bloemfontein camp, already
known to be unhealthy and full of fever.
Among the things pressing hardest, and which
tend to undermine the health and constitutions of
the women, are the following :
Lack of Fuel.—lmagine three small sticks of
W °cn * nc^es l°ug, or small stony coal enough
to fill the well of a soup-plate, for daily cooking.
-*• be weekly baking becomes almost impossible,
and often the meat cannot be cooked, and the
bread is sodden because underbaked. . . .
Lack of Beds and Mattresses.—Only a few have
beds or mattresses—the great majority lie on the
ground. Even if each tent had a bed, it would
not accommodate more than one or two Inhabi-
tants of the tent. Meanwhile, the damp of the
ground, the occasional streams of rain that run
through, the draughty night air coming beneath
the nap of the tent, combine to lower the health
of the children and to kill them off in convalescent
and delicate stages.
Lack of Soap.—This necessary was not given in
any camp. After much urging and requisitioning,
a very occasional and quite insufficient quantity is
now doled out.
Diet.—The food is monotonous, and does not
suit children. Some vegetable diet is greatly
needed. It presses hard when the meat (as often)
is maggotty, and the coffee coppery and undrink-
able.
Water. —In Bloemfontein the supply is in-
sufficient, and it is also bad. The clothes of thou-
sands have for months been washed in a small
dam of stagnant water only occasionally freshened
by rain. It is foul. Many other camps need
wash-houses.
Overcrowding. . , .
Scarcity of clothing. . . .
Sanitary Accommodation.—This is very in-
adequate to the number of people. They are
separate for men and women, but otherwise wholly
without privacy, open to the sun and rain. Where
'properly looked after by the authorities all is sweet
and clean, but elsewhere, notably Bloemfontein, the
effluvia is terrible, making it impossible to
approach within fifty yards, unless with nose and
mouth tied up. The effluvia reaching one side of
the camp makes those tents at times unbearable,
and has resulted in tonsilitis and various throat
troubles. The people feel these places a terrible
degradation.
And these people are nominally British
subjects—their country annexed a year ago !
Now half the nation is taken captive, forced
into unhealthy camps, fed on bad food, un-
provided with beds and clothing, deprived of
a sufficiency of water and fuel, and then
charged—helpless as they are—with disre-
garding sanitation and neglecting children,
because they naturally die (i.e., are mur-
dered) in the circumstances. What mon-
strosity of meanness and patriotic ” malice !
So low has Britain fallen.
Such war on women and children has
never before been made by ,a nation calling
itself civilised. Even Weyler, in Cuba, was
not more cruel. Even the atrocities in
China were not more cruel. Chinese men
and women were murdered or ravished, but
the children were left to live in their homes.
What practical difference is there between
murder and rape outright, and murder of
children by certain disease, rape of women
by inevitable dishonour when they are forced
to sell themselves to gain bread.*
Ala guerre comme ala guerre ! Yes ; but
make war on men, not on women, not on
children. Kill the men ; kill the youths ;
choke the last cry for Liberty in the throat
of tiie last Boer patriot -but surely this
slaughter of weaklings must make every
honest Englishman blush for his country.
It is no use declaring that the women
sheltered fighters, or that they are collected
for protection ; —the war has gone past that.
Kitchener has beer, for months burning
farms and wrecking towns away from railway
lines, away from districts where the Boers
are recruiting,—with the deliberate inten-
tion of stabbing the Boers through their
wives and children. What other motive
is open 1 Many of the herded women have
friends in Cape Colony : they are not allowed
to go to them ; they are not allowed, though
“ British subjects,” to live freely in town-
ships under British rule and distant from
the scene of war ; —they are kept prisoners in
order that anxiety on their behalf may induce
their husbands and fathers to lay down their
arms. What foolish miscalculation ! As if
this worst outrage of the war would not
madden the Boers to fight till death.
Yet British and Australians, forsooth !
must acquiesce in this barbarous method of
warfare because it is necessary to “ see the
thing through” to “save the Empire.”
History may yet show that the very worst
foes of the British Empire are those within its
own borders who have abetted the schemes of
Rhodes and Chamberlain, and shrieked or
sat silent while the two brave little Republics
were being throttled out of existence. _ His-
tory may yet show that the very worst foes
of the British Empire are those who clamour
to extend its huge territory at any cost of
peace or justice, at any risk of lighting
world-wide hatred, at any risk of destroying
all that the British Empire has gained from
the past. Even now it is being brought home
to the average Briton that the wisdom in this
matter lay with men like Spencer, Bryce, and
Morley, and folly with the Jingo Govern-
ment. Besides the immense cost of the war
in money and lives, it has cost the apparently
eternal alienation of the Dutch in Africa ;
it has intensified the European hatred of
Britain ; and, in its aims and its conduct, it
has permanently lowered British national
reputation and character.
These are the points stressed by John M.
Robertson in e his book, “ Wrecking the Em-
pire,” recently published in London by
Grant Richards. This book collects a series
of letters written from Africa; and, as a
book, it should have been systematised and
re-written. Apparently publication was
hurried to secure the effect of a party pam-
phlet ; for the book is chiefly partisan— i.e.,
it is the book of a man who has taken his
side, and who gives attention more especially
to the facts supporting that side. It is a
book of details and incidents, with prin-
ciples taken for granted or justified in-
cidentally ; and the writer’s temper is rather
advocate’s than judge’s. That Robertson’s
view is probably right, and his matter appar-
ently correct, makes no difference to this
summary of his attitude. And he points
out very truly that—
The procedure of martial law was sufficiently
well-fitted to destroy the allegiance of the Dutch
in our own colonies; the policy of universal de-
struction in the Republics, involving the utter
misery of many thousands of women and chil-
dren, is fitted to build up through Dutch South
Africa a passionate • unity of racial antagonism
to everything English, such as never could
have been accomplished by the most zeal-
ous Dutch propaganda, if such propaganda
should have been attempted. This is Em-
pire-wrecking par excellence. . . . We are com-
mitted to a task of destruction such as Eng-
land has not put her hand to, I think, for three
hundred years. A people which will not yield is
to be fought until there are no adult males left in
the field to fight; and then we are to undertake to
prepare the remnant and their children to be
“colonists” under the British flag. It is the
most insane political undertaking of modem times
—far more insane than the determination of
George 111 to conquer the American colonies;
for there was a large loyalist population, whereas
in the two Republics there are certainly not a
thousand Dutch who would raise a finger for the
British flag; and in the two colonies, in all likeli-
hood, there will soon be no more.
That is a sound judgment soundly ex-
pressed. England is sowing the wind to
reap the whirlwind ; and her worst foes at
this day are those unintelligent “ patriots ”
who shriek detestation of the Boer, and are
prepared to go any lengths in support of the
Ministry which made the war. It will be
History’s revenge to show that the true, the
only patriots in England at this day are
those who continuously have warned the
nation that persistence in extolling private
greed and public injustice have opened,
what Bismarck prophesied, the grave of the
British Empire in Africa.
There is no reference to Australia in
Robertson’s book, which is recommended to
all desirous of learning the real facts of the
war—of which so little can be learnt in this
country from the daily press misled by corres-
pondence from tainted sources. But we
already know that many Australians lured
away in the contingents are thoroughly sick
and ashamed of their ignoble task ; and we
have heard their testimony that the Boers
are a race valiant and honourable, not
less worthy of respect than any race on
earth. Indeed, they are now attesting with
their lives the words of a great Englishman
at a heroic period of England’s history—that
Pitt who, when the Jingoes of a century ago
were bringing calamity on the Empire, de-
clared in the House of Lords : “If I were
an American as I am an Englishman, while
a foreign troop was landed in my country, I
never would lay down my arms—never,
never, never ! ”
It is no wonder that Europe stands amazed
at the desperate resistance of the Boers to
overwhelming force. The world’s history
can show no more glorious defiance for the
sake of Country and Liberty. Those grey-
beard fathers and stripling sons who died
together in the trenches to beat back foreign
arms have become as immortal as the Spar-
tan band whom Leonidas led, or the little
band of Texans who perished in the Alamo.
Their courage will nerve hearts and
strengthen arms for many a century to come •
wherever Freedom is prized men will take
hats off and speak of them proudly—and
humbly. They have proved themselves
worthy of their Dutch forefathers—the men
who resisted Spain on land more signally and
more tenaciously than England resisted her
at sea. And now it is England who plays
Spain’s part, and at the height of empire
attacks Liberty for the sake of greed and
aggrandisement. Absit omen !
THE BOOKFELIiOW.
»
TO A FOILED REVOLTED OR REVOLTRESS.
Courage! my brother or my sister!
Keep on! Liberty is to be subserved, whatever
occurs !
That is nothing that is quelled by one or two
failures, or any number of failures,
Or by the indifference or ingratitude of the people, or
by any unfaithfulness,
Or the show of the tushes of power , soldiers, cannon,
penal statutes.
What we believe in waits latent for ever through all
the continents, and all the islands and archi-
pelagoes of the sea.
What we believe in invites no one, promises nothing,
sits in calmness and light, is positive and com-
posed, knows no discouragement,
Waiting patiently, waiting its time.
The battle rages with many a loud alarm, and fre-
quent advance and retreat,
The infidel triumphs—or supposes he triumphs,
The prison, scaffold, garrote, handcuffs, iron neck-
lace and anklet, lead-balls, do their work,
The named and unnamed heroes pass to other
spheres,
The great speakers and writers are exiled^—they lie
sick in distant lands,
The cause is asleep—the strongest throats are still,
choked with their own blood.
The young men drop their eyelashes toward the
ground when they meet;
But, for all this, Liberty has not gone out of the
place, nor the infidel entered into possession.
When Liberty goes out of a place, it is not the first
to go, nor the second or third to go,
It waits for all the rest to go—is is the last.
When there are no more memories of heroes and
martyrs,
And when all life and all the souls of men and
women are discharged from any part of the
earth,
Then only shall Liberty be discharged from that part
of the earth,
And the infidel and the tyrant come into possession
Then courage ! revolter / revoltress /
For till all ceases neither must you cease.
Ido not know what you are for, (I do not know
what lam for myself, nor what anything-is for,)
But I will search carefully for it even in being
foiled,
In defeat, poverty, imprisonment— for they too are
great. , f
Did we think victory great t
So it is—But now it seems to me, when it cannot be
helped, that defeat is great,
And that death and dismay are great.
WALT WHITMAN.

mixtures for the cure of a really
troublesome cough. These mixtures
usually contain opiates that soothe and
quiet the cough temporarily, hut do not
cure it. They also frequently disturb
digestion and thus do more harm than
good. What is needed is a remedy that
while having a soothing and healing effect
upon the mucous membrane of the lungs
and air passages, will at the same time
promote appetite, aid digestion, and build
up the health and strength, putting the
system in a condition to resist disease.
Nothing is equal to Angler’s Petroleum
Emulsion for accomplishing this result,
and we are constantly receiving reports
from all over the English speaking world,
showing its remarkable power to relieve
and cure coughs and colds and all chronic
lung affections.

Various Verses.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
"Laugh, let us Laugh.”
Laugh ! let us laugh,
To-day, to-day.
Mock laws, break straws,
To-day, to-day.
Let us keep faith endlessly,
Let us face death friendlessly,
Just you and I,
To-day—and alway.
Weep ! let us weep,
To-day, to-day.
So brief is grief,
Even grief won’t stay.
Let us grieve with heavy heart,
Let us ween as though to part,
Just you and I,
To-day—and alway.
Love ! let us love,
To-day, to-day.
Wild, w ;i d, my child,
To-day, to-day,
Let us love, child, shockingly,
Laugh, mock, love mockingly,
Or love like sad death,
To-day—and alway.
LOUISE MACK.
Sheelah—A Song.
When Sheelah in the morning
Comes down the way,
It needs no more adorning
To make it gay ;
The stones upon the street,
Sure they kiss her feet.”
She dresses all in green,
And that’s no sin ;
And she wears like any Queen
What she stands in.
If she had not a shawl—
Sure Sheelah’s under all.
She looks at me so shyly
With dark-grey eyes j
She looks at me so slyly
In sweet surprise;
And, when she passes on,
My heart she treads upon.
The world is full of girls,
Men say to me ;
The sea is full of pearls,
My pearl is she ;
Though other pearls there be,
She is the pearl for me.
When Sheelah, some fine morning,
Walks down the way,
She ’ll vanish without warning,
And what will I say ?
I’ll say : “O Saints, be true !
Sheelah, is it you ? ”
O Sheelah, Sheelah,
Sheelah, Gframachree!
In all the world of girls
She’s the one girl for me.
N.S.W.
VICTOR J. DALEY.
The Old Land.
I gave my hand when the birds built—
For life, come gain or loss—
To the keeping true of a strong man,
In the land of the Southern Cross.
My dear ones all are bold and free
Within its wide sea-walls;
Yet there are times when far bells ring,
And a fading echo calls.
0, far land 1 foregone land, I never more may see,
Until I cross in the phantom ship that sets vain
longings free.
The grey spire of the minster-tower,
The dog-rose in the hedge,
The poppies red ’mid yellow corn,
The lark’s nest in the sedge ;
The dim-lit haunts of busy men,
By feet of centuries worn
My heart is fainting, yearning still,
For the land where I was born.
O, far land ! 0, dear land, I never more may see,
Until I cross in the phantom ship that sets vain
longings free.
Australia ! land of generous pulse,
I loved thee as my own :
The Queen of all the Southern Seas
Upon thy grey Bush throne ;
The sons I bore will serve thee well—
They knew no other love ;
But my worn heart beats to and fro
As to the Ark the dove.
O far land 1 Omy land, I never more may see
Until I sail in the phantom ship that sets vain
longings free.
N.S.W.
JOHN CAREW.
Reading a moral in the Conway case,
M.L. Govt, has just issued stringent regulations
under the Sale of Poisons Act. Anyone buying
strychnine or arsenic has not only to give his name
and address and the purpose for which the poison
is bought, but must also state where it is to be
kept and when and where it is to be used.

Notice to Subscribers in Victoria, S. Australia,
Westralia and Maoriland.
In consequence of the imposition of One Halfpenny
postage on Interprovincial Newspapers, the annual sub-
scription outside N.S. Wales (except Queensland) is now
£l, with 2s. 2d. added for postage.
No newspaper can be sent unless the subscription is
prepaid. There will be no exception to this rule.
To Subscribers in Queensland.
Owing to the imposition of 1 id. postage, the subscription
to The Bulletin to Queensland will be £1 6s. 6d. per
annum, payable in advance.
To ease off in some degree the burden of the unreasonable
postage — \fd. on a Bulletin of ordinary weight—now
exacted by the Queensland Post Office, a special Thynne-
paper edition o/Tiie Bulletin is now sent to Queensland.
NOTICE.
By sending direct to Xn® Bulletin Office Twenty
Shillings sterling—with postage added where required—
which you may pay by Post Office Order, Postal Note,
One Pound Note, or stamps of any Australian province,
you can have The Bulletin posted regularly to you every
week for a year.
Cheques, Drafts, etc., and of]. Business Communications
to be addressed to William Macleod, managing director.

Archibald.
Special Notice.
The whole contents of this paper are copyright by the
publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
“ Correspondence” column all contributions submitted,
whether in the form of Political, Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in length,
or, say, 8000 words, are specially acceptable), Paragraphs,
Letters, or Newspaper clippings. All communications
will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when the
address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that we
are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.
To A mateurs in JtlaeJc and White.
Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous
or other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient
merit and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or
any interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduction
— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn—will be paid
for as a suggestion only. The conditions under which
such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, AUGUST 17, 1901.
The Anti-Alien Bill.
The Federal Government has introduced its
Immigration Restriction Bill, and Bruce
Smith, M.H.R., describes it as a “hypo-
crisy.” In that respect there is an unusual
correctness about Bruce Smith, M.H.R.
The Immigration Restriction Bill is rather
more than a plain hypocrisy ; it almost rises
to the dignity of a lie. At the same time it
is a lie forced on us by Joseph Chamberlain
and the British Ministry generally ; and the
man who has any large dealings with Joseph
Chamberlain must lie or be a hypocrite—
or else get left. The object of the immigra-
tion Restriction Bill is to keep out paupers,
diseased persons, and, above all, 800,000,000
closely adjacent niggers with whom the
white Australian can’t intermarry or associate
without lowering the national type. Every
one knows this : but in order to satisfy a
ridiculously hypocritical pretence that no-
body wants to maintain the purity of the
Anglo-Saxon type, and that the race which
considers itself the best and strongest and
most intellectual on this earth isn’t worth
preserving in a pure state, the persons barred
out by the Federal Immigration Restriction
Bill are mainly these :
Any person likely, in the opinion of the Minister or of
an officer to become a charge upon the public, or upon
any public or charitable institution.
Any idiot or insane person.
Any person suffering from an infectious or contagious
disease of a loathsome or dangerous character.
Any person who, when asked to do so by an officer.
FAILS TO WRITE OUT AND SIGN IN THE PRESENCE OF THE OFFICER
A PASSAGE OF FIFTY WORDS IN LENGTH IN THE ENGLISH LAN-
GUAGE, DICTATED BY THE OFFICER.
* » * •
Of course, it is not intended to apply this
test indiscriminately. If every immigrant was
required to write out with reasonable cor-
rectness a difficult passage from an English
author, half the British arrivals, and almost
all the Germans and Scandinavians and
other peoples of Continental Europe, would
be shut out equally with the Kanaka and the
Chinaman. The Hindoo would get through
just about as frequently as the Frenchman
and the Italian, and the Act would rain alike
on the desirable and the undesirable. So
the Bill does not, apparently, make the test
quite compulsory. The immigrant who
can’t write out the passage in ques-
tion, when ashed to do so by an officer
shall be an ejected person ; but it isn’t
set forth that the officer must ask him to do
so. The apparent idea is that the authori-
ties will ask the man they want to keep out,
and the man they want to let in won’t be
asked. And the difficulty of the passage
will probably be proportioned to the intensity
of the desire to keep the immigrant out ; the
most undesirable man will get the longest
polysyllables. It appears to be a law which
will work well so long as the people keep an
anti-nigger Government in power, with a
strong anti-nigger majority behind it, and
keep a sleepless eye on both of them. Of
course, this can’t be avoided. A measure of
such a kind, if applied automatically, would
bar out the bulk of our desirable European
immigrants. The only way is to make it
strong enough to bar out almost everybody
when applied, to leave such scope that
it may be applied or not, at the
discretion of the people in authority,
and then to make sure, by perpetual
vigilance, that the right people are in
authority, and that the law is applied in the
right place. Some of the best of the Aus-
tralian population consists of well-educated
German, Scandinavian, and Danish immi-
grants, and if they had been confronted on
arrival with a long passage from Macaulay
with “ transubstantiation ” in it, read to
them hurriedly by an unintelligent officer
with a powerful Aberdeen or cockney or
Cork accent, they would have trooped
almost unanimously back to their respective
Fatherlands.
* • * *
It is announced, apparently on official
authority, that the British Government will
assent to this kind of Bill, and will not
assent to any other kind—at least, not with-
out enormously lengthy argument. The
brown and black and yellow Powers would
be offended if their people were shut out
because they are black and brown and yellow,
and Britain, it appears, can no longer afford
to offend the varied niggers of the globe
with its old aggressive courage. Of course,
the black and brown and yellow Powers
know well enough that the educational test
is intended, arid will be used, to bar out
their subjects and for no other purpose.
They know that their subjects are to be
barred out simply because they are people
with whom Europeans, can’t inter-marry
without doing irreparable damage to their
racial type, and without becoming akin to
the mongrel breeds of the Levant and the
Asiatic ports, whom the Anglo-Saxon regards
with such unutterable scorn. Also because
they are cheap toilers, content to live under
conditions which lower our aggregate civili-
sation. And, further, because Australia
isn’t large enough to lift Asia to its own
ideals, even if Asia wanted to be lifted, which,
being quite content with its own ideals, it
doesn’t, and even if Australia was in the
lifting business, which it isn’t. And Aus-
tralia knows all these things also. Likewise
Britain knows them. But by a process of
mutual hypocrisy it is to be understood that
none of these parties know anything.
In fact, it is to be understood by all concerned
that the only question at issue is the preser-
vation of the British spelling and punctuation
in all its natural beauty, and that Australia
considers it essential to bar out the person
whose commas are mixed and whose semi-
colons are unreliable. The tacit agreement
is that there is no awful example before us
in the shape of the color trouble in the
Southern United States, and any ill-feeling
that exists there arises solely through the
inability of the negro population to correctly
write out extracts from the masterpieces of
American literature. It is understood that
the European type is not worth preserving ;
and the European civilisation doesn’t matter,
and if the traditions of the Anglo-Saxon
race are lost in a general mongrelising
process it is a thing of no conse-
quence. The Eurasian is quite a de-
sirable person ; and the idea that the Briton
treats the “ nigger ” and the half-breed with
any degree of scorn, and doesn’t look on the
black man as his equaJ, is all a mistake. All
the teachings of science and experience may
go to show that a black-and-white community,
divided against itself by a color-line, is a
a failure, and a black-and-white community
which loses its color line and becomes a
mongrel is a worse failure ; but the fact must
not be mentioned. The British Government
insists on preserving appearances in the
matter, regardless of the fact that appear-
ances deceive no one, and that they would
be a most disreputable set of appearances
if they were preserved. In the plenitude of
its wisdom it says that if Australia pretends
to be so unspeakable a fool as not to know
the value of its white status it may preserve
its white status by underhand means. As
the British Government is master of the
situation it is probably as well to follow the
line of least resistance and let it have its
way.
* * * *
It is reported that a large (and honest)
section of the Federal House of Representa-
tives is opposed to the Barton Government’s
Bill, and intends to procure its rejection, if
possible. This section desires a Bill which
will directly exclude the Asiatic and African
and Polynesian, and not get at its result by
the devious means and false pretences of a
scared and diplomatic Judas Chamberlain.
The principles of these individuals are
admirable, but they appear more upright
than practicable. There are at least three
good, worldly, useful, and unprincipled
reasons in favor of the Barton Govern-
ment's Bill, as follows :
(1) It is comprehensive enough to exclude almost any-
body or everybody if properly applied. It will be the
business of Parliament and the people to see that it is
applied in the right place for all it is worth. There isn’t
one colored alien in 100,000 who can write to dictation a
passage of 50 words from an English author—if a suffi-
ciently difficult passage is selected, and it is dictated fast
enough, and badly enough, and the authorities are suffi-
ciently rigid about the degree of accuracy which consti-
tutes "writing the aforesaid passage “ within the meaning
of the Act.” Let the customs officer at each port fully
understand that he will lose his billet on a charge of
drunkenness or general inefficiency if two black or
yellow or brown people manage to write that passage of
50 words in the same year, and things will go pretty well.
(2.) Apparently we can ge t this kind of anti-Alien Act at
once. If the Federal Parliament passes the straight and
honest kind of Act, years may be wasted in obtaining the
sanction of the British Government. And the Asiatic
will be pouring in all the while.
(3) When this Act is secured there is absolutely nothing
to prevent this country passing an amending Act which
will shut out Asiatics, Africans, and Polynesians en manse.
It might take years, as above-mentioned, to secure the
British Government’s assent to it —but the Asiatic won’t
be pouring in all the while (if that passage of 50 words is
read to him by a sufficiently hostile officer), and that is a
diiference of some importance.
It is good to be honest, but in this world
it pays better to be successful. Australia
can achieve a reform of immense import-
ance by adopting Joseph Chamberlain’s
mean, devious, and rather cowardly subter-
fuge, and time is pressing. Also this country
isn’t the keeper of J. Chamberlain’s con-
science. There are many unclean things
lying about in Australia, but the horror of
having Chamberlain’s conscience on these
premises has been spared us.
The Sweated Producer.
The Australian daily papers lately published the
depressing and alarming news that a British 00.
in the Bovril, or some other extract-of-meat line of
business, lias decided not to open a factory in
Australia. It sent a representative out here, and
the representative was shocked, if not stunned, by
the Socialistic inclinations of the people, the ten-
dency to revolutionary legislation, the old-age
pensions, the early-closing, the minimum-wage
ideas, and the upheaval of labor generally. He
announced that his company is off to Argentina,
where this dreadful state of things does not exist.
And in certain circles the fact that another great
absentee concern is not going to get a lien on this
country causes much grief. The warning of the
extract-of-meat gentleman, prior to his departure,
was that, unless Australia is much more humble,
and accepts much less wages, it can’t expect to
get the job of supplying England with “ vittles ”
as a regular thing. England will get its proven-
der from Argentina, and this country can go and
perish miserably.
* * * •
On the whole it seems that this country is just
as well without the foreign capitalist in question.
The greatest curse that has fallen on Australia
thus far is the investment of foreign capital—the
investment of the absentee’s money as distin-
guished from the money of the man who comes
here along with his cash, and becomes a resident
investor. The investment of English capital in
Australia simply means more money owing by
Australia to England—just as the investment of
the pawnbroker’s capital in anybody’s house
means that there is a bill of sale over his furni-
ture. The influx of the British money-lender’s
money into this writer’s country, and the influx of
the Mont de Piete’s money into his house are the
same thing—debt. And debt isn’t a desirable cir-
cumstance. The rich countries of the world are
those which own their own industries —such coun-
tries as the United States,, Britain, Germany, and
Erance, where foreign capital-isn’t being invested
nowadays. The poor countries of the world
are those which, to a great extent, don’t
own their own industries —countries like India,
Mexico, many of the South American States,
South Africa, Egypt, and the other places to
which there is a great and beneficent influx of
foreign capital. The country whicli sends capital
to another country is the professional lender; the
country which receives it is the professional bor-
rower. If anyone can show that the borrower is
habitually richer than the lender, then the influx
of capital is a good thing—not otherwise. The
most prosperous State in Australasia just now is
Maoriland—the State which, by protective duties,
absentee tax, and other devices, most systematic-
ally discourages the foreign investor and helps
along the local one instead. The most conspicu-
ously decaying section of the British empire is the
one where the investment of outside capital has
been carried to the greatest extent—lreland, the
land of the absentee landlord, otherwise the man
who invested outside capital in the country for
the purchase of the earth’s surface. It took a
great influx of the absentee man’s capital into
Ireland to reduce it to its present pitiable condi-
tion, but so long as that influx is large enough any
country may attain to Ireland’s condition in
course of time.
» * * *
When any extraneous capitalists propose to
come here and start a new industry—whether it
is a Great Boulder mine, or a Union Bank, or a
South Australian co., or an Australian Agricul-
tural co. (with its big Newcastle coal mines), or a
Sunlight Soap co., or a meat extract establishment,
or a lot of branch insurance offices, or a shipping
concern, or anything else, it always implies that
the profits will go to the country where the out-
side capitalists are, and the wages for plain, hard
toil will come here. Then let anyone consider
how many men he knows who ever got rich on
mere wages, no matter how economical they were,
and decide how gaudy are the chances of the
country where there is a great influx of foreign
capital. If the influx of foreign capital is only large
enough, it means that all the work of being boss be-
longs to the outside investor, and the work of being
the employee, at 30s. a week, is left to the native
of the land. Every foreign-owned industry which
starts in Australia and takes its profits out of the
country means so much the less room for a
locally-owned industry which will spend its profits
in the country. Britain didn’t grovv rich by
having foreign capital invested in Britain, but by
investing British capital abroad, And until it
grew rich enough to invest its capital abroad it
remorselessly shut out, by Protective duties and
Navigation Laws and other devices, the foreign
capitalist who wanted to get a lien on Britain.
And when the British capitalist tells 11s that what
is best of all for Australia is an abundant influx of
the absentee’s money, and lots of debt, and a
stupendous mortgage on the country—well, it is
better to do as the Briton did when he was in an
undeveloped state just as we are, rather than as
he says.
* * • *
Yet the recent visitor who decided not to start
his absentee-owned industry here, but to go td
Argentina where labor is cheaper, spoke quite
truly when he said that the business of supplying
the British market will probably depart from us if
we persist inourcareei of laborlegislation. Austra-
lia is, on the whole, a rather barren country where
a man’s labor, applied to primary industries, pro-
duces less from a given area than it would in many
other countries. Further, Australia’s produce
has to pay a higher freight over a longer distance
tlian that of other countries, before it reaches
the European market. Therefore, to hold our
own on the British market with such rising com-
petitors as Argentina (which has already beaten
us badly, in quantity, as a supplier of wool and
meat) it is necessary to put our produce on board
ship at least as cheaply as, probably rather more
cheaply than, the Argentine does, and that can’t
be done if the industries are put on the wage basis
which the Australian democratic party hopes for.
As a matter of fact, the industries in which Aus-
tralia does its greatest export trade—wool, wheat,
butter, cheese, meat, &c., —do not pay anything
like O’Sullivan’s ideal minimum wageof7s. a day
for eight hours labor, and thechances are that there
would be no great export trade if they did. A
comparatively barren country can’t pay the
highest wages and the heaviest freights, and yet
compete in the European markets with the pro-
duce of countries where wages are far lower, dis-
tances shorter, freights cheaper, and where,
through superior soil, the same amount of work
produces more results. If a large section of the
community is going to exist by tearing produce
out of the ground to supply the British market, it
must work at a wage which will enable it to com-
pete in that market against all comers. And if
the country is more heavily burdened with foreign
debt- with interest on public loans, dividends to
foreign investors of all sorts, and payments to all
kinds of absentees—than some cheap foreign
competitor, then, so far as each man’s share of
that extra interest bill goes, he has to be a still
cheaper worker than the competitor in question.
* * * »
Australia must send to Britain enough produce
to pay for the produce it buys from Britain, and
enough over to pay the interest on its public and
private debts, rents to absentee landlords, divi-
dends to absentee banks and insurance companies,
profits to miscellaneous absentees of all sorts. Of
course it pays a lot of the interest just now by
fresh borrowings, but that is only a temporary
shelving of the problem. It must supply the
produce at prices which will enable it to
compete with rival countries, and it must
supply more of it, in proportion to popula-
tion, than any other country on earth, because, in
proportion to population, it owes more to Britain
than any other country on earth. That is why
even the most ardent reformers in this country
hesitate about applying the eight-hour system and
the minimum-wage idea to the industries which
supply our chief exports. These industries struggle
through mostly with 10 or 12 hours work per day,
and wages that are so miserable that the workers
are prepared to desert wholesale that they may go
and slaughter complete strangers in a distant land
at two-thirds of O’Sullivan’s minimum pay. By
running the great exporting industries on a
hideous system of sweating, and subsidising the
community generally with employment on public
works at good wages paid out of borrowed money,
this country has pulled through so far. But it
can’t do that for ever.
* * * *
And the remedy ? Well, there is no remedy,
except to put an end to the foreign tribute, to pay
which Australia has to place every year on the
English market some £26,000,000 worth of pro-
ducts, obtained by low-priced labor in order that
they may be cheap enough to be saleable. Aus-
tralia must cease from foreign borrowing. It must
cease from local borrowing, unless it can devise
some method whereby the bonds sold locally won’t
drift abroad and become foreign debt in the
end. It must set to work to repay its foreign
debt. It must systematically extinguish the
foreign investor in Australia, whether he
is land-owner, money-lender, or manufacturer, but
it is no good discouraging the latter unless the
local manufacturer is encouraged in every possible
way to take his place. It must, by every possible
means, reduce its imports, and that can only be
done by learning to supply its own wants—unless
the country relapses into a lower state of civilisa-
tion and learns to do without things now regarded
as indispensable, which is not at all desirable.
When a country reduces its imports, as the
United States lias systematically done, by build-
ing up great local industries, it means wealth ;
when it reduces its imports, as N.S.W. did in the
days after the boom, without any correspondii g
increase of local production, it only means
poverty. When Australia has no huge annual
bill to pay in England to the public and
private money-lender, and the great aggregate
absentee, and the British manufacturer, t!i en
it won’t be compelled to compete with the
Asiatic or the cheap Argentine half-breed on the
English market as it has now. But it can only
get to that point by a more complete system of
Protection than even Victoria has yet attempted.
* * * •
Unfortunately the Labour party in Australian
politics, though it worries itself to death if one
pick-and-shovel man on a drainage contract is
sweated by his boss, apparently cares very little if
all Australia is sweated and enslaved by the
foreign money-lender. It never, except in the
most perfunctory manner, tries to establish a
policy of financial independence. It goes mad
about a sweated individual; a sweated continent
is a thing it doesn’t notice. The Fat Man’s posi-
tion is brutal and logical. He says simply:
“ While things remain as they are we must send
to England some 25 or 30 million pounds’ worth
of something annually to pay interest and rents
and absentees’ profits, and to pay for our imports.
We send most of this in raw materials, for we have
nothing else to send. We must supply these cheaply
enough to compete with rivals in the same line, such
as the cheap half-breed of Argentina —and this
despite our greater distances and heavier freights.
We can do this while we pay miserably low wages
in our great exporting industries. lam quite con-
tent to pay miserably low wages.” The democrat
isn’t content to pay miserably low wages, so if he
was equally logical the first items of his creed
would be the abolition of borrowing, the repay-
ment of our foreign debt, the extinction of the
absentee investor in every shape and form,
scientific Protection, and the creation, by every
possible means, of local industries. Unless ne
adopts this policy it is tolerably certain that
something will break when the law endeavors to
enforce fair hours and fair wages in the great ex-
porting industries of Australia. It may not break
immediately—a great deal of staving-off can lie
done by a vigorous O’SuLLiVAN-esque loan and
public work's policy—but it will almost certainly
break in the long run. Perhaps it will be just as
well if it does.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
Federal Defence.
“Viking” uses some language that warms this
paper’s heart :
I note with pleasure your remarks re Federal defence
(20/7/’l). The Bill is all right, but where is the man that
highly-placed politicians will allow to enforce its salutary
provisions? Have you ever heard the cogent reasons
that can be given for retaining the “ inestimable ser-
vices ” of Col. Blank when that officer is about 10 years
over the age-limit? Any soldier knows that if the Col. is
persona grata —even though he be gouty and senile—h s
loss “ at this juncture ” would be a “ national calamity.”
While there is power to retain time-expired officers for
special reasons, those special reasons will always be forth-
coming in the case of a man who belongs to the same
club as, say, the Minister for Economy.
Every regimental commander who holds command for
m >re than the proper time blocks the promotion of every
officer in the regiment, and while they stay blocked they
are creeping nearer to the age-limit for their respective
ranks. Extending the term for the O.C. means decreas-
ing the age-limit for his juniors : robbing Peter to feed
Paul’s vanity. And hope deferred maketh the heart
sick.
In no regiment where promotion has been at a stand-
still for five years is there any efficiency whatsoever, save
in numbers perhaps, or on paper, or in the cooked
musketry-returns.
The cheapest, because the best, branch of our forces
should be the partially-paid, provided that it is run by a
man who knows his duty and is courageous and free
enough to do it. The pay need not be excessive, but the
best available men should be enlisted for a term certain,
and taught, and compelled to do their best when en-
rolled. The weeds that now disfigure the ranks do not
look like soldiers —a minor affair—nor do they resemble,
nor are they, men ; which is a very tough bullet for the
public to chew. They cannot drill, they cannot
manoeuvre, and, print it in big letters, THEY CANNOT
ENDURE. Cigarette-heart and premature lewdness do
not make for grit, and a soldier without grit is a poor
thing. But we cannot blame the medical officers. Look
at the chain of causes. Capt. Brown’s company, nomin-
ally 60 strong, puts 40 decent-looking men on parade.
Capt. Smith’s company musters 63 hollow-chested, cough-
ing objects in uniform. The adjutant passes the time of
day with Brown as he collects the absentee reports.
Then the 0.0. reproves Brown, coram publico, for lack of
“zeal.” Zeal is a mystic word with O.’sO. ; almost as
mystic as “ tact.” Brown, cursing inwardly, tells the
O.C. that “ it won’t occur again,” and at once fills up his
company with nondescripts. Ten full companies of
physical and moral wrecks make a full regiment and a
proud colonel.
Then the officer who thinks that his alleged men will
follow him through Hell’s gates if he be “ popular,” must
be carefully pole-axed by a reliable fool-killer. Popu-
larity is at present earned by taking your men out for
exercise and letting them lie down and smoke cheap
cigarettes, because “you must not overwork the men, y’
know.” The popularity of officers is nothing like the
same incentive to valour as a righteous cause that is near
to the hearts of the army. Witness Cromwell’s Ironsides
and Washington’s rebels.
The volunteers would be valuable if stripped of all
“frill.” Men do not generally stay long in that force,
but there is a constant stream passing through the ranks,
and they all carry away some knowledge of soldiering. I
contemplate writing a work in 26 vols. (revolving book-
stand extra), on the uses and abuses of rifle clubs, and so
refrain from comment here.
Consider some reforms : Staff-officers should keep rea-
sonably sober, and everyone should turn out with clean
boots, even if the occasion be only a royal visit. Soldier-
ing must be looked at as a serious business, and not as a
coxcomb’s recreation. “ Banjo’s” suggestion that troops
should be sent for a week’s march, away from a railway
line, instead of to a ridiculous camp, is most excellent.
Mess-dress and review order should be abolished, except
for regulars. It would save even the infantry subaltern a
trifle of £3O. That is not much if you say it quickly, but
it means a saving of £lO5O to the officers of each foot
regiment. Officers of other arms would possibly be able
to live on the income they would save.
Dreams —idle dreams ! There are 20 men in Australia
who can plan an army. Have we one who will build to
the design ?

The Gambling 1 Hypocrisy.
Australia is not the only country where Betting
Acts and other legislative checks upon the
“ gambling evil ” are essentially hypocritical.
The makers and guardians of the constitution in
Europe and America transgress against the spirit
of their gaming laws, and are just as contented
with their own humbug, as the official moralists
who disapprove of pitch-and-toss under the British
flag. But seeing that Australia’s first Federal
Parliament threatens to oust the harmless
consultation from its last stronghold in this
country The Bulletin may as well pub-
lish two striking illustrations of the Law’s
hypocrisy. They happen to be photographs of
social scenes in Melbourne, where Commonwealth
law is manufactured. One of them shows the
members of the respectable Athenaeum Club at
dinner—periodical house dinner—on the eve of the
recent V.R.C. meeting. A Supreme Court Judge
in the chair raps the table hard and announces
that a Calcutta Sweep on the G. N. Hurdle
Race is about to be perpetrated. Again he
knocks the table and commands “Order!”
for the auction “drawing” of the sweep, where-
upon the high-toned citizens proceed to
gamble furiously. As much as £(5O is paid by a
punter for one horse’s chance in the sweep, whilst
the learned Judge beams through his glasses
upon the speculation. The Federal Government
proposes to make it impossible for that clubman
to send five shillings to “ Tattersall ” through the
post. Photo. No. 2 shows a group of card-players
in a Melbourne suburban court-house, _ adjoining
the town-hall. The local Mayor is giving a ball
in the municipal building, and the court-house is
converted for the night into a smoking-room, with
card-tables placed around for the accommodation
of gambling guests. Green-baize covered tables,
allee same as those found in the “ silver hells ”
which get raided by the police occasionally. An
inspector of police presides over the principal
card-table in the court-house. They are playing
‘ poker.’ And the exalted myrmidon of the law
makes some joking reference to the fact that his
“boss,” Minister Trenwith, is looking over his
shoulder. The possibility of an M.P., armed with
a straight flush, winning a pot of money from a
four-aced inspector of police in a suburban court-
house is worth thinking about.

A Proposition of Unemployment
Two meetings of Melbourne citizens —at Bruns-
wick and Collingwood—lately passed, and sub-
mitted to Premier Peacock, resolutions to the
effect that “ it is the first duty of a Government
to find work for those persons who are out of
employment.” They might have added without
any risk of straining the truth that it was also the
first sign of common sense on the part of a
Government to find work for the unemployed.
The reason is plain enough. The aggregate
income of a country arises through production,
and if a section of the community can’t get a
chance to produce anything, then the country is so
much the poorer. The unemployed individual is
producing nothing, and he is consuming some-
thing, or he wouldn’t be an unemployed;
instead he would be a corpse. He is there-
fore a loss to the community and that loss makes
the public revenue less ; it makes shop-keepers’
and merchants’ profits less, it lowers rents,
it compels the Government to put up the income-
tax in order to keep the accounts square, it helps
to prevent the Commercial Bank paying a divi-
dend on its ordinary shares, and it does damage in
every way. In a well-ordered community there
are no unemployed; if the work of supplying the
whole community can’t keep the whole com-
munity busy, then there is a leakage somewhere,
and it is the business of Premier Peacock, or any
other man similarly placed, to find it out. If he
can’t find it out at his present salary, then it is his
duty to inform the country at what salary he
thinks he can find it out. The difficulty can’t be
settled by the crude process of raising a big loan
and paying wages to the unemployed out of that
to do something more or less useful or useless.
There is no more sense or solvency or
ultimate prosperity in paying out of loans
for producing a country’s supply of railways
and bridges than in paying out of loans for its
supply of wheat or wool or boots. One is as much
the production of ordinary necessaries as the
other. It is the first business of the Government
to find work for the unemployed by putting things
on such a basis that there need not be any unem-
ployed, and therefore the indignation meetings
that were recently held in Melbourne struck a
great truth, and when the Argus and similar
organs ridiculed the idea they merely displayed
their vast and expensive ignorance. The
aggregation of land in huge estates; the drain of
money out of the country to pay interest on
foreign borrowings; and the further drain to pay
for goods which are now imported but which
should bo locally manufactured, are the principal
causes of national poverty. And poverty leads to
lack of employment by. reducing the demand for
goods and consequently the demand for people to
make them ; and lack of employment leads to in-
dignation meetings ; and indignation meetings
lead the Argus to make a greater fool of itself
than usual. And so the circle goes round.
—
Matrimonial Agencies.
John Hurley, M.L.A., invites N.S.W. Parlia-
ment to suppress matrimonial agencies, and put
parsons, agents, or other aiders and abettors of
these evil establishments in gaol, or some other
suitable place. Supposing the well-meaning John
is successful, who will define a matrimonial
agency ? The name, of course, will disappear;
and there will no longer be any visible layman
running the show and keeping his hired sky-pilot
on the premises. But supposing a somewhat
frayed parson hangs out his own personal shingle
at the old office, and runs the business himself ?
He can’t very, well be a matrimoni d agent; a man
can hardly be an agent for himself. If he isn’t a
matrimonial agent when he performs the cere-
mony in his own house or church, he can’t become
one merely through doing the same thing at his own
office, and putting his advertisement in the papers.
And when one considers the alarming decrease
of the birth-rate, and the increase in the
illegitimacy rate, is it a sensible thing to
put any difficulty in the way of cheap and
sudden marriage ? The agency wedding may be
ordinary and squalid, but it is presumably better
than nothing at all. The Australian is of a casual
disposition and apparently doesn’t feel the need
of marriage to any very great extent. If it is
there he may fall into it; if it is difficult to find
and surrounded by needless forms, ceremonies,
and frills he often iets it slide. And it is a thing
which slides easily. Apparently, what is wanted
is a system whereby anyone may be married by a
policeman for 6d., so that the person who can’t be
troubled going down to the agency may see all the
facilities for holy matrimony walking past his door
daily. The trouble with the clergyman is that he
won’t come when he is whistled to. He has to be
approached in a somewhat grave and ceremonious
fashion, whereas he'needs to be as accessible as a
threepenny beer to cope with the increasing
illegitimacy statistics. And even if the agency
wedding is a squalid affair, it must be remembered
that there is a fitness and an unfitness in
things, and that kind of union isn’t half so
squalid as the three-roomed wooden cottage out
Pyrmont way which probably follows. Very often
the person who wants to be married “ just any-
how ” feels so inclined because any kind of mar-
riage is good enough to legally entitle him to take
his best girl to the sort of home that they have
both been accustomed to, in the sort of street
which is furnished under the tired, incapable legis-
lative system of which John Hurley is a part.
If the home and the street were improved, there
might be a tendency to go to them with more
dignity. When one considers the miles of hovels
in Sydney’s bye-wavs, and the bye-ways them-
selves ; and also considers the meanness and un-
picturesqueness of the ordinary individual who
is reared therein; and then considers the kind of
girl who would be willing to live in that kind of
house with that kind of individual, it doesn’t seem
at all wonderful that both parties to the trans-
action should take any kind of wedding that pre-
sents itself, and not be too particular if it doesn’t
present itself at all. What kind of wedding does
Hurley, M.L.A., feel that he would like if he
was a young man working in a non-union boot-
factory, with the moral atmosphere of the boot-
factory in his soul, and was going to start life in
Waterloo? Ancbhow would it be to begin with a
good Building Act and a general street-repairing
crusade ?

The Maoriland Arbitration Bogey.
Among the poisonous-gas bubbles which Judge
Backhouse pricks in his Industrial Arbitration
report to N.S.W. Govt, a front place belongs
to the bold lie that in Maoriland the law has com-
pletely knocked out “ freedom of contract ” and
lias ukased that employers shall employ only
trades unionists. This has been so often alleged
that most Australian unionists, anyway, have
accepted it as a pleasant matter of fact; while on
the other side it has been received just as
unquestionably as sufficient reason for resisting
compulsory arbitration altogether. Judge Back-
house shows, however, that in the first
place the law and the courts don’t know
trades unions —* that what they do know
are “industrial unions,” formed as part of the
machinery of the Act, and as likely as not com-
posed of men who will not join a trade union.
And, in the second place, though a trade union
may register as an “ industrial union,” and though
members of an “ industrial union ” may be granted
a right of preference, “it is invariably accom-
panied by a condition that any competent work-
man of good character, without ballot or other
election, upon payment of an entrance fee not ex-
ceeding 55., and of a subsequent contribution of
not more than sixpence a week, must be admitted
a member of the union and a superior non-
union workman may at any time be employed.
Nor is even this small right to preference always
conceded :
In the case of the seamen it was refused, one of the
reasons being' that it might interfere with the discipline
of the ship. In the case of the Christchurch engineers it
was refused, as the number of unionists was propor-
tionately small.
Anyhow, if a country wants to prevent strikes, the
sensible tiling seems to be to grant a preference to
men who agree to go to arbitration and abide by
it; though here the preference is so hedged about
that, as “ one of the Judges ” put it to the N.S.W.
Commissioner, “the granting of it can only be
objected to on sentimental grounds” —and they
seem pretty thin ones even at that.
Griffith, M.L.A., has a little Bill for the aboli-
tion of tied public houses in N.S.W. Considering
the outrageous character of the tied public-house
agreement it is wonderful how difficult its aboli-
tion appears to be. The system by which a
brewer, in consideration of a small advance, often
at both high interest and reasonable security, can
stipulate that the publican, even after every penny
of his liability is paid, shall be bound to deal exclu-
sively with that one brewer as long as he remains
in the house, and all his successors shall be simi-
larly bound, is an affair compared with which
the ordinary usurer’s terms for discounting a bill
are pure philanthropy. Apropos, if the measure
propounded by John Hurley, M.L.A., for the
abolition of usury becomes law, it should also
badly shake up the brewery man. Hurley’s draft
law provides that no money-lender shall, “directly
or indirectly,” charge more than 10 per cent.
How much does the profit gained out of the ex-
clusive trade of even a small pub represent in-
directly on a loan of perhaps £2OO ? It must repre-
sent something, or the brewer wouldn’t insist on
the concession. Seeing that the arrangement may
go on for 20 years after the original debt is dis-
charged, it must come, in many cases, to represent
an immense amount per cent, for the time the loan
was in force —something that leaves the 10 per
cent, hull-down on the far financial horizon.
Hurley’s scheme further provides that if more
than 10 per cent, is charged the borrower can at
any time discharge the debt by paying the princi-
pal and 10 per cent., and the lender must accept
same and give a discharge within 48 hours under
a very heavy penalty. This idea seems bound to
knock out the tied-house agreement in the first
round. If both measures pass it is highly im-
probable that the agreement will last through one-
half of the first round.

S.A. is the only State that has no graving dock.
The Government says it can’t build one because
the money will be borrowed at 31, per cent, and
the dock will only return 1| per cent. This ridi-
culous and slavish subservience to the money-
lender meets U s at every turn. Suppose such a
work cost £500,000. If the Government raised
£500,000 by extra taxation for the purpose, it
would only be putting into temporary circulation
£500,000 that is now idle in the local banks.
Then it would employ a number of the men
who are now idle about the streets. Result: The
£500,000 would still be in the country when the
work was done, and the country wouldn’t be a
penny poorer in cash. The country would also
have a public work worth £500,000 which it didn’t
have before. If it yielded 1\ per cent, the country
would have a new income of =±-7500 a year clear.
The spending of the £500,000 in wages, and its
expenditure by the wage-earners among the shops
would have greatly increased trade while the
making of the dock was in progress. The
clock would give permanent work to a num-
ber of men and further improve trade.
It only requires a Government with grit enough
to start the system of building public works
out of revenue, and the advantages will soon be
apparent. Of course if the money is borrowed at
per cent, it is quite a different thing. There
would be the same improvement of trade while the
job was in course of construction, but after that
the dock would be yielding £7500 of revenue while
£17,500 would be going out of the country in extra
interest, and the loss would more than swallow
up any benefit that might otherwise accrue.
“ X. 0.” writes re the Federal city :
Don’t mind the Federal Capital being a “fit subject
for a great artist’s inspiration,” or “an ideal in marble
and bronze,” &c., but would like to make sure of it being
a healthy open and sunlit conception in bricks and
mortar —hr preferabty cement. Bar all galvanised iron
and scantling, or lath arid plaster, or weatherboard. Too
liable to fire, also bugs. Lay the streets to the half
points of the compass ; this will allow of the sun finding
its way around every house built square with the streets.
To further ensure sunlight, the great deodoriser, abso-
lutely prohibit attached or semi-detached houses or
terraces ; allow a chain between every two residences.
Pass a comprehensive Building Act prescribing lofty and
spacious rooms. Insist on fire-proof construction and pro-
per bathing and other sanitary facilities. In other words,
prohibit jerry-building. Make every room at least 18ft. by
18ft. and 10ft. high, with at least one window opening on
the open air, or on open court-yard inside house. Insist
on proper damp-courses, which very few jerry-built
houses possess. Damp is a prolific source of disease when
it invades floors and walls, causing rot and mildew. And
for Heaven’s sake don’t put the control of the Federal
city under a gang of aldermen ! Australian cities have
suffered enough from aldermen. Appoint a city commis-
sioner solely and absolutely responsible to the Home
Secretary. As to climate, elevation, water-supply, drain-
age, average rainfall, sunlight and temperature, a commis-
sion of an engineer, a meteorologist and a doctor should,
with the assistance of a first-class architect and bill-
drafter, be able to originate and plan a good practicable
city for human beings. We will chance the “ dreams of
beauty,” “ towering domes,” “ lofty spires,” “conceptions
in marble,” &c., if we can get a real useful up-to-date cle’an
capital.

A systematised attempt is to be made to knock out
one of the most useful measures'introduced to the
Victorian Parliament this session. Minister for
Agriculture Morrissey, a large producer himself,
has brought in a Bill to amend the Exported
Produce Act. This measure deals with the export
of butter and meat (beef, mutton, poultry, rabbits,
&c.). It provides that all butter sent out of the
State shall be examined by experts and divided
into three classes, and that the experts shall have
power to prevent the export of any meat which,
in their opinion, is unfit for use. At present ex-
porters can send butter of any quality out of the
State, and can ship any sort of meat provided that
the experts cannot prove it to be absolutely
diseased. The re ult of the present law is that
large quantities of butter and meat absolutely
unfit for human consumption are shipped from
Victoria. These goods seriously affect the repu-
tation of all Victorian produce in foreign markets.
The idea of the amending Bill is to prevent
a continuance of this state of affairs. Nothing is
more essential to the development of the export
trade than the shipment of the very best
goods only, and Morrissey was right on the
spot when he declared that producers and
exporters should be coerced into exporting only
the best. There is no necessity for the existence
of bad produce, and the fewer outlets there are
for it the less of it there will be. The Bill was
no sooner launched, however, than a howl that it
was unnecessary was raised by the Tory crowd.
Said crowd also says that the butter marked
second-class in Victoria might improve on the
voyage, and that the buyers in London ivould use
the mark to lower its proper value. Further, it
says that the London buyers take, no notice at all of
marks, and rely wholly on their own judgment,
which two statements don’t seem to hang together.
The Tory’s devotion to Britain is pathetic at
times, but when it is proposed to show that devo-
tion by not sending Britain bad victuals, then the
sentiment gutters out like an exhausted candle.
The right to poison the beloved mother country
witli decayed provender, all for sordid temporary
gain, is a right that the Vic. Toi’y will defend to
the last.
Premier Jenkins’s scheme of reform in S.A. pro-
vides for the maintenance of two Houses in the
State Legislature. So does Premier Peacock’s in
Victoria. Peacock said he hoped “never to see
the abolition of the Council brought about.” In
the name of all that is wonderful, why ? There is
only one other British Federation to sup-
ply a precedent, that of Canada; and six of
the eight divisions of the Dominion have only
one House of State Legislature apiece. If that
fine old British Imperial precedent isn’t good
enough for Peacock, and he is becoming per-
meated with the republican traditions of the
United States, where each State has two Houses
of Legislature, then Peacock’s loyalty is evi-
dently giving out, and it will soon be time for
him to be shot down the stair after Findley. If
Findley is still lingering about the foot of that
stair he had better shift before the falling body of
Peacock drops on him.
A great deal of space is being devoted in the
daily papers to expounding the fact that Robert
Reid, M.L.C., Victoria a city man, who
usually dwells in an office—went up as far as
Bundaberg, Queensland, which is a long way this
side of Rockhampton, and not in the tropics at all,
and that, after being two days there, he learned,
to his complete satisfaction, that the Kanaka
couldn’t possibly be dispensed with for tropical
sugar-growing work. It was always understood
in Melbourne that Robert Reid was politi-
cally a nigger-labor man, so he presumably
went to Bundaberg only as a matter of
form, and, as a person who had spent his life
in an office, he naturally understood all about
a great agricultural problem in two days.
The local inhabitants pulled R. Reid’s leg so
effectually that, when last heard of, he
was delivering speeches to the effect that “ the
sugar-industry was worth three times as much
per inhabitant to Queensland as the butter indus-
try was to Victoria,” and an industry of such
stupendous importance must not be hastily de-
prived of its nigger. According to official pub-
lished values the sugar industry is worth a
little more per head to Queensland than the butter
industry is to Victoria —but not three times as
much, or anything like it. When a guileless Mel-
bourne merchant goes up to a place outside the
tropics to learn all about tropical agriculture in
two days, he is apt to fall in like this, Reid pro-
bably pictured himself working in the cane-field
in his frock-coat and his bell-topper, and decided,
from observations of the temperature, that he
couldn’t do it, he being accustomed to the icy
•winter blast on the bleak shores of St. Kilda, and
that was good enough evidence for him that no
other white man could do it either.

Vio. Assembly is gabbling at great and useless
length over the Bill for the Establishment of a
Convention to draft a new State Constitution.
Everybod3’- knows the Assembly will pass the
Bill by a huge majority, but people like Reay
insist on talking for three hours at a stretch about
their reasons for being in favour of it. It will be
well on in the session when it reaches the Council,
and by the time the Council has gradually re-
jected it there will be no time to think out any
way of fighting that body this session, even sup-
posing Peacock means fighting. If Peacock
were only the right kind of Premier he
would push through the Assembly swiftly,
and, as far as possible, in dead silence, the
Convention Bill, a Woman’s Suffrage Bill,
a Bill to abolish the last remains of the plural
property vote, a Bill to impose a heavy absentee-
tax, a graduated Land Tax Bill, and a Bill giving
extended powers for the compulsory resumption
of large estates for closer settlement; and lay the
whole lot before the House of Landlords in a heap.
And when it rejected the lot then there would be
a good case on which to stop supplies and start a
great Political Strike till the Council caved in.
The country might not be sufficiently roused to
back Peacock in a great Political Strike over the
rejection of one Bill, but over the rejection of a
batch like that it could be roused to a white heat
—if only the right man were at the bellows.

Melbourne Argus delivers itself further re
Seddonism :
Mr. Seddon is fortunate in that his Government has
been coincident with continued and growing prosperity.
The advance of science made the frozen-mutton trade
practicable. Private enterprise saw its opportunity, and
translated the possibility into a fact ; work became
abundant and prosperity widely diffused. The Govern-
ment had no share in creating or fostering a prosperity
which was naturally shared in by the working classes —as
all prosperity must be.
This is the Argus’s favorite way of explaining its
broken-up prophecies as to how Seddon and co.
would ruin Maoriland past repair. And it is a
pitiably silly way. The Maoriland frozen meat
business had been booming for eleven years before
Seddon became Premier. The average year’s
export during his Premiership has only been
£400,000 more than it was for years before he
became Premier. Moreover £1 gained by the ex-
port of frozen meat is no more use than £1 gained
by the export of anything else. And the exports
of Queensland, Victoria, Tasmania, and Westralia
are far more per inhabitant than they were nine
or ten years ago, while those of Maoriland,
with all the frozen mutton included, have in-
creased very little. The poorest of them shows
three times Maoriland’s increase of exports;
the luckiest of them 32 times its increase.
So if the export of anything in particular
is the cause of Maoriland’s present prosperity
then there should be vastly more prosperity
on this side, and there should be no need for the
Argus to make foolish apologies and explanations
like the above. The increase of Maoriland’s
frozen meat export industry in Seddon’s time
hasn’t been anything like so big a thing as the
increase in Victoria’s butter output, or Queens-
land’s gold production. And these States, alas !
have a very humble boom on hand. Possibly it is
for want of a Seddon and a Seddon policy. All
the materials which the Argus says made Maori-
land’s prosperity are there—but Seddon’s socialis-
tic policy and his Labor legislation are not there,
and his boom isn’t there either.

The Anglican Provincial Synod sitting in Sydney
last week was badly cornered by a no doubt rever-
ent but too pragmatical (for such a body) layman.
The Rev. Boyce, who fights with a knife on the
liquor-question, cooed plaintively over the State
vagaries on the age of consent. He detailed—
In England the age of consent was 16 years. In many
of the American States it stood at 18 years. In South
Australia the age was 17, in Victoria and Westralia
it was 16, while in this State it stood at the low age of
14 ;
and wound up by asking the Synod to urge the
passing of a law to raise the age of consent to 17.
It was here that the lay representative drove his
logic home. He did not think they should, as a
Church, recognise any age of consent. Canon
Boyce objects to State consent to the consump-
tion of liquor at any age or in any quantities, but
if his motion amounts to anything he agrees to
State consent to immorality after 17 years of age.
If it is a crime for the State to permit in one case.
