MELBOURNE SHOWS FOR COMING WEEK.
Princess’s “ Why Smith Left Home."
Her Majesty’s “ Sign of the Cross.”
Theatre Royal “ The White Heather.”
Opera House Rickards Variety Co.
The happily-recovered R. Brough made
his re-appearance at Sydney Royal in “ Mrs.
Dane’s Defence,” an undefinable four-act play by
that finished but rather undefinable dramatist,
Jones. Jones’s plays are not comedies, nor are
they exactly tragedies, for they always end with
a vague suggestion that one or two of the charac-
ters may be happy with difficulty by-and-bye, and
that the others will look elegant and graceful in
their misery. Jones is largely a writer of soul-
wails, in some of which the wail predominates,
and in others the soul. In the present work
Jones’s sorrowfulness is alleviated by the gratify-
ing and well-earned humiliation of the Fat
Woman. Mrs. Dane is a wealthy lady with a
Past which was located in Vienna. She is trying
to bury it in a quiet English town when the
Judge’s son falls in love with her. Also about
this time a young man recognises her as the lady
who had the disastrous Past in Vienna, and rashly
tells his relative, the bitterly pious Fat Woman.
He feels sorry afterwards, and says it is a case of
mistaken identity, but the moral Fat Woman de-
clines to be appeased. Her morality rises up like
a strong lion in the night, and she tells the story
everywhere with the best and most horrible inten-
tions, so that Mrs. Dane may have a chance to clear
herself. That poor woman would almost as soon
have ten shares in the Mercantile Bank, but her
friends rally round her, and the J udge insists on
taking her part and proving his future daughter’s
purity, and she is driven to a desperate career of
lying. The young man who recognises her still
retracts manfully, and the private detective who
finds the proofs of her guilt is paid sufficient to
induce him to bury the evidence in the inside
pocket of his mind and swear to anything. The
meddlesome Fat Woman is crushed but still
malevolent, and is allowed a week in which to sign
an abject apology. And then, through a single
slip on the part of the defendant, a beautiful lie
falls through ; and the Judge and Mrs. Dane’s
best lady-friend find out everything. The one re-
deeming feature just here is that the lady-friend
insists on keeping the knowledge secret and
making the Fat Woman apologise all the same.
The Fat Woman is crushed in evening dress, and
made to Wallow in the dust, and withdraw every-
thing.
* • * *
The strongest scene in the play is where
the lie begins to fall through, and Judge Brough’s
slow, methodical cross-questioning grinds the
truth out of the lady with a Past. It isn’t a
pleasant scene. The hysterical woman trying to
patch the hole in the old lie with new ones, which
grow more and more incoherent till the last one
wouldn’t impose on a curate, and inventing
imaginary land and marine relatives to commit
her sins for her, and trying to explain that the
fallen woman—when it became evident that some-
body fell in the family—was two or three distant
female connections of the same name who took_
the job in turns, and who all died leaving no
trace and no visible tombstone, looks too like a
Erocess of slow torture. And the Judge, who is a
achelor of 50, and who has admitted that he once
tried his level best to elope with his friend’s wife,
speaks kindly but severely to the crushed woman,
and breaks off her engagement with his son. He
states that it was through no fault of his that he
didn’t elope with his friend’s wife—the lady
thought better of it, but he continued thinking as
badly as ever; yet as a man who made a disgrace-
ful failure of his great attempt at immorality, and
who is still sorry that he failed, he repudiates a
woman for being more successful than himself.
Also, it is hinted that there were many times
when he didn’t fail, yet his repudiation of the
woman who only succeeded once is severe and
complete. Brough makes the Judge a huge artis-
tic success, but the character is that of a miser-
able, white-headed hypocrite all the same.
* * * *
Apart from Brough, there are half-a-dozen
good characters, all more or less well rendered.
Miss Vaughan, as the justly suppressed Fat
Woman, is a triumph, or something so near it
that the difference doesn’t matter. Miss Mary
Chatteris, who was only a sketch in the “ Second
in Command,” has a pretty little part as the girl
whom the Judge’s son threw over for the woman
with a Past, and, as she is a pretty and clever
little actress, the part gets a good show. Certain
unaccountable and none too successful struggles
after a Scotch accent may be overlooked. And,
though Mrs. Brough is a tall and well-dressed
heroine,_ the conduct of the man who left Miss
Chatteris to wander after her is difficult of com
prehension. Leslie Victor, Charles Garry (look-
ing like a long note of interrogation clad in ink),
Orlando Daly, and Ernest Vere (especially the
latter, who is somewhat reminiscent of the
departed Joseph Carne) fill most of the
rest of the bill with success, and Miss
Temple does almost the best work in her
long Australian record. All “ Mrs. Dane ” wants
is a new ending. Just by way of a novelty,
that lady’s tale should have held water, and
she should have married happily under false
pretences, with the full knowledge and consent of
the audience, as a proof that a lie is mighty, and
will prevail if it is only well enough corroborated
by a skilled and well-paid corroborator. The cor-
roborator is worthy of his hire.
** * *
“Rigoletto,” which alternates with “ Ai'da”
at Her Majesty’s (Sydney), is one of those operas
which, given in English, would be gruesome, but
which in Italian succeed in stopping at the pathe-
tic stage. It deals mostly with the Seventh Com-
mandment, or rather the vacant space where that
Commandment used to be before it was broken
too badly to be of any further service; and shows
up the peculiar variety of humor current in
mediaeval France. Said humor consisted mainly
in abducting another man’s wife or daughter, and
doing other little jests that nowadays would get a
man ten years from a humorless and narrow-
minded Judge. The stolen wives and daughters
do not seem to have been annexed for love, but
merely, as it were, to bring a little comic relief
into Court life, and enable the perpetrator to see
how the bereaved one would take the joke. The
opera has several songs on the weakness of women,
and_ they are mainly such exquisite songs that a
testimonial should be presented to the easily-won
portion of the sex for causing them to be written.
There is also a Curse that is launched on entirely
the wrong person. This malediction is pronounced
by a black-robed gentleman who chooses a crowded
ballroom to complain of the Duke’s treatment of
his daughter. The Duke, annoyed at being
troubled about these trivial matters, departs from
the scene, leaving his J ester to get the full tonnage
of the imprecation. This seems decidedly unfair,
as the J ester merely did as the Duke told him, and
was not responsible for the peculiar brand of humor
that was fashionable in the days of chivalry.
Nevertheless, the Curse pursues the personage
with the diverse-colored legs quite unrelentingly,
and leaves the wicked Duke untouched. The
malediction gives its first knock-down blow when
the courtiers, in a fit of mediaeval playfulness, get
Rigoletto to steal away his own daughter, under
the idea that she is merely some one else’s wife,
and give her over to the Duke. When the Jester
discovers his mistake he loses his sense of humor
permanently. At the same time, having evidently
no great knowledge of women, he fancies that if
he can show the Duke to Gilda as a worthless male
coquette she will immediately drop all affection
for him and return to her seclusion. So she
listens outside a door while that volatile personage
makes love to an innkeeperess, and then promptly,
on the first opportunity, sacrifices her life for his.
The Jester has hired an assassin to murder the
nobleman, and Gilda, taking his place, is killed
instead. The Duke wasn’t at all worth the
sacrifice, but probably, if he had been, he wouldn’t
have got it.
• * • *
The performers in “ Rigoletto ” are much
slimmer than the “Aida” crowd, and to that
extent they look their parts better, more
especially Dani, who is an ideal wicked noble-
man. His voice is rich and mellow, and
when it strays into the upper regions in
his supreme contempt for the sex it proves
that the contemptible sex has not lived
in vain. If that sex were less despicable,
Dani wouldn’t have the same cause to lift his voice
in disparagement of it, and thus a thrilling top-
note would have been lost to the world. There
are some top-notes that sound as if they were
worth a broken Commandment. Dani can even
take the tin-kettley “La Donna E Mobile”
(though it is Verdi’s it is tin-kettley, all the same)
and makes something rich and great of that
meagre little tune. Signo. ;i_ De Vere Sapio is
pleasing throughout, from tile first surreptitious
interview with the disguised Duke till the end,
when she is dying in the moonlight, and the Duke
passes along by the waterside with his new love
on his arm. She looks the part better than most
prima-donnas, and with her, as with Dani, her
upper notes are her strong point. Bozzoli, as
Rigoletto, does not disdain to act as well as sing,
and introduces a convincing heartbreak into his
voice at the different stages of the Curse’s pro-
gress. The scenery is all that it should be, and
the opening ballroom scene is a particularly effec-
tive spectacle.
** * *
“The Mariners of England,” by Robert
Buchanan, is now performing at Sydney Lyceum.
It stands rather apart from the ordinary melo-
drama, though the betrayed girl is troublesome
during the first act or so, and the hero turns out
to be the lost heir on, as usual, very small provoca-
tion. The drama consists mostly in Nelson put-
ting things straight. Sometimes Nelson helps
England and sometimes he helps Eugenie Duggan.
Inman is accused of trying to murder Nelson,
and that gentleman devotes a considerable amount
of his valuable time to proving that he didn’t.
The proofs of Inman’s heirship don’t amount to
much, but Nelson remarks that they look all
right, so his father recognises him. When Nel-
son has arranged these matters, and likewise par-
doned a convicted spy in the most unlikely and
outrageous manner, he gets aboard the Victory
and dies as he does in school-books. He makes a
very finepictureas he perishesamidstthesmokeand
the firing, with the ships seen moving across the
sea beyond, to the tune of what England expected
every man to do and wasn’t disappointed. As
might be foreseen England began to expect very
early in the play. Nelson expected likewise ; also,
the orchestra, at odd moments during the per-
formance, and especially towards the last act, ex-
pected in a very emphatic manner.
• * * *
The most cheerful man in Anderson’s
company just now ought to be H. O. Willard.
He is Nelson to the life, and especially to the
death. He is the only person of any importance
in the drama, and when his slim-looking person-
ality with the weighty head and empty-seeming
arm-sleeve wanders off the stage nothing else
really matters—except, perhaps, that Ethel Buck-
ley is a neat little soubrette, and redeems the village
maiden from the usual charge of being loud and
elephantine. Also, the love-scenes in which both
she and Miss Duggan indulge are somewhat
brighter than usual. A song programmed for Aot
11. came on in Act 111. The audience had a pre-
sentiment that it wouldn’t escape that song.
*# * *
“Polite” Vaudeville at Sydney Palace
Theatre began its courteous career last Saturday
with a purple-ribboned programme, a kid-gloved
conductor, and a crowded, and (for a variety show)
well-attired dress-circle. “The World’s Enter-
tainers,” in keeping with these genteel accom-
paniments, presented a pretty, fresh, and un-
usually varied performance. There is no first part
in their entertainment; no people sit round on
chairs half the evening; no blackened men shout
stale witticisms at each other and ask each other
conundrums about Mr. Johnsing. The Palace
company does not shine vocally all round, but
the grotesque dancing of Miss Agnes Mahr
and Arthur Nelstone, and the opening perfor-
mance of Miss Agnes Mahr and the other Miss
Mahr, are something unique in what is
called by the daily paper man the terpsi-
chorean art. Every item in the performance
is worthy of mention (except, perhaps, the pro-
jectoscope which, however, is harmless enough):
and all the various persons who come in and
dance, or fling themselves about in the air
in any respect whatever, can consider themselves
specially commended. Miss Frances Gwynne is
responsible for an illustrated song or two, which
is rather a childish form of enjoyment at best;
and when a verse describing how the rejected
lover fled to foreign lands is helped along by a
picture of the large, prosaic train in which he did
the flying, the performance is more infantile than
ever. Probably the feature among many features
is Henry Lee’s representations of various great
men. Lee, who has been in Sydney before,
tackled such diverse personages as Pope Leo XIII.
and King Edward VII. He differs from other
entertainers of this sort in not only looking, but
speaking, his part, though how he hit on Shake-
speare’s voice is a mystery. Fortunately, no one
is in a position to deny that Shakespeare spoke in
that way. His make-up of Tennyson as a badly-
dressed Weary William gives rather a shock, and
persons in the audience were heard to say that it
must be meant for Kruger.
** # *
At the Empire, the fragile Faning and the
Leslie Brothers hum along in the hey-dey of their
popularity. The supply of fresh artists flows in
uninterruptedly, the latest being a gentleman
named Hart, with a nice round tenor voice, a pair
of song-and-dance artists, a serio-comic, and the
Missies Rose Andrew and Mabel Maie.
** * *
“ When London Sleeps ” will continue to
blaze and bang for but a short time longer at
Sydney Criterion. The heroine, who is so persis-
tently pursued by flames and unwelcome atten-
tions, will probably be drowned several times by
way of variation in the next drama, but in the
meantime she flies by night on the telegraph-wire
in the presence of large audiences. She is the only
heroine in The Bulletin’s recollection who
escapes by telegraph.
** * *
The cornet-like voice of one Brinkmann is
the only feature in Rickards’ “first part ” which
struck the writer with any great violence this
week. Just now, there is a blessed relief from the
infant prodigy at Sydney Tivoli. Marie Lloyd
has returned for a “few nights only.” There are
other people in the show, but, compared to those
mentioned, they don’t count to any great extent.
»* * *
The illogical drama of foredooms in which
Wilson Barrett opened his season at Her Majesty’s
(Melb.) was displaced on Saturday by “ The
Manxman,” the most popular item in W.B.’s pre-
vious Australian campaign. Human nature gets
a fair show in “ The Manxman ” until the curtain
is about to fall, whereupon the convincing female
who has adapted herself to circumstances, whilst
remaining true to her honest improper instincts,
suddenly determines to make the worst of her
luck instead of making the best of it. She was
a reasonable sinner under difficulties, but as
soon as her burden of deceit and discomfort is
going to be removed by a decree nisi she becomes
an unreasonable angel. Also the management
testifies that her foolishness has come to stay by
letting loose a white dove which settles on the
roof of the pub. where she hopes to find peace and
purity. The idea of a soiled matron returning
home to the parental care of old Caesar Cregeen,
to work out her salvation by pulling beer for the
local unregenerates, is a weak idea for a strong
play to wind up with.
» * * •
Caesar Cregeen and Ross Christian, the
cute little cad on castors, are impersonated as be-
fore by Ambrose Manning and Horace Hodges.
They get inside their characters and provide a
serio-comic relief to the neavy moral and immoral
emotionalism of the Man, the Wife, and the
Lover. Manning slightly over-accentuates the
bitterness and nonconformist cant of Caesar, but
is a striking old hypocrite all the same. If there
were a military law compelling Little Bethelites
to attend performances of “ The Manxman” it
would probably do some good. The mirror is
held up to Snuffle, but Snuffle doesn’t come there
to indulge in reflections. Wilson Barrett’s sound,
solid performance of Pete was the best thing he
did last trip, and it looks a little better
this time. The style of . costume, which
seemingly sticks to him during his absence in
S’Africa, suits Pete’s appearance of uncertain age,
and assists him to seem about the noblest innocent
who ever stood in fishing-boots. Stage boots are
a guide to characters in many cases, and Pete’s
leather indicators seem more virtuous than the
Silver King’s, because they are baggier above the
ankles. Basil Gill is a sombre, clean-cut Philip
Christian, but too juvenile a figure for the part,
more especially as Barrett’s figure errs in the op-
posite direction. And Miss Lillah McCarthy is
not much more than a charming young woman in
the places where she tries to be a powerful actress.
When she is not required to be strong the lady
with the eyes—Miss M. is rather a slave to her
fine eyes—gives some pretty suggestions of deep
feeling. On Saturday the co. will make its
first Australian appearance in “Sign of the
Cross,” with Wilson Barrett as the girl-struck
Roman.
*# * *
At Melbourne Princess’s the story of
“ Smith ” and the demon cook proceeds with great
fury. Several things account for the public in-
terest in Smith’s domestic economy. Charles
Arnold’s proverbial good luck is said to be a prin-
cipal reason why people stood two-deep around
the back of the d.o. last Saturday night, but
Arnold’s luck, when you come to think about it,
seems only the commercial effect of two palpable
causes in this case. A bright, well-balanced com-
pany tackles an ingenious farcical comedy with all
the go that is available, and push it along for all it
is worth. The co. that made much of “Jones”
scores a bigger success in “ Smith,” and seems all
the time to be toiling for fun rather than sordid
salary. Besides, there is a genial spirit
of manslaughter pervading the narrative,
from the moment when the energetic husband
conspires with Sallie Booth to remove his wife’s
aunt by attacks of indigestion which, if necessary,
will prove fatal. No other farce has attached an
awful meaning to a slice of cake. “ Smith ” opens
up new possibilities in pastry, pork-chops and
delusive green vegetables—cabbages with hearts
of oak and sprouts warranted to expand within
the human interior. The presence of the cook
makes itself perceptible to a sympathetic audience,
even when she is away in the kitchen preparing
fiendish foods. Neatly acted, “Smith ” and “ The
Grey Parrot ” are a prosperous programme. Inas-
much as Ada Lee and E. H. Thomas are at their
best in both the comedy and the curtain-raiser
they deserve a little more mention than the main
body of the insurgents. The “ Smith ” season has
only three more weeks to run, and the manage-
ment mentions this fact by way of apology for a
matinee on Saturday (17tn).
#* * *
The high-born stockbroker who hopes to
be a dook some day is meeting his weird scenic
doom every evening till further notice at Mel-
bourne Royal. He goes down the ladder into the
waters prepared for him by artist John
Brunton, and another ghoulish figure goes
after him, with the result that only one
of the goggled-eyed monsters returns to tell
the tale of what happened in that sea of
trouble. The other lies numbered with the many
brave hearts that sleep in the deep. And Walter
Baker, the dook as was to be, is the bold bad
diver who collapses among the shellfish and sea-
weed. One gets so used to a triumphant re-
appearance of Walter at the finish of a Bland
Holt show that the tragedy aforesaid seems
awful in its after-effects. The death of Baker
is more expressed by his final absence than
by his dim downfall when the other fellow
cuts his air tube. But why can’t he burst into
the fancy dress ball, give a vague scientific ex-
planation of how he didn’t die —an explanation
with bottled oxygen in it —and sue for pardon
from his lawful Harrie Ireland ? A penitent Baker
in a diver’s dress, renewing his Scotch marriage
vow before the full strength of the company, would
make a neat climax to “ The White Heather.”
Meanwhile, the drama continues to prosper on its
original lines.
** # *
At Melbourne Opera House a number of
moderately exciting re-appearances happened on
Saturday. John Coleman, rather fatter than of
yore, was seen again as his old friend the
Scarecrow. Albert Whelan imitated people
and things, and got half-a-dozen encores,
also Latona, the trombone humorist, provided
a good turn with the assistance of his
musical little wife. The Faust Sisters acrobatted
effectively for the first time in Melbourne, and
the wonderful Zobedie was marvellous as before.
Zobedie’s work from start to finish is exception-
ally clever, but as an entertainment it is rather
tiring some part of the time.

These figures, as regards the last three States, have very
little comparative value nowadays. In most of the
States it is a case of comparing the revenues now, with post
and telegraph receipts and part of the tariff revenue not
included, with those of a year ago when these things
were included. The exceptions are Victoria and Westralia.
These States include postal, telegraphic, and all tariff
revenue in their receipts just as of old, and put their
share of the Commonwealth expenditure among the out-
goings, so their increases are absolutely genuine. The
.decreases in the other States are not wholly genuine, but
aiise chiefly through the new system of accountancy. In
fact, Tasmania is the only one which would show serious
decrease if the accounts'were kept on the same basis as
last year. This is how things stand in that State

The Austral Otis Engineering Company have
now delivered one of the water-ends in connection with
their contract with the Melbourne and Metropolitan
Board of Works. The second water-end will be delivered
within a week or two. These water-ends are a very
massive piece of work and reflect credit on the makers.
They are made of a special mixture of iron, which was
subjected to severe tests before being put into the work
and gave highly satisfactory results. Each water-end
weighs about 70 tons.*

If further proof is needed that Luhrig Tanners
are the best and most economical machines for dressing
fines and slimes, it will be found in the fact that the Tas-
mania gold mine, Beaconsfield, Tasmania, has decided- to
increase the number of these machines in the extension
of the present mill and reduction works. When com-
pleted it will have over 100 head of stampers at work, and
60 Luhrig Yanners, including the additional 24 of the
large type. Before finally adopting plans the Tasmania
board sent its manager to inspect the chief reduction
works in other States. Messrs. W. and J. Lempnere, of
Melbourne, are the Sole Agents for the Luhrig appli-
ances.*

From Clermont (Q.)Clermont (Q.) dredge
still vainly sucks for the untold wealth of gold contained
in Sandy Creek. Last fortnightly returns, as usual, a few
dwts. over 50z., and as the dredge has been working only
a few hundred yards below where one of the richest leads
ever worked on the Peak Downs—the Cemetery lead, to
wit —was traced into Sandy Creek, shareholders are not
drowning the town with “ fizz.” Apropos, a nasty little
point has cropped up re the Dredge Co.’s right to dredg-
ing. Within the area granted to co. two main roads cross
the creek, said crossings being totally dry 90 per cent, of
the year. The dredge, to work out co.’s area, must in
course of time either cut channels about 30ft. wide and
4ft. deep to cross these roads, or be pulled to pieces and
reinstated on top sides of crossings.
The consistent Bedford’s reef at M‘Donald’s
Flat, Clermont, has just given holders a handsome
div. out of cyanided tailings. Workings, 170 ft.
deep ; reef in most places about 7ft. wide, but gold
not uniform right through. Permanency seems assured ;
average returns about loz. to ton through battery, and
nearly ditto for tailings. Up to date above is the best
reefing show held on Peak Downs.
Latest Peak Downs rush is to an ironstone blow
about 25 miles S. of Clermont, about 9 miles from Retro
railway siding. Small assays from blow have returned as
high as 6oz. to ton and thousands of tons of apparently
similar stone are in sight. Locals, of course, maintain
everything is similar to Mt. Morgan ; this, however, is not
correct, the only resemblance being that ironstone pre-
dominates in both, but of different formation, Clermont’s
hill being titanic iron highly magnetised. A rush pend-
ing further development is to be deprecated. An assay
from continuation of blow 7 miles off original hill has
returned 2oz. to ton.

deficit of about £6oo,ooo—the eighth big shortage in 11

* • • *
Tasmania has now got its great accumulated
deficit down to £52,556. Tasmania, like the other States,
fakes its accounts in a hideous manner by charging
ordinary expenditure to loans —if it didn’t the deficit
would remain about stationary. Still, other States steal
tlie loan money in the same way, and don’t pay off the
deficits, so Tasmania, just at present, is better than most
of them. At the end of this year the island hopes,
despite the great revenue slump, to have a surplus of
£10,071.
* • * •
The Queensland Government doesn’t propose to
do anything towards paying off its awful shortage except
that it will auction some more land when a favorable
opportunity presents itself. Meanwhile the deficit is to
be added to the public debt —temporarily at first; per-
manently, as likely as not, later on. This will make about
£2,100,000 of Queensland revenue deficit put into the
public debt. And no extra taxation is proposed
to prevent another £528,000 shortage next June.
Treasurer Cribb simply “ estimates ” that the new
financial year will yield £115,000 more land revenue
(mostly by auction sales, which same auction money is
also supposed to go to pay off the old deficit), £34,000 more
from railways, and £77,000 more from “miscellaneous.’
Also he assumes, despite the fact that the interest bill is
much increased, that the State will spend less. He figures
out a surplus of £20,000, so things will have to be £548,000
better than they were last year. Meanwhile, with only
one month of the new financial year gone, things are
already £26,000 worse than they were at the same date
last year—income is £48,376 less than in last July, and
expenditure only £22,308 less. And consider how last
year panned out!
If N.S.W. is reasonably fortunate it will not
have to pay anything like £439,052 in the new financial
year for plague, contingents, and celebrations, though
there is still some contingent expenditure to provide for.
But it has old-age pensions to pay, and nearly two
years’interest on the cost of the resumed area against
only a small part of one year’s interest paid last year.
Also there is nearly £90,000 more interest to pay on public
debt. These will amount to more than the £439,052 of
extraordinary expenditure of last year, and there will be
no extra railway receipts at celebration time to swell the
revenue. Also, there is a promise to put the two insol-
vent Superannuation Funds straight. Unless there is a
big increase of income somewhere N.S.W. looks like
having a still larger deficit next year. The principal
hope seems to be that the Federal tariff will come along
quickly, and that it will be a very large tariff.
* • » •
The Maoriland Advances to Settlers’ Office, or
State Land Bank—the first and also the largest institu-
tion of its kind in Australasia —closed a good year at 31st
March, 1901. The great trouble of this institution is
that, instead of borrowing money locally as required
at 3j per cent., and getting full face-value for its loans,
it raises the funds required for its business by issuing big
3 per cent, loans in England at a huge discount. As a
result, its loans, when their full term has expired, will
have cost over 3J per cent, all round. As a further
result, its accounts are disfigured by a debit balance,
while the S.A. and Victorian institutions are accumulating
reserve funds. Also, the practice of leaning on the
British money-lender is bad, unpatriotic, and destructive
to the spirit of self-help and self-dependence. This is the
record of the Maoriland Advances to Settlers’ Office so
far :
Loss on
Loss on Other Loan Total
Business. Profits. Flotations. Deficit.
March, 1896 £105,897 £137,056
Profit on
Business.
„ 1897 .. £1,610 £344 135,102
„ 1898 .. 18,283 5,646 111,1/3
„ 1899 .. 17,598 93,575
„ 1900 .. 14,073 28,142 107,644
„ 1901 .. 20,325 9,889 97,208
The “ Other Profits ” arose through the temporary in-
vestment of superfluous funds in miscellaneous securities
and their re-sale at enhanced prices. So far as concerns
the actual banking business, the year just ended is the
best up to date.
A most remarkable feature about the Maori-
land Advances to Settlers Office is the low cost of manage-
ment. The working expenses of the office (which, of
course, do not include interest on borrowed money) have
been as follows :
Total cost of Proportion to
Management. Nominal Capital.
March, 1897 .. £11,294 .. £0 15 1 per cent
„ 1898 .. 6,216 .. 0 8 3 „
„ 1899 .. 6,869 .. 0 9 2 „
„ 1900 .. 7,267 .. 0 7 3 „
„ 1901 .. 6,869 .. 0 6 10 „
These are remarkably low percentages. The Maoriland
Advances to Settlers Office has so far made £40,730 profit
on its actual banking business, and £5990 otherwise—
total profit, £46,720. Against this it has lost £143,921 by
the flotation of loans at a discount. As the loans have
still 44 years to run these losses will, of course, he more
than made good out of earnings long before the date for
repayment arrives. Still, the fact remains that the
S.A. and Victorian State Land Banks get money
locally a shade cheaper than the Maoriland institution
gets it in London; lend at lower interest; and by
borrowing at higher interest and therefore getting a
better price for their loans they have no great deficit or
dead horse to work off. Instead, they have a small
reserve fund apiece, and that always looks better in a
halance-sheet. Yet, with all, the Maoriland State Land
Bank is a splendidly prosperous institution, and even the
worst Tory on the two islands now indignantly denies
having said the things he said about it in 1894, and alleges
that he was wrongly reported by a malicious or inebriated
press.
* • * •

ment of the year (paid-up capital, £250,000; reserves,
£87,509 ; total £337,509). This is an altogether extra-
ordinary figure, being equal to three or four times the
average earnings in Australia and Tasmania. There is no
State in Australasia where bank earnings are anything
even dimly resembling those of Maoriland (the Bank of
New Zealand also earns stupendous profits, though, un-
fortunately, they have to be set aside at present to pay on
losses built up in the old days of Tory government), and
all this has come to pass during the era of Labor legisla-
tion, and under the “ ruinous Socialistic policy ” which
was to “ drive capital out of the country.”
This is the first time in its existence that the
National Bank of Maoriland has paid 10 per cent. In the
good old days when there was no “ ruinous Socialistic
legislation ” the dividend varied from 2£ to 7 per cent.
In these tijnes, the reserve-fund (now £110,000) never
exceeded £lO,OOO, and most of the time there was no
reserve-fund at all. With a larger paid-up capital, the
earnings, in the period before Maoriland took to ruinous
Socialistic legislation, only once got up to half the last
year’s figure. Also, the National Bank twice had to
write off serious losses in that good old epoch—in fact,
its first gleam of good fortune arrived with the Labor
legislation period. The other Maoriland bank —the Bank
of New Zealand—is also earning about twice as much as
it ever did before the lamentable advent of the Labor
policy. And, as compared with Maoriland, Queensland
is the most Tory State in Australasia, and also the one
where the banking business is at the most disastrously
low ebb.
The National Bank of Maoriland has now a
note circulation of £236,000 and over 2£ millions of de-
posits. The reserve of cash and liquid securities is
£654,053, equal to 19J per cent of all liabilities to the
public. This is a very small cash reserve, and is the
material weak point in the business. Many a bank much
stronger than the National has suspended through having
its funds too much locked up in a time of sudden demand,
and this policy makes bigger profits at very great risk.
Also, there seems to be no systematic effort to keep the
reserves separately invested in State securities, as they
should be in a well-ordered institution ; out of £118,294
only £51,032 is thus invested. Still, the National is a
strong and growing concern, and but for the heavy.losses
of capital in the days of safe, solid, “constitutional
Tory government it would be a good deal larger than
it is. _ .

At the end of 1892 the Mercantile Mutual’s funds con-
sisted of paid-up capital £15,000 ; reserve fund, £40,000 ;
and undivided profit, £1082; total £56,082. They are
now paid-up capital, £30,000; reserve fund, £40,000 ;
and undivided profit, £7702. During the 8* years, £15,000
of profits have been capitalised. The only dispiriting cir-
cumstance lies in the falling off in the return from invest-
ments. In 1892 it was £2241. In 1901, though the funds
have grown by over £21,000, it is only £2377—an almost
imperceptible increase of £136. The balance-sheet gives
very little information about the nature of the invest-
ments, which are described only as “ Property and Mort-
gages, £58,913,” and “ Deposits, fixed and at call, £25,848.
The whole collection seems to have yielded last year an
average return of between 3 and 31 per cent., which is
by no means a gorgeous-looking figure. All the same,
the Mercantile Mutual shows a steady growth that is a
good way above the average in these times. It has now
departed from its long-established policy of doing busi-
ness in one State only, and opened a Melbourne office.
How the experiment pans out will be seen hereafter.!

sheet which ought to be suppressed by Act of the Federal
Parliament.

Shares of the Royal Bank of Queensland have
slumped remarkably since the end of June. Whether the
proposal to call up more ordinary capital and to issue
preferential shares has anything to do with it is another
question. The quotations issued monthly by the Insur-
ance and Banking Record for the first half of the year
were : January sellers, £3 Is. ; February, £3 ; March, £-
19s. 6d.; April, £2 18s. 6d. ; May, £3 9s. ; June, £2 18s.
On July 5, Sydney Telegraph quoted, buyers ios ,
sellers £3. On August 12, the same paper reported
sellers at £2 65., without any buyers mentioned. wo
other bank shares in the Telegraph’s list snowed a co
responding tall.

The new Adelaide Steamship Co. publishes
its first annual balance-sheet. The old Adelaide S.o. 00.
was one of the most consistently fortunate institutions ot
its kind on these coasts. When it closed up a year ago,
prior to resurrection on a larger scale, it had
paid-up capital ; £157,993 insurance reserve; £79,942
reserve for repairs and renewals ; and about
divided profit-total, £450,977 ; this after paying 16 per
cent, for the year. The new co. apparently capitalised
all, or much of, biese reserves. Its paid-up capital is
£507,175, and assuming that (as was apparently the case)
it brought no reserves forward, but started with an abso-
lutely clean sheet, its first year’s results are as follows
Profit. Dividend. Reserves.
May, 1901 .. £47,040 .. 5 per cent. =£25,359 .. £21,681
The reserves are Insurance Reserve, £5772 ; Reserve for

£29,550, and at May, 1900, £64,656 ; so the results shown
by the new co., good as they are, are much less than
those of last year, and barely up to the average of the

Lead having fallen to £11155., the B.H. Prop.
“ did something.” In consequence of the slow rate at
which sulphide concentrates can be smelted, partly on
account of the presence of zinc and partly on account of
their powdered condition, the furnaces at Port Pirie ai e
unable to deal with the full output of the concentrating
mill Last half-year the directors sold some of the pro-
duct for export; but smelters’“ returning charges’’are
now so exorbitant that sales are no longer profitable.
The mill has been running six days a week ; and adjust-
ments and repairs, which are very numerous, have been
done on Sundays, time and a quarter being paid, as a rule,
for the work. What the B.H. Prop, has now done is to
have the mill shut-down on Saturdays, and the
repairs done that day, thus at once reducing
costs per ton and the output. Of course, the miners
&c are idle when the mill is not running. A recent cal-
culation shows that the B.H. Prop, can work, paying at
the same time for prospecting, and an average amount
for plant-extension, with lead at £lO 10s. 6d. per ton.
* * * * *
Block 10’s position is worse. With lead at £l2
it can hardly pay working expenses; and yet it has to
supply about 400 tons of concentrates a week. A sugges-
tion that the metal co., which is the buyer, should modify
the contract, was not accepted—some profess to think the
metal co. among the “ bears.” In this awkward position
Block 10 suggests a reduction in wages; Block 14 imme-
diately sent along its support, but the B.H. Prop, and
others held off. A good proportion of the managers don t
think the men earn any too much for the place and the
work; miners get 95., and the surface hands, doing the
brutal open-cut work, 7s. 6d. The proposal circulated
among directors believed to be sympathetic is Bs.
for miners, 7s. for laborers, 10 per cent, off
others, while lead is below £l3; 10s. for miners,
8s for laborers, 10 per cent, on for others with lead £l6
or over. Anyhow, the £l6 would have to come down to
£l5 at the most before this scale would be looked at.
To Block 10, Block 14, and other smaller mines, 10 per
cent, of the wages and salaries is equal to about bLS. per
ton of lead ; to the B.H. Prop., including smelter and re-
finery employees, it means 235. But the B.H. Piop. will
not attempt'a 10 per cent, reduction, and does not like
the idea of seeking any at all. In that the Sulphide Cor-
poration concurs ; and these are the two biggest em-
ployers.

Simultaneously with the assembling of
N.S.W. P. rliainent came another outbreak of fire
at the Parramatta Asylum for Old Men. Luckily
some of the less decrepit inmates checked the
flames in time. The responsibility of keeping
nine hundred aged and infirm men in this
three-storeyed building, without proper stairways
(let alone fire-escapes, of course useless in a com-
munity of the average age of 60), has now passed
on to .Premier See. Parkes, Dibbs, Reid, and
Lyne all shirked the expense of providing decent
buildings for the unfortunates immured in the
ancient death-trap, and See also seems dis-
posed to risk it. But the inevitable conflagration,
which can’t be checked, will come, and then
N.S.W. will demand the blood of a Legislature
that can calmly vote over half-a-million for “ex-
traordinary” expenditure, in the shape of junket-
ings at home and other people’s wars abroad, and
disregards, year after year, the ominous warnings
of the dreadful catastrophe which daily threatens
this unhappy institution. “Terrible Fire at
Parramatta Asylum ! Many hundreds of old
men burnt to death ! ” It will be a pretty holo-
eaust. x
* * * *
The latest case of leprosy to be admitted
into the lazaret, Little Bay, is a young white
woman, the mother of two small children. Though
not Australian-born she has been out here for
many years, and has contracted the terrible disease
in Australia. Like nearly every other case among
Europeans in New South Wales this one comes
from the North Coast district, where, among the
sugar-cane growers, the black and colored races
are slowly, but surely, gaining. It will take many
tons of sugar to atone for this one terrible inci-
dent, which is the sixth of the sort from north-
east New South Wales within the past few years.

The Queen Victoria memorial is Still
occasionally brandished in public in Melbourne.
David Syme makes sullen remarks about it, and
contemptuous references to the animal callousness
of the democracy that refused to warmly back up
his latest effort to impress the Throne with his
personal worthiness, and now and then somebody
wails dolefully about the “inactivity” of the
Town Hall committee. The committee, it seems,
is expected to rush round with the hat for funds,
but realising that it would be necessary to rush
round with a gun, it does nothing. The Dukean-
dutch have left Melbournians “ stony,” and nob
all the King’s horses nor all the King’s men can
chisel a statue out of them.
* * • •
The Mayor of a Maoriland town that,
when the war-boom was on, accomplished wonders
in the way of sending men to the front, lately
called a meeting re a memorial tablet for those
who fell. His worship and one solitary citizen
attended. And it is practically the same every-
where. Despite all the Jingo gas, it is certain
that no decent memorial will be erected in Sydney
unless funds are provided by the Treasury. All
the enthusiasm is now of the strictly machine-
made order.
* * » *
Some time ago the people of Casino
(N.S.W.) went to much expense in erecting a
“Mafeking lamp,” and the lighting of it was a
day, or rather a night, of rejoicing ; but something
went wrong with the works, and the concern
wouldn’t generate worth its salt. They have now
to hang a small kerosene-lamp alongside it to show
the fool residents where it is, lest in their ignor-
ance and blindness they might run against it —and
damage the memorial.
*•* . *
The Yorks, during their stay in Mel-
bourne, worked the royal-privilege racket for all
it is worth. A photographer who had royal orders
for a large batch of assorted sizes of the Duken-
dutch delivered the goods, and waited loyally and
patiently for the money. The money did not
come, and so the bill was sent to H.R.H. in
Perth. It was returned in due time with a polite
note from the private sec. to the effect that
Royalty never pays for its photographs. There
was an air of pained surprise about the note as if
Royalty were shocked to find it had sat to a
photographer of pro-Boer proclivities.

tralian on the subject of the Jook’s visit:—
Dear Teacher, —The Duke promised to give a present to
every baby born the day he arrived. But he didn’t keep
his promise. He only gave a bunch of violets to a boy
who broke his leg- trying to see him.—Yours truly,
G. Jenkins.

Monday night was of the most sumptuous description,
and far surpassed anything of the kind ever before held
* n Australia. The Prince made his way quietly out of
the banqueting hall, changed his uniform for evening
?°thes, and soon found himself amidst the vast crowds
in the spacious streets, passing" remarks to everv Tom.
Dick and Harry, hearing their criticisms about himself,
the Duchess and the proceedings generally. A free fight
was going on near the theatre, which pleased him mightily,
He was particularly gracious to the girls, some half-dozen
of who 1 ) 1 he treated in Australian fashion at the bars of
!n this manner he romped about for more
than two hours, was recognised by many but was not dis-
turbed. The Duke is a sensible man in seeing for himself,
in overhearing the remarks of his delighted subjects, and
no doubt jotting everything down for future use. By-
and-bye, the Prince will be able to enliven many a quiet

* * • *
Melbourne papers were loyally silent as to
much that happened incidental to the opening of
the Salvation Army Federal Garrison on Eastern
Hill by Her Ex. of Hopetoun recently. A special
platform was rigged and ragged up outside the
building so that Her Ex. might unfurl a flag on
the roof therefrom, and a distinguished co.,
ranging from Mrs. Booth to Federal Premier
Barton, took up positions sharp to time. But they
reckoned without atheist Jos. Symes, whose Hall
of Science is just opposite. Jos. had engaged a
very loud band, and had planted it in the porch
of the Science Hall and told it to wait. When
Her Ex. &c., appeared on the Garrison platform,
the signal was given and the Science band broke
into a hammer-and-tongs selection of the raciest
“ toons ” known about town. None of the Garri-
son’s distinguished guests could hear themselves
speak and the word was passed to cut the cere-
mony short. Before the party could bolt, how-
ever, a banner was unfurled over at the Joss-house
bearing the astounding and impertinent legend,
‘ ‘ Who invented Christ ? ” and, to completely
celebrate the occasion. J. W. Fleming led up a
contingent of unemployed and began a speech of
much vermilion vehemence in the middle of the
road. The “Army” flag was shot up with the
rapidity of a rocket, and the bloated plutocrats
who had dared to flaunt and rustle opposite the
Hall of Science trekked southwards in great
haste, while the “Army” people locked all the
doors and windows and pulled down the blinds
until the cold night air broke up Symes’ orchestra
and Fleming’s flying column.
* • * *
On the question “ Could Clegg have been
saved,” “ R.P.” writes
Re the recent alleged scientific assertions that the un-
fortunate young fellow who made the fatal leap from the
parapet of Hordern’s building, 110 ft. from the ground,
could not have been saved. Three men were thrown from
the Council-house on the Radschin, by the Bohemians on
the eve of the Thirty Years' War ; two escaped “ unhurt,”
whilst the third survived his injuries. The height of the
window was 105 ft. The two who escaped fell on a litter of
old papers ; but this should not have affected the issue
according to our alleged scientists.
* * * *
“Pyalla ” : At Watson’s Bay, t’other day,
one young fellow was climbing a rope suspended
over The Gap when, just as he was reaching a
place of safety, he. fainted from exhaustion. He
fell a sheer distance of 31ft. on to the rocks below.
Everyone expected an inquest. A friend
descended and poured brandy down his throat,
and in a few minutes he revived and ascended per
rope-ladder that had been meanwhile procured.
A bystander afterwards remarked that “he fell
like a pound of tripe.” The phrase aptly illus-
trates the state of a fainted man with his complete
relaxation of all muscles, etc. This should throw
some light on the falling drunken-man question.
* • • *
All things considered, Adelaide seems to
have got through its troubles with the Yorks
better than any other capital. It is (or soon will
be) the richer by one badly-needed Maternity
Home, and there are no unpaid bills to squabble
over. And if two or three people have found
signed photos, a poor substitute for knighthoods
and snuff-boxes and such baubles (as expected),
they have said nothing about it.

Black and White, one of the leading
London sixpenny illustrateds, gives a picture of

The raid on Melbourne reporting-staffs for
State and Federal Hansards still continues, the
Argus having lost a third man, while the Age
has been deprived of two leading reporters. Few
of the old familiar faces now peer from the gal-
leries in either Parliament, and the proprietors
find it a hard job recruiting new men unless they
are prepared to pay bigger “screws ” than usual.

Apropos the rumoured recrudescence of
Plague. Several Sydney doctors have of late no-
ticed in the course of general practice a prevalence
of the somewhat unaccountable glandular swel-
lings which preceded the last outbreak of the
dreaded disease.
A Sydney su-
burban Benevolent
Society has a curious
way of doing busi-
ness. It boasts of
a well-to-do presi-
dent, who annually
votes a substantial
cheque to local
charity. But lie
stipulates that he
must have all the
spending of his dona-
tion, and so he doles
it out himself in
cash to persons
whom he considers
deserving of assist-
ance. The com-
mittee of the society
has no say in the
disbursement of this
particular item in its
balance-sheet, and
the donor has all the
patronage and influ-
ence which the
spending of it gives.
Still, it figures on
the books as a dona-
tion to the Benevo-
lent Society, on the
total amount of
which the Govt, sub-
sidy is annually
claimed ! Therefore
the society is glad to re-elect him as president, and
he has the satisfaction of posing as the Lord Boun-
tiful of his suburb, the workers on the committee
of the society coming in a bad second.

The Yictorian Attorney-General is at last
contemplating a prosecution for perjury against
one of those imaginative young females who bring
groundless charges of criminal assault. If the
A.-G. means business he had better set apart a
portion of the Crown Lands Department and a
couple of Crown Prosecutors to deal solely with
these interesting little dears.
■* * • *
Those two girls of Cobden, Vic., who
seriously tried to have their father gaoled for life,
and then relented, and declared their shocking
charge to be absolutely false, and the result of
pique, arising from some trumpery dispute, are not
to be prosecuted by the Crown. But the Crown,
through its mouthpiece, declares quite angrily
that “it is felt that their conduct merits very
severe condemnation.” Which is very like a
Sunday-school superintendent’s reproof of the
naughty little girls convicted of chewing stickjaw
during prayers. The girls’ names, by the way,
are Emily Jane and Anna Susannah. The Bul-
letin mentions this as a warning to fathers who
may have children to ohristen. Give a dog a bad
name and he’ll bite you some day.

The Sydney carters and draymen are mak-
ing a strong effort to abolish sweating and secure
fair wages and fair hours. Some work now from 80
to 90 hours per week for from 255. upwards. The
Union rules provide for a minimum wage of £2
for 60 hours per week. The men claim that the
time is opportune for the advance, and anticipate
no trouble in enforcing these conditions.
• * * •
Townsville has long been famous for heat,
thirst, piebald population, and heathen squalor.
It now comes easily first (in Bananaland) in muni-
cipal enterprise. Some time ago a number of
squalid shops in the main street were burnt. The
municipality then set to work and erected on the
vacant site handsome buildings which will bring
in a rental of £2lOO per annum. The Council
calculates it will easily make a profit of £lOOO
yearly after paying all expenses.

face the court unabashed ; cross-examina-

they never have hysterics when

“Truth is stranger than fiction.” From
Melb. Argus :
At the inquest held by Mr. Candler, City Coroner, at the
Morgue, on Thursday, on the body of Thomas Douglas
Smith, who was found mangled on the railway line at
Middle Park on the morning of 7th inst., the jury found
that deceased died from injuries sustained by being
struck by a passing truth, but there was no evidence to
show how he got upon the line.

. There is trouble brewing between the
Sydney master-bakers and their much-squeezed
employees, who have at last risen in earnest to de-
mand a living wage and abolition of 13 to 16 hours
a night work. The bosses’ association had a meet-
ing last week, and, as a preliminary, two members
had a few rounds, catch-as-catch-can—Queens-
berry rules right out of it. One employer
received a black eye and lost two teeth ; t’other
only suffered a few scratches, One of the con-
testants (the victor, of course( was bundled from
the room, and the meeting proceeded to consider
its business of how best to defeat the desire of the
men to secure civilised treatment.
• * » »
South Australia has an interesting legacy
of the historic Russian war-scare in the shape of
three heavy guns, which cost that State several
thousands, and hard-up Treasurers have spent
weary years in trying to dispose of them. The
Japs were on the eve of buying them when their
Chinese war was on, and the British Govt, nearly
took them over to fire at Kruger’s tall hat. They at
present rust in a building on Glenelg beach, and
at latest S.A. was pressing them at an alarming
sacrifice on the Federal Govt.
* • * »
“S.S.": Have come .across three books
about Westralia dating from Perth during the
last year or so, and find that Westralia consists
mainly of John Forrest’s drawing-room; its
scenery is, generally speaking, found in John
Forrest’s back-yard, and its notable citizens are
all John Forrest in various stages of his glorious
career. The frontispiece is always an imposing
picture of John Forrest at his full growth, and
the letterpress deals with the rise and spread of
John Forrest. In fact, Westralian literature
consists of views of John Forrest and John
Forrest’s views.

Cable announcement that a minimum age
of 40 has been fixed for Hungarian barmaids “in
the interests of morality,” will tend to sharpen
the point of the refrain in Marie Lloyd’s barmaid
song—
Since I’ve been here, with chat and “ cheek ”
I’ve raised the trade a hundred-a-week ;
The horsey swells,
The saucy swells,
Were never known to drink so ;
For I’m so gay
The men all say—
“ Awfl’y jolly girl! don’t you think so ?”
Marie’s song sounds pretty much like a temper-
ance hint for the adoption of that new Hungarian
law by the Federated States of Australia. The
swell who drinks to destruction on account of a
more-than-40 Hebe may be fairly left to his fate,
but his saucy younger brother is sometimes worth
preserving from the allurements of the chat and
cheek sisterhood.

At Forbes (N.S.W.) police-court. Sydney
monte-man arrested for “language” on the local
racecourse pleads guilty (evidently expecting a
light fine.) TheP.M. : “£sor 14 days.” Monte-
man (astounded): “Five Pounds! why, it’s only
ten bob in Sydney ! ” Moral—
The wedding of a colored brother of
Mahomet with a sweet young white girl recently
caused more excitement in a southern city than
Ducal celebrations. The young lady was em-
ployed by Othello in his little fancy-goods shop,
and in six short weeks, despite the fact that her
relations were mostly opposed to the match —or,
perhaps, because of that fact—she became Mrs.
Othello. He had his bride arrayed in all the
glory of brocaded silk, fashioned in style by a
leading modiste, rigged himself up in a grey suit
and straw hat, and drove round to the Anglican
Cathedral to be hitched. But as the lady hadn’t
secured the father’s consent, and as the ebony
gent had only recently divorced his former wife,
the parson demurred at the contract. Eventually,
however, another clergyman was found willing to
do the deed. Nearly all the girls of that city
(and their name is legion) crowded into the church
to gloat over the sacrifice. After the ceremony,
when the happy couple were leaving the church,
an excited female (presumably the former wife,
a red headed white woman, by the wav) ran amok.
Othello’s face was badly scratched, his tie torn
from his shirt-front, and most of his clothes
disarranged. He broke loose and took to his
heels down the street, leaving the red-headed
female the centre of an admiring crowd. But
safety is not always to the swift. As the scared
citizen stampeded he ran right into the arms of
another man (presumably another “Indian
hawker ” who loved one or another of the leading
ladies in the drama) and was promptly .stoushed.
Othello (both of him) fought like tiger-cats in a
tight corner and wiped the muddy streets with
each other, while the crowd, or the male part of it,
cheered wildly and sent emissaries out to intercept
the casual policeman and persuade him not to
hurry. How long the battle lasted deponent
can’t say; but if such hilarious proceedings are
to mark every black-and-white wedding, the
ordinary pale tame ceremony will suffer by
comparison. But what one noticed most was the
avidity with which so many fair onlookers took in
the details of the wedding. Of course it may
have been innocent curiosity that made them
crane their necks and jostle each other for a
glimpse of the study in black-and-white by the
altar-rails. On the other hand, it may have been
something quite different. Writer heard a very
cynical politician say recently that if the op-
ponents of a white Australia knew their business
thoroughly they would fight tooth and nail on the
side of woman-suffrage.

An officious M.P. recently busied himself
in urging that married couples in Adelaide Desti-
tute Asylum should not be separated. A curious
result was the discovery th t a certain pair had
been in the local institution for 15 years “quite
unbeknownst to each other,” but the lady, when
informed of the fact of her husband’s presence, in-
timated that she only wished to see “ Bill ” once a
week at most, as his society was by no means con-
genial. Carpenter, M.P., whose kindly nature
prompted the inquiry, is an unsophisticated young
thing with but little knowledge of the sadness
accompanying married life.

After a short period of immunity there
comes another crop of marine accidents through
propeller-shaft breakages, three having occurred
in almost as many days among the inter-State
fleet of coastal steamers. Two of the mishaps
occurred just outside Albany, and the Buninyong
and Willyama lay alongside one another, each
crippled in exactly the same way.

A dignified remonstrance :
Re hospitals : Is there any good reason why among
other little discourtesies of tone and hearing the nurses
should be required to address men patients by their sur-
names, minus the handle of “Mr.,” and the women
either by their Christian names, or in a few cases by their
surnames ? A certain stately lady of my acquaintance,
widow of an officer, has quite got over the complaint that
took her to hospital, hut has never quite recovered
from the shock of being “Georgina” to numerous strange
young women. Why a paying guest of the State should
be made to feel like a pauper, or even a pauper should
be made to feel more like one than is necessary, is a mys-
tery. There may be an impression in the man-managed
State that it cheers a sick woman up to be called
“ Annie,” or gives the men-patients a feeling of Home
and Mother to be addressed as plain “ Brown.” But it
doesn’t. It makes them inclined towards language, and
it gives the haughty damsel with the cap-string a strong
impression that she is superior in some way or other to
the people whose paid servant she practically is.— Mary
Salmon.
» * •* *
Some time ago Ah Mook, an aged market-
gardener, who had spent 20 years pursuing the
mild and gentle cabbage in Victoria, went home
to China. The other day Ah Mook returned and
presented his naturalisation papers at the Cus-
toms—quite a young Ah Mook, who spoke prac-
tically no English, and who said his market-garden
was in “Lil Bulk-stleet.” The fraud was too
glaring and he didn’t get in. Neither did four
other young men who presented papers, according
to which they should have been between 40 and
70. The naturalisation-paper fraud is nearly
played out now. No new documents are being
issued, and the old ones are worn and aged, and
the dates are becoming so remote that only grand-
father Chow can make use of them and look the
part. But the birth-certificate of young Chow,
alleged to have been born in Victoria, does just
about as well.

“S.S.”: I was hurt when she went past
my carriage and entered the next compartment —
she was so young and so sweet,
with large, dark, expressive
eyes, and the appearance of
having stepped right out of a
pale blue Sunday-sehool-library
book. Her skirts were still above
her boots, and she carried a little bundle
of school-books in straps. The train
started, and about 4min. later the round,
flushed face of a gentleman of about 50
appeared above the partition dividing
the two compartments. The face wore
a worried look, and there was a light
of terror in the eyes. Seeing me,
the citizen heaved a great sigh pf
relief, and murmured: “ Thank the
Lord!” Then he climbed pver,
and took a seat facing mp. I
did not speak, but the curiosity
of an insatiable scribe was
legible within my beamless eye.
The newcomer marked it, and
answered with admirable
brevity: “Little girl in there
—pretty little girl—wanted me
to tie her shoe-lace. I’ve got a
wife and family depending on
me.” In a rail way-carriage now-
a-days you know a wise man by
the company he gets out of.

JUDAS.
The Asylumed Poor.
The Bulletin is constantly receiving piteous
letters from the inmates of benevolent and old-age
asylums with regard to the utter dreariness qf
their lives. Their food is of the plainest and
cheapest, there are no comforts, and the old men
have neither tobacco nor reading-matter. The
yo men are as badly-off, being absolutely without;
any of the little comforts that decently-placed
people in the outer world consider indispensable
to the old age of their womenkind. And the
things that are requisite and necessary for the
relatives of each of us are necessary for those poor
old women in the dreary asylums. None of us
know where we will end, and certainly some who
read this are doomed to spend their last days in
some of these houses of living death. We all
read papers, and most of us novels, and the lack
of reading and smoking matter is about the worst
feature of asylum life. So, when you have read
your paper or your novel, if it is bright and
cheery, tie a string round it and address it to
some of the benevolent or old age asylums, or
hospitals, in whatever State you live in. Here
are the addresses of some of the institutions where
tobacco and reading matter will always be accept-
able:
New South Wales. —Old Men’s Home, and Cancer Hos-
pital, Liverpool; Infirm and Destitute Asylum, George-
st> Parramatta; Old Age Asylum, Macquarie-st.,
Parramatta . Age d Women’s Asylum, Newington ; Bene-
volent Asylum, Parramatta ; The Lazarette, Little Bay.
Victoria.—Austin Hospital for Incurables, Heidelberg,
Melbourne ; Benevolent Asylum, North Melbourne;
Carlton Refuge, 31 Keppel-st., Carlton ; Convalescent
s )’ Cheltenham, Vic. ; Convalescent Home
(Women s), Cheltenham, Vie.
v ’ . , _ . ,
Queensland.— Benevolent Asylum, Dunwich ; Benevo-
]en(. Asvlunl Toowoomba; Benevolent Asylum, Rock-
hamptoh
south ’ Australia.-Poot Asylum, North Terrace,
Adelaide ; Female Refuge, Norwood ; Incurable Home,
Fullerton, S.A.
Westralia. —Pensioners’ Barracks, West Perth ; Poor
House, Perth.
Tasmania. — Benevolent Asylum, Hobart; Benevolent
Institution, Launceston.
