A WOMAN'S LETTER.
Sydney, August 13, 1901.
Mt Dear Arini,—
Rumored that the next social sensation is to be
a breach-of-promise case with a male plaintiff. A
much-gilded lady has recently left for ’Ome with
a partner for life, taken on second thoughts. The
forsaken one is very wroth, and is said to have
already taken the necessary steps to start action.
You would be delighted with our shop windows.
They are showing such tender spring tints and
lovely fresh materials. Nature is doing the same
—a proof that Nature is not behind the season.
Half-an-hour from the city and you’ll walk
amongst wattle-bloom and maiden-hair fern and
pink and white heaths. The tall gums that a
month ago looked as if they were sheathed in
white kid are now a violet-grey, and made poly-
chromatic with pea-like blossoms that climb and
cling. And up in the trees green birds are
singing love-songs with a view to matrimony.
Already they are cribbing tufts from dozing cows
and strands from front mats to cradle the nestlings
to be.
But I daresay you’d rather I wrote about hats.
Well, I’m going to get one trimmed with Aus-
tralian flowers. It shall be of a bunch of wattle,
some gum-leaves, a spray of purple sarsaparilla,
sprigs of heath, and a mopoke. Then, after I’ve
put it gently in the letter-press to give it a
fashionable flattening, I’ll ask Mrs. Brough to
wear it instead of one trimmed with a mat of
sweet-peas which she wears in “ Mrs. Dane’s
Defence.” Some of the clothes worn in this tragi-
comedy are choice and desirable. I liked well
Miss Temple’s pale-green silk veiled with black
net worn under a long polonaise of apricot bro-
cade. Mrs. Brough’s lavender gown harmonised
beautifully with the blue of Lady Eastney’s Blue
Drawing-room. You might copy the coloring
and arrangements of the room with perfect safety.
And it would look exquisite in a photo. Lounges
and chairs are of a greyish shade of electric-blue,
and away in a corner there’s a white piano. Miss
Susie Vaughan’s talent for the choosing and
wearing of her clothes amounts to genius. Hence-
forth when I meet a woman with a dress of alter-
nate V-shaped bands of the tints of light and dark
tobacco I shall know that she’s a Mrs. Bulsom
Porter, and that she’s going to blue-metal my
moral character.
Miss Chatteris’s comical, plump little figure
isn’t improved by the tangle of roses that she
plants on her corsage in the first act. One spray
falling almost to her feet wobbles and swings, and
somehow draws attention to the ingenue’s rather
short-stepping, jerky gait. Miss C. ought to
tether those roses or give them to the poor.
Concerning the play itself: it is a very attrac-
tive one, and beautifully mounted. All the same,
Henry Arthur Jones wrote it, or parts of it, with
his tongue in his cheek and saying “Rats !” “I
am not a good woman,” remarks the owner of the
Blue Drawing-room. “After all,” respond* the
man, “ I don’t know that good women are the best
for us men.” (Gallery goes frantic with appro-
val.) Later, the “eminent Judge,” Sir D.
Carteret, gets on the high ropes about the woman
“ who, robbed of her priceless jewel, comes as a
beggar bride to her husband.” (Gallery madder
still with more approval). As to the famous cross-
examination scene: I wonder what Mr. B. R.
Wise, who was in the circle, thought of it and of
the subsequent logic of the Judge. Justice
Carteret’s library, by the way, is more pretty than
bookish. It hasn’t at all a calf-bound-Coke-on-
the-top-of-Littleton aspect. Mrs. Dane was a
poor sort of witness—but she bore herself just as
Waxellency Madden would like to see guilty
women comport themselves when found out. He
has been complaining that women of to-day, when
in disgrace, face the matter boldly and look the
Court square in the eye.
Somewhere —-in Ecclesiastes I think—it is written
that “Sorrow is better than laughter, for by the
sadness of the countenance the heart is made
better.” It isn’t true, and I’m not sure that it’s
apropos. Lugubrious “ Rigoletto,” performed to
a packed and full-dressed audience on Saturday
night, brought the verse to mind. “Aida’s”
corpse-making was harrowing enough. “Rigo-
letto’s” was more painful, because the corpse
Sapio is so sweet to look upon, and sings so
divinely. But the audience didn’t seem as if its
heart was being made holier any more than its
countenance looked sad. Whenever it got the
chance (that was when the lights were up) it
looked through its glasses at Princess Kalanialoe,
who, with her husband, was in the centre
of the circle. The Princess, who was in
white sequined net and white satin, with
diamonds, is a handsome, stately young woman
resembling Miss Susie Vaughan in figure and
carriage. Her features are European, her com-
plexion like that of an M.L. half-caste—a pale
copper. She was educated and has lived much in
Pans. Her husband is considerably darker than
she. The Kalanialoes, who have had a private suite
at the Australia, left on Tuesday per V entura.
Bill Nye said that Italian opera was not as bad
as it sounded. So far, I think it sounds better
than it is—that is, the telling of the story in song
is better than the story. We have been having so
many unpleasant bodies lately. There are the
bodies got out of Hordern’s holocaust; there’s a
buried-alive bride in the play “Dangerous
Women and out of Devonshire-st. cemetery
workmen have ju3t dug up the perfectly-preserved
body, with veil and wreath intact, of a long, long-
dead bride. And now comes the burying-alive
• n “Aida” and the “Rigoletto” tragedy of the
murdered girl in the sack.
A correspondent writes that “ Some up-to-date
enthusiasts in the upper galleries at a * Rigo-
letto’ night wildly shouted ‘Bis! Bis!’ at the
termination of the fine duet at the end of the
second act, much to the alarm of the d.c., which
thought a tumult had broken out aloft. It was
relieved to find that the upper circles merely
wanted an encore, and were giving the Italian
artists the proper 1 Bis,’ instead of the imported
French usual on such occasions.” The enthusiasts
may have been Frenchmen. An Australian friend
of mine visited Paris, and the first night he went
to a theatre and shouted “ Encore ” he was
“fired” for creating a disturbance. If he had
used the correot word, “Bis,” he would have said
the right thing, and been anathematised only by
the ckef-des-claque for not waiting for the organised
claqueurs.
Maud Adams, who will come to us by-and-by
through Mr. Williamson, has an unusual taste for
an actress. She is a lover and collector of books,
rare editions and finely-bound specimens. I hope
she’ll bring her collection with her. Talking of
books, I see that the Authors’ Society has just
conferred its first pension. A woman is the
recipient.. Returning to actresses. When Mrs.
J. 0. Williamson went behind the scenes tosee Sara
Bernhardt in one of the intervals of “L’Aiglon,”
the great actress received her standing on one leg,
with the other leg and one arm stretched out at
right angles. A dresser was mending a rip in the
knee of her smalls, and another was darning a
rent under the outstretched arm. Yet Sara stood
at ease on her one foot and talked brightly, and
gesticulated with the disengaged hand.
“ Palotta,” .writes a Brisbane correspondent,
“is rapidly winning all hearts. Brisbane’s smart
ser left their cards on her in shoals, and she had
already been given a luncheon-party by the
A.D.C. To be a friend of an A.D.C. (even A.D.C.
to a Lieut.-Gov.) stamps one hall-marked at once in
this city.” Also : “A smart society wedding took
place in the Wesleyan church last week, the bride
(a daughter of . the late Hon. W. Graham) being
of that persuasion. In this city of ugly churches,
the Wesleyan is certainly the best adapted to
shows of this kind—having a splendid gallery
above the floor. Said a youthful sight-seer to me,
‘ Where did you see it from—l was up in the dress
circle.’ It was worth seeing from any point of
view, however, the bridegroom—Carew-Smythe—
being a fine-looking man well over 6ft. and his
bride and her attendant maidens all more
than ‘common tall.’”
Last week a. number of Mrs. Fred. Aronson’s
friends entertained her at a tea in the drawing-
room of the Australia and presented her a silver-
framed address testifying to the value of her
charitable and literary work in Sydney, and a
case of toilette silver. The address, designed by
Mr. Souter, was read by Mrs. Spencer Brun-
ton and presented with a few apropos re-
marks by Mrs. Matthew Harris. Mrs. Aronson
replied brightly. Miss Eva Mylott and Miss
Ruby Adams sang. Mrs. Douglas Mackenzie,
Mrs. Lachlan Beaton, Miss Rose Scott, Mrs.
Teece, Consul and Mrs. Boesen were among those
present. A few days later, Mrs. Aronson gave a
large farewell party at Pfahlert’s Hotel. A
number of Sydney artists sent her a book of
original sketches and verse. Mr. Macmillan, of
Brookes and Co., made the presentation, which
had been contributed to by Messrs. Julian Ashton,
Fred. Leist, Souter, Taylor, and others. Mrs!
Aronson is now resident in Melbourne.
A Perth girl tells me that her townspeople took
the Yorks to the Zoo, which is on the other side of
Swan River and reached by a ferry. Duke and
Duchess planted trees and looked at the monkeys.
’Gropers looked at the Royalties, and by-and-bye,
when they were resting, the ’Gropers marvelled
much that Royalty drank its tea like, for instance,
a mayor and mayoress. The Duchess presented a
camel to the Zoo. It had been given her by some
Fuzzy Wuzzy or other, and there was no spare
cabin for it on the Ophir.
Grov. Gen. Hopetoun, who has borne up pluckily
against severe physical trouble ever since he left
India, hasn’t done much since the Yorks left, save
hang blue ribbons on prize sheep at the Mel-
bourne wool and mutton show. Now he is in
Brisbane convalescing. There would have been
some festivities at Government House this month
but for the death of the German Dowager
Empress, for whose demise all our flags are half-
masted.
The hand of O’Sullivan is strongly manifest in
the age, quality and number of workmen now tin-
kering at what will be the path of the electric
tram in Castlereagh and Pitt-sts. There are boys
from 16 upwards, and men who look like decayed
bankers, and “fired” parsons, and bankrupt
water-color artists mixed with the real pick-axers.
Awhile ago it used to be “ run ’em into the rail!
way” and half-a-dozen guards extra would be
cjmging to the train. By the way, the O’S.
went t’other day with his daughter and a lot of Par-
liamentarians and medical comforts to Balmain to
launch the dredge Glaucus. Perhaps the dredge
spotted O’Sullivan, or perhaps it distrusted Miss
O’Sullivan’s launching wine. Anyway, it decided
to take no chances and proceeded to launch itself.
Off it went at a fine rate “ on its own ” right under
the O’Sullivan’s astonished eyes. The scandalised
Minister declined to have any more to do with
such a discourteous dredge and hastily adjourned
the function to Sydney.
Cardinal Moran will go to Rome in a month or
two. It is compulsory for Cardinals, lam told,
to visit Rome at stated intervals. What a lovely
compulsion! Wish such a nice “Thou shalt”
had been inserted in the Commandments, and
that the Govt, had to see that it was complied
with, especially by persons named Akenehi. I
saw in a paper t’other day that in Rome the Car-
dinal would have a right to great style, as, for
instance, cushion-bearer, hat-carrier and umbrella-
holder servitors. He dispenses with a lot in Aus-
tralia. Dr. O’Haran humping the Cardinal’s
indoor hat, which was wrapped in a half-sheet of
Granny, is what I saw at a Waitara function one
day. The Doctor looked tired. Granny is
heavy.
That was, indeed, a singular acoident at Quir-
mdi. A hundred-and-fifty persons, assembled at
a fashionable polo function, crash through a rickety
balcony, and no one is killed. Many fractured
limbs, but not a broken skull or spine amongst the
injured. Now, think of the same number of per-
sons slipping on 150 pieces of orange-peel. Accord-
ing to the problem of chances there would be many
deaths from concussion of the brain.
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“At that District Nursing Ball (Aug. 7) white
gowns had it all their own way, most of the fancy
sets swearing by them as the best groundwork for
flowers, powder and patches, hunting-pink,
Gainsborough hats, &c.
“Mrs. Baronet Clarke, tall and vivid in white
satin with badge posy of crimson carnations on
her shoulder, fenced her dark pedestal of hair
with diamonds; chiffon frills softened her low-cut
corsage, and, after an interval of vandyked
polonaise, swarmed lower down over under-dress
and long train, the latter fearlessly abandoned to
the tender mercies of the dancers. Anyone who
hasn’t seen said handsome, breezy Australienne
may mentally depict her type of looks by think-
ing of Elliot-Page, or some other smart ‘ beauty-
actress.’ This is a compliment, mind you, for,
after all, these stage stunners get to the front
through personal comeliness, passing that of the
ordinary born fashionable.
“ Dowager Clarke, bossing the rose-set, looked
absolutely radiant in white, a suspicion of black
lace on her skirt, and a sprinkle of black-chiffon
dots peeping out from under the massed red roses
on her bodice, Ibe Cliveden widow, running on
completely different lines from those chosen by
her successor at Rupertswood, lives up to the
‘ best British traditions.’ Her coiffure, her chari-
table zeal, her penchant for military, religious,
professional and academic leaders, no less than
for smart society and sporting high-steppers,
su gg e st profitable study of the English princesses.
At the ball, t’other night, she danced with a
brain-specialist, promenaded with a ’Varsity Pro-
fessor, got partners for the Argentine naval
visitors, and smiled impartially on kharki and gold-
laced red.
“He the dressy widow’s haberdashery dots.
Pin-spots are to be popular this summer. White
muslins and linens are manufactured, hem-stitched
with pink, blue, or mauve, and the hems all
freckled to match. Scribe knows of an imported
white muslin, with narrow red borders, speckled
with white. This novelty is to go to the Cup,
topped by a rustic pancake hat, bent up here and
there with red roses and twine lace.
“At the ball, Mrs. Frank Clarke, the Cliveden
bride, in white satin and roses to match, carefully
held up her train all the time, and looked very
pale, though radiant with matronly importance.
The young couple set off at once to their new
home at Oxford, there to abide till the bridegroom
gets through his law course.
“Remember, in ‘David Copperfield,’ how
schoolboy Trad dies was always drawing skeletons ?
Tis told of a local little lady, who recently
annexed a rich husband, that, at school, she
always sketched fashionable brides. In those
needy days, circumstances seemed dead against a
golden future for the little artist, whose school-
fellows contemptuously greeted her pictures with
choruses of : ‘lt won’t be a stylish marriage j you
can’t afford a carriage ! ’ And, all the time, Fate
had up her sleeve as pretty, dressy, thick-gilt a
wedding as even the most ardent worshipper of
white satin, presentation jewellery, and society-
paper fame could hope for.
“ Prominent in the ‘ hunting set ’ at the ball
were Mrs. Gordon-Lyon ( nee Calvert), and Mrs.
Cox (nee Stanbridge), both looking well in white,
dashed with i’ally-ho pink. These high-steppers
seem to hunt in couples, they became engaged
simultaneously while staying at the same house,
were married on the same day, and each is now
the mother of a baby daughter. Miss Baronet
Clarke’s debutante sister Muriel is a dark, stout,
rather handsome girl, less blooming than sister
Hilda Cumming, less dashing than the Dame of
Rupertswood, but something of a belle, never-
theless.
Widow Clarke, leaning on an escort’s arm,
gave a spice of vanity to the scene, recalling for
an instant the vanished prestige of the male elbow
as a ball-room factor. Mrs. Frank Madden,
standing protectively by her daughters, was
another archaic item in a world of go-as-you-please
girls and white-frocked, skittish dowagers. Were
I a chaperon, now that duennas ask for partners
and go without chairs, I’d cart round a camp-
stool. It’s matricidal to keep old ladies on end
for hours.
“ This year’s Findon Harriers’ Meeting was a
frost from the feminine point of view. Moonee
Valley on a grey, foredoomed sort of day is not an
inviting place, so the pitch spread for the fashion-
able throng in the Special Pen was trodden by a
few—very few—brave souls. Besides, rain drib-
bled all day, and everyone huddled into the tent
and snugged themselves away into corners where
the wind , didn’t whistle through their feathers.
One lady actually sent for her feet warmer, and all
the rugs in her trap, and looked (with her snowy
head just showing) like a sleigh-rider in the Frigid
Zone. ‘lsn’t there a feeling in the air that’s
something’s dead ?’ asked a pensive girl. ‘ Possibly
a bird, ’ replied Toorak’s and most popular spinster.
. Mrs. George Russell, in a chocolate Eton
frieze, and Miss Yera Clarke, in a smart ‘ Mag-
pie ’ Eton, looked the trimmest of the Findon fol-
lowers. Russell Clarke came with his sister, and
looked on with a tolerant smile, while all the
girls of the Miller party staked crowns and half-
crowns on the favorite. None of the Snodgrass-
Clarke family are wicked enough to gamble. The
day in question all the race slang in the tent
seemed double-dutch to Miss Vera Clarke, who
sat and looked at the rabbits scurrying over the
course in the intervals of the racing, and made no
effort to smile or translate the sporting idioms. A
slangless girl is a refreshing circumstance in the
realm of gee-gee.
“First night of the dear old ‘ Manxman ’ (Sat.,
Aug. 10), Edyth Latimer, that Sydney girl, now
pronounced by chieftain Barrett ‘a finished
actress,’ was the belle of the stagey group looking
on from the left-hand box. With her slim figure,
soft, round face, smooth pale complexion and dark
hair, draped over her ears and coiled atop, the
Latimer was decorative in white muslin, long
pale-blue sash and flung-open seal Eton jacket, its
high collar lined with white-fur. But the actress
group was fidgetty as a bag of fleas. N.B.—Since
the plague-scare, fleas are not tabooed in polite
conversation.
“On the boards, Lillah M‘Carthy, as Kate
Cregeen, wearing practically the same frocks as
buxom Maud Jeffries used to sport, suffers by
comparison. Lillah, as one gets used to her,
gradually takes her own plaoe as a tall, dark,
spirited actress with splendid grey eyes. Just at
first, in the familiar white sun-bonnet, kerchief,
and ruffled dark hair, she seems like a sparse,
defiant ghost of her radiant predecessor. Barrett’s
Pete is better than ever. Less caperish than of
old in the presence of the baby, and, when robbed
of the infant, limiting himself to a mere pre-
liminary hint of convulsions ! The flesh and blood
baby, when carried in on Saturday night, looked
severe and implacable. Its histrionic papa, har-
dened old stage hand that he is, grew scarlet with
apprehensions of audible infantile wrath, ven-
tured upon few blandishments, and gladly wel-
comed its dummy substitute. Such small things
hash a stage situation. In ‘ Man and his Makers,’
the Barrett, laboring heavily through one of his
fatherly love-scenes, was just placing a kiss on
Miss Latimer’s brow when a she-simpleton in the
audience proclaimed in sugary though far-reaching
tones, ‘ I see you ! ’ Result—total waste of care-
fully stirred jam. Some people, I fancy, brought
dunderheaded relatives to that heredity drama to
cheer them up with the news that every fool
originates his own folly. Carter-Edwards looked
very realistic as Faber, the rabid scientist. Grey
hair grew low on his forehead and bristled round
his face in monkey-whiskers. His small ears,
painted crimson, peeped out apeishly from the
silvery fur.
“The latest Parisian confection in blue and grey
tones was aired at ‘ The Manxman ” on Saturday
night by a returned tourist—Miss Elsie Garter.
This beauty, who is as dark and richly-colored as
the red red rose, has returned engaged to a
brilliant foreigner whom she met on her travels —
a Dr. Pfund, reputed to be a very lettered man
and a very high-familied one. Miss Carter
returns to Europe shortly to wed this erudite gen-
tleman and live in one of the picturesque homes
of Dresden.
“ The Melbourne bazaar in aid of the Abbots-
ford Convent is to happen at the Exhibition
Building during the four Racing Carnival weeks
in October and November, when the largest pos-
sible number of punters can be got at. What a
convent doesn’t know about the ways and means
and best times for taking a cash rise out of this
wicked world, is not worth knowing. A prime
mover and adviser in the bazaar scheme is an
enthusiastic O’Connor—the same O’Connor who
business-managed the Amy Castles boom, under
the patronage of Holy Church. Probably it was
O’Connor who told the rev. mother of the convent
that the Turf abounds in beautiful pitfalls for the
charitable, and Melbourne is full of money when
the annual Feast of Moses comes to pass. Any-
way, that good and wise lady grasps the situation,
and if the prayers of the convent can make every-
body back a winner at Caulfield and Flemington
they’ll do it, sure enough.
“ The suffrage-seeking woman has been content
with the leadership and rule of energetic Miss
Vida Goldstein so far, but the petticoated Govern-
ment clerks declare she is arrogating too much to
herself, and are suspicious of her kindly intentions.
The last note distributed by Miss Vida among
the public service girleens calls for co-operation in
the work of organising a union to protect them-
selves against tyrannous courses. At this, they
tilt their noses in the air, lock their lips tight (the
fair toiler is not a talker), and await passively the
next move of the would-be priestess. It is Vida’s
first experience of a rebuff. After all, she will
find cajoling the members up at the House, and
talking two languages, with her lips and her eyes,
easier and more profitable work than championing
contented ‘slaves.’
“ Talking of plague ; people accuse returned
cohentingenters of bringing back with them some
S’African germ responsible for the new gastric,
eruptive ‘ influenza.’ Four of the travellers them-
selves have died of the effects of ‘ measles ’ and
juvenile ‘mumps,’ and swelled glands are so pre-
valent that alarmists are not laughed at when
they hint darkly that worse ills than ‘malaria’
may have accompanied our boys from Africa.

Arthur Ooningham has returned to Syd-
ney after a ten weeks insurance-drumming visit to
Maoriland.
The present N.S.W. Parliament is,
according to a local financier, the poorest in a
monetary sense that N.S.W. has seen.
Queensland has lost two Federal members
Dickson and Groom, both Englishmen—in the
first eight months of the Commonwealth.
In the Westralian pensions’ list :—“John
Forrest, late Surveyor-General, £500.” Does Big
John draw this in addition to Federal pay ?
Melbourne Age staff is kaleidoscopic in its
changes. Fox, now chief, has not been seven
years on the paper, and all the others have joined
within the past four years.
The death of Mr. T. H. Kelly, of Sydney,
struck the insurance offices for over £70,000.
The Sun came in the heaviest with £32,000, and
next to it the A.M.P. with £lB,OOO.
Lady Tennyson has a keen taste for
political crises and has visited the Assembly to
listen to every no-confidence motion tabled since
her husband has been Governor of S.A.
The Chief-Justices and Lieutenant-Gover-
nors of Vic. and N.S.W. are Irishmen, one from
the North, the other from the South. The two
Chief-Justices who preceded them were also Irish
one from the North, the other from the South.
The new Victorian Governor is “Colonel
Sir George Sydenham Clarke, Superintendent of
the Royal Carriage Department.” Up to date
there has been plenty of livery in connection with
Australian Governors; but this is the first instance
of livery-stable.

Walker is “Alice in Wonderland,” O’Connor is
“Dick Deadeye,” Millen “The Mighty Atom,”
aid Pulsford is mostly “Aunt Emma” and
“Black Maria.”

Adelaide pressmen chuckled when King-
ston, the most “unapproachable” man in Aus-
tralian politics, went over to Melbourne to run
t'he Customs. They thought C.C.K. would get
badly broken-up "by the Melbourne reporters if he
didn’t treat them better. But Kingston’s reserve
has, if anything, armor-plated itself, and he even
refuses to see the pressmen at all —at which much
impotent growling.
The Vic. Minister whose speech finally
settled Findley’s expulsion from the Assembly is
himself a holder of one share of the Tocsin in
which the alleged libel was published. By the
way, the Tocsin aforesaid is understood now to
be under offer, at a nominal figure, to a well known
N. S. W. politician-journalist.

tains four new members—Messrs. Burgess, Levy,
Kelly and Sullivan (in no way related to the
historical miscreants of those names) who, in
Maoriland, would probably earn it the name of
“ the Maungatapu ” (pronounced Mokatap) Par-
liament.

Righteous VVrixon, now Victorian Leg.
Council President, is the man who seriously pro-
posed, when the Shiels Divorce law was
enacted, to have two sorts of marriages—to make
the Divorce Act applicable to those unregenerates
who had become one by registrar, but to deny its
privileges to others foolish enough to have paid
three guineas to a parson. The Argus charac-
terised Wrixon’s proposal as one to create “limited-
liability and no-liability marriages.”
When Chas. Gavan Duffy's Ministry was
in power, in Victoria, its fate depended, on one
occasion, upon the vote in the Assembly of the
late “Jacky Tommy” Smith, and no one knew
which way it would go, the wily tactician refusing
to give any sign. His son, the late J. T. T.
Smith, K. 0., was an applicant for the position of
Crown Prosecutor. All at once, the appointment
was given to the young man. IN ext day, the pater
voted with the Govt., whose bacon was thus saved
for a time.
Did John M‘Kenzie of Maoriland did not
long survive the receipt of his title. He was an
iuteligent democrat, a stupendous worker in the
best interests of the people, and he was a large
part of the Ballance and Seddon Cabinets in their
good days, yet had the luck to drop out of active
service before the Jingo grovel-disease came on
his colleagues in its worst form. M’Kenzie came
•from Ross-shire (Scotland) where tne big men
grow. He died at the age of 66, having been
plain, good, honest Big John for 62 years and
some months, and a K.C.M.G. for a few weeks
only.

The recent sudden death in his office of
M. J. Peden removes a figure well-known in
Sydney. Mr. Peden formerly resided down Bega
way, and it was he who conducted the inquest
when the s.s. Ly-ee-moon went down. He was a
storehouse of old Sydney lore, and leaves behind
him a very clever son in “Jack” Peden, who
carried all before him at Sydney Grammar School
and the University, and is now doing very well at
the Bar.

The tree marked with an S by King on the arrival

When the S.A. farmers were crowded out
into “the mallee,” they found that it would cost
more than the country was worth to get out the
innumerable roots. But thereupon a plough was
invented which, striking a stump, automatically
rose above-ground, clearing the obstacle. It
answered, and there are now hundreds of these
ploughs in use in S.A., Vie. and elsewhere; and
the principle has also been applied to other imple-
ments. The inventor, C. H. Smith, who had his
works at Kadina, died late in July. Mallee far-
mers propose to put a monument over his grave.
How they put the square pegs in the
round holes! Archdeacon Morse, who died in
S. Australia last month, didn’t go to the Church
until he had tried and failed as a squatter ; and
Samuel Goode, who died a day or two afterwards,
left the draper’s counter to take up farming and
become president of the Agricultural Society.
Which recalls that the founder of the great
Hordern business was always disgusted with
calico-selling, and insisted that he should have
been an engineer ; while the founder of the S.M.
Herald affirmed to the last that he should have
been far happier as “the poorest Congregational
minister in the land.”

Not one of the dazzling: political moves made by the
Dictator has been of any real benefit to the people of this
State. The barren results of his trip to the Old Country
were an unprecedentedly heavy bill for “ shouting ” ex-
penses, and a cable announcement that P. C. Seddon
carried two pounds more dead weight than Mr. G. H. Reid,
His greatest exploit, the forcing of seven contingents upon
the Imperial Govt., will benefit no person so much as it
will secure the advancement of the Tactful Premier.”
Already, M.L. is being made to' feel that the whole busi-
ness was a huge mistake. The returned troopers are
swarming over the country like brigands, seizing every
vacant offise and clamoring for the creation of new ones,
The country’s “ defenders ” are fattening on the corrup-
tmn of the Administration .
W. H. Groom, M.H.R., to mark whose
death Commonwealth Parliament adjourned, sat
for 39 years in Q. Parliament, and served a term
as Speaker. For many years he owned the
leading newspaper at Toowoomba, Q., where he
was the centre of political life, and did much
useful work, helping himself and the township at
trie same time. Groom s early life was full of ups
and downs, but his not too scrupulous energv won
success in a struggle which would have crushed
weaker men. As a politician, he was noted for
cunning rather than for capacity ; and mistrust of
his shiftiness prevented him from ever attaining
the Ministerial office he craved for. Groom was
a small replica of Parkes in many ways.

Some 13 years ago, during a debate
in the Maorilaiid House of Reps, on the Central
Otago Railway, the late Vincent Pyke, then the
most popular member in the House, waxed. fervid
in support of the proposed route. He was so elo-
quent about the rolling plains,' fertile valleys, etc.,
etc., which the line would open up, that he almost
wept. After a 50 minutes oration he sat down
perspiring. Then William Pember Reeves, the
present Agent-General, rose up with that devilish
cadaverous smile about his mouth and spoke,
From a book he read of the wondrous towering
mountains of Central Otago, the vasty deep can-
yons ; the great bouldered hills rising to the skies,
and the utter precipitousness of Pyke’s Promised
Land. “Authority? Name? Name ?” cried the
House. “ Certainly,” said Reeves, with the smile
of a lean demon on his mouth. “ The book is
‘Wild Will Enderby’ by Vincent Pyke.” And
then the House roared till the dust rose in clouds
from the rotten cushions.
The octogenarian Archbishop of
Canterbury declares that he was formerly
able to walk 50 miles any day without
feeling tired. And it is irreligious to
laugh when an Archbishop tells a yarn
of that sort.
Theseus Kerry, whose yacht. Ariadne
was lost on Maoriland reefs a little time
ago, is a short, fair, fresh-colored, square-
built pocket Hercules, with bright blue
eyes and an alert, courageous look. He is
preparing the story of his life for publica-
tion. If- he tells his adventures as they
occurred, without coloring or chastening,
the result should be a human document
the like of which we have not seen for
many a day. Kerry says that, iri his
opinion the only two books written about
the sea which can be read by seafaring
men without nausea are Dana’s “ Two
Years before the Mast” and Bullen’s
“Cruise of the Cachalot.” All the
others, and more particularly Clark
Russell’s, are, he says, unrea], menda-
cious, and beneath salt-water-criticism.
And his final conclusion, after going?
around and around the world, is that the
only career with a spice of adventure in these
days is the life of a whaler. When he is rid of
the Law he thinks of building a whale-ship.
Premier See became a selector in tne
Hunter River district, at 16 years of age,
and he worked from daylight to dark.
But it was easier than cultivating the
Labor party. He had hardly any school-
ing—which is probably why he likes to
run a newspaper.

Premier Seddon’s statement that the
-»*- .- A . • . • ~ ,
Maimland Arbitration Act is being ridden to
deat . h has Possibilities of. truth m it. This world
carnes everything to excess-eating, drinking,

sels>' re }^% n ' war ‘ stl ?, ke '!> and all ether things,
0 courae the r “ ry d ° esn 4 Propose to abolish food
when someone dies of over-eating, but he certainly
will propose to abolish Arbitration Acts because
some persons resort to arbitration over trivial
quarrels. _ Maoriland established the first-Cempul-"
sory Arbitration Act of modern times, and
Seddon’s complaint seems to be that it wasn’t

great point is that Maoriland’s industries are

creases in the number.of persons employed in manu-

lasian States during the past two years: Maoriland

Minimum Wage Law) 9266, equal to P2O per cent,
of the population ; Victoria (witli Minimum Wage
Law), 9432, or 0.80 per cent, of population ;
N.S.W. (with no Compulsory Arbitration or
Minimum Wage Law), 8261, 0r0’62 per cent, of
population. While Maoriland’s industries are
growing twice as fast as those of N.S.W., it would
certainly seem that the Compulsory Arbitration
system isn’t holding them back very badly.

Aboriginalities.
To Contributors.—ln the case of ethnological and
natural-history items, etc., always give date and
place of experience; or as nearly as possible. Thus
many interesting observations will have scientific value.
“The Duke,” said the old Maoriland war-
veteran, “is a perfect gentleman. Why, he actu-
ally shook hands with me, asked how I got my
medal, and when I’d told him of the fighting I’ve
seen he said I had set a noble example which he
hoped every young fellow would follow.”
“Come home, you d —- old bald-headed pig-
thievin’ curse ! ” said a rough-looking female, lay-
ing hold of the Noble Example by the coat and
half dragging it off him. “You and the Duke —-
do what I will I can’t keep you out of bad com-
pany. If the Duke only knew you as well as Ido
he wouldn’t touch you with a long pole. Your
medal for service—stealing the Maoris’ pigs and
potatoes when the warriors were away fighting for
their country! As old Colonel Four-eyes said,
you were the most infernal pig-and-spud thief in
the whole army ; if there had been many more
like you, there would have been no Maoris left for
the Duke to see—they would have all starved to
death. And the little devil actually hopes the
young fellows will follow your noble example,
does he ? As if the gaols aren’t full enough already.
Better for them to follow your funeral. Now you
just follow me home and think yourself lucky to
have a wife who will look out that you don’t go
hungry while you’re sneakin’ round after the
Duke like a little poodle ” and keeping a firm
grip of the pig-pilferer she marched him off out of
sight of the grinning crowd.

“You coves talk about punkins,” scorn-
fully remarked a Hunter River native at a recent
Queensland show. “ Well, can you do better than
them there?” asked a patriot, pointing to his
prize lot. “Do better?” quoth the Cornstalk;
“ why at Broadmeadows the vines grows that
quick that they wears out most of the young pun-
kins pullin’ them along the ground ! ”

The fighting Waikatos took no part in the
grovel over the Yorks’ visit to Maoriland. They
were willing to meet the Yorks on their , own
ground, and have Mahuta formally recognised as
their rightful King—and rightly, as they were
never conquered. The Government refused their
demand, foolishly suggesting that the Waikatos
should go to Rotorua and join the subjugated
tribes in welcoming the Royalties. An insulting
proposal, seeing that Rotorua is the home of the
Arawas, who fought on the British side against
the liberty-loving Waikatos. Latter avenged the
affront and showed their contempt for their old
foes by staying at home and leaving the Arawas
to do all the crawling. The big Rotorua gather-
ing, intended to represent the whole Maori
nation, therefore failed in its object, through the
absence of the grandest fighting tribes. To for-
give and forget is evidently not the motto of Wai-
kato. The living survivors of Orakau’s famous
fight must feel proud of the spirit shown by their
people. The jeering remarks on the absence of
Mahuta and his people made by some of “King
Edward’s Maoris ” shows that the contempt of the
Waikatos has cut deep.
“Quad”: Aborigines at Wallaga Lake
station (Tilba Tilba), under King Meredith, have
an eye to business : they are executing an order
for 50 boomerangs at 18s. per doz. These will be
retailed by a Sydney firm at half-a-crown. The
king, a full-blood, now 75 years old, possesses all
his faculties, and entertained a visitor t’other day
with many aboriginal songs. Remembers many
old lag yarns, which he retails for a fig of ’baccy,
and is a strong believer in the re-incarnation
theory, but holds that all things living, human and
animal, were oi’iginally blackfellow. About half
the camp is full-blood.

Queensland Government has of late taken
to protecting the aborigines very extensively. No
doubt the aboriginal badly needed protecting, but
to appoint a protector (for a sparsely populated
western district) at £4OO per annum to distribute
£2oo’s worth of tucker, seems ludicrous.
• * * 9
Thus Q. Home-Secretary Foxton :
Now, instead of regarding the native police force as
their natural enemies, the aborigines of the bush have
come to look upon them as friends whose visits are, as a
rule, looked forward to with pleasure.
Sounds odd, but let us hope it’s true.

N.Q. nigger on recent blanket distribu-

sing out ‘ God Save King ! ’ What for then you
gib it this dam lillee fellah blanket ? Suppose you
gib it big fellah blanket me plentee yell out ‘ God
Save King ! ’ Suppose blackfellah leave’m blanket
longa gunyah, white fellah steal ’em. Then you
say sing out ‘ God Save King ! ’ some more.”

“ Gurnsucker ” : Cycling in Yic. Western
District during a somewhat dry year, I stopped
at a nice, clean-looking hotel for lunch. After a
“refresher” I inquired if they had a bath-room.
The landlord answered in the affirmative and con-
ducted me to it. The bath contained some doubt*
ful-looking water, and I asked him if it was clean.
“ Oh, yes,” he replied, “it’s quite clean; a gentle-
man had a bath in it yesterday.” I passed.

Many years ago a young surveyor was
sent out by the Queensland Government to make
some surveys in the North. It was a very
wet season, and the floods in places
made the task by no means easy.
The Survey Department allowed
10 per cent, above ordinary charges
to compensate for extra difficul-
ties. After a while the sur-
veyor made an application for
100 per cent, extra. The
Government asked at great
length on a very large sheet
of paper for a reason. An-
swer came promptly by
wire : “ When the water was
up to my knees and
the alligators 6ft. long
I wanted 10 per cent.
Now the water is up
to my stomach and
the alligators are
10ft. long I want an
increase.” The offi-
cial answer came by
wire and briefly
stated that if “choicer
language was not used
in future a severe re-
primand would be the
only result.” Also, the
surveyor was informed by letter
that he musn’t mention his stomach
in official correspondence.
Maoriland law was paralysed lately by
some brown men and certain dogs. The Maoris
at the Chatham Islands had refused en masse to
pay their dog-tax, and a steamer full of armed
constabulary was despatched to exert moral
suasion. Even at this stage of the proceedings a
considerable minority of the rebels refused to
liquidate the assessment on the mongrel, and were
arrested and flung into gaol. Then the officer in
charge of the punitive force approached the local
publican—the only available providore—with a re-
quest that he would tender for the supply of
prison rations, but he refused to have anything to
do with “prison rations.” The only thing he
would tender for was three square meals a day
for each prisoner at Is. per meal, and if the force
didn’t choose to supply the confinees with his food
at his price the Maoris might starve to death in
gaol, and the police be hanged for it later on. As
a result the Maoris had a good time. It was the
longest succession of square meals the Chatham
Islands remembered, and the natives who had paid
their dog-tax demanded a refund that they might
get into gaol also. The bill ran to £3OO ere in-
structions could be obtained from headquarters
to throw the Maoris indignantly out of gaol, and
let the dog-tax slide. When the constabulary de-
parted the Maoris wept bitterly, and promised
faithfully to refuse unanimously to pay any
dog-tax next year.

“ Trotter” : Clermont (C.Q.) is in a piti-
able way. Mining is almost at a standstill, and the
neighboring country is a vast desert—not a vestige
of grass or herbage anywhere. Last year the
Western stations were sending their sheep here ;
this year we are sending ours to the West. Peak
Downs, which usually depastures about 200,000
sheep, has hardly a hoof, and the other stations are
in the same boat. Ewes have been fetching £1 per
head, and one big station in Longreach district
bought no fewer than 100,000 in small lots at that
price. In Clermont, recently, I saw 12 women—
mostly with a child or children —go to the P.M.
tO “ rations.”

South Australia gives the electoral franchise
to aborigines, and all the blacks at the different
mission stations have their names on the rolls.
Forty of them voted at Port Macleay last election,
and the member they supported asserts that they
exercised the franchise most intelligently.
A foolish and very
old joke was revived some
time ago in a midland
N.S.W. town. A selector
stated loudly in a pub.
that lie would sell his
mare to anybody who gave
him £1 down. Another
selector, who knew the
mare well, decided that
his brother agriculturist
was drunk, and dashed
down the sovereign in red-
hot haste in the presence
of reliable-witnesses. The
first selector gathered up
the pound, and said in a
thick voice “Oil rite.”
Next day No. 2 went to
collect his mare, and was
met with a demand for
£5. “What for?” “The
price of the mare.” “ I
paid for the mare.” “You
didn’t. I said I’d sell her
to anybody who gave me
£1 down. You gave me
£1 down, and I’m a man
of my word. I’ll sell her.
The price is £5. “ You’re
a swindler.” “You’re a
_ .” The old
original joke, which selec-
tor No. 1 had found in
some mouldy column of
“Wit and Humor,” ended
there, and jokes ought
always to end in real life
the same way as they do
in the country paper ; but
this one panned out differ-
ently. No. 2 sued for de-
livery of the horse, and
a mirthless bench decided
that it was a genuine sale,
and ordered that the beast
should be handed over in
return for the £l. Legal
expenses about £lO. Mare
handed over with curses,
and died same night. In-
effectual proceedings by
selector No. 2 against
selector No. 1 for poison-
ing mare before delivery,
and more costs. Then in-
effectual proceedings by
selector No. 1 against
selector No. 2 for libel in
saying he poisoned the
mare, and further costs.
Then they abandoned the
law and took to wrecking each other’s fences
in the dead of night, and like amusements;
and now neither party gets any sleep worth
mentioning through hovering round in the
silent hours to see what devilment the other
fellow is up to. Several free fights up to date;
several teeth lost; and five assault and battery
actions.
Dear Bulletin, Mention of Larry
Petrie’s trans-States journey reminds me of “wal-
laby ” customs in that country. Once left a vessel
in New York and, having won a court-case
against one of the legalised thieves who prey on
seamen, was strutting around with “ victory ’
aggressively displayed on my phiz, when I was
suddenly heaved into the East River. Next night
I got another bath, and half-an-inch of steel in the
groin, so concluded change of scene would be
interesting. Winter—and dead-broke. Beat the
Jersey Ferry per friendly drayman, and started
for Phila. on foot, but was initiated by prof, tramp
into the arts of “ train-jumping ” and cadging,
but only secured a “pass” in latter branch
of the profession. First attempt was at
an empty coal-truck going through Eliza-
beth, and nothing but sheer desperatiou gave
me the pluck to jump when to draw back meant
death. At Phila. we both voted twice at half a
dollar per vote. Lost mate at Baltimore. Hun-
dreds of tramps—no swags—slept in police-cells
and elsewhere. Under flat truck with nigger,
who started singing and got us both literally
thrown off by switchmen. Some conductors slow-
up to eject tramps—these didn’t. Nigger went
down embankment, and I landed in sandy cutting.
Accommodated in Petersburg gaol till ankle got
better. Rode into Washington on cowcatcher of
express. Sampled box-cars, inside and out, nearly
crushed in one by cargo shifting on up-grade.
Sometimes train turned in wrong direction- -hard
to get information, as everyone is on qui vive for
tramps. From Norfolk, Virginia, shipped as
stoker, £5 for the run (in advance) in a floating
scrap-heap—British, of course. Hell and starva-
tion, and only four of us to carry her across the
Western in winter. General remarks about U.S.
—Pure American conspicuous by his rarity.
Country mainly run by white-washed Yanks and
German or Italian-Americans. First-rate country
for rogues.— The Wreck.

N.S.W. Agricultural Dept, notifies that,
though pasteurisation of cream for butter-making
is desirable, N.S.W. Govt, has no intention of
making it compulsory. Seeing that Denmark,
Sweden and other large butter-exporting countries
have made pasteurisation compulsory, N.S.W.
Govt, should provide the public with its reasons
for caving-in to a few hand butter-makers.

“ Trotter ” : In the Gulf-country recently
I saw the bones of over 70 camels. These were
carrying bales of wool to Normanton, and all
perished on the road. The wool was left there for
nearly ten months.

Each Man his own Premier.
Every man presides over a cabinet of his own
Ministers. Like many premiers he finds it
difficult or impossible to control the action of
some of the members, while others readily respond
to his slightest wish. The ministers for external
affairs, the eyes, ears, tongue and limbs, are
generally willing to obey him in all things, but
the ministers of the interior, the heart, liver,
kidneys &c., must be humoured and treated m
the most diplomatic manner, or there is danger
of a split in the cabinet.
As man’s years accumulate he acquires cunning,
and he is careful to avoid giving offence to the
sensitive members. In common parlance, when
a man reaches the age of forty he is either his
own doctor or a fool.”
The most delicate of all the members of the
human frame are the kidneys and liver. Great
care must be exercised to keep them in order, or
we soon become afflicted with rheumatism, gout,
neuralgia, indigestion, dyspepsia, jaundice, bili-
ousness, anaemia, blood disorders, gravel, debility
or even Bright’s disease. All these arise from
a defective action of the liver and kidneys, but all
can be cured by a course of Warner’s Safe Cure.
This medicine has a specific action upon the
kidneys and liver which ensures their health and
activity ; consequently, all such disorders as are
due to their neglect to perform their duty,
speedily yield to its healing influence.
Nicholson Place,
West Melbourne, Vic.,
March 18th, 1901.

During the S’African war an Australian
soldier was sent by his commander to let certain
British troops know that they had opened artil-
lery fire on friends, not on Boers, as they sup-
posed. The noble Australian delivered his
message by yelling to the Imperial colonel in
command : “ Stop that firing, you blanky cow ! ”
Before the exalted officer could get over the
shock, the Cornstalk went on to tell him in the
best bullockese that his sort oughtn’t to be
trusted to shoot rabbits with a borrowed gun on
E ister Monday. When the Colonel recovered
sufficiently he tried to reinstate his shattered dig-
nity before the Tommies by roaring at the
messenger that he was an insolent scoundrel,
and he’d have him shot. “Yous chaps couldn’t
shoot a paddock you was in,” retorted the back-
block swaddy ; “an’ if you was to try, our coves
’d jes’ come over that there hill an’ wipe yous off
the blanky earth ! ”
“Prospector”: Edward S. Sorenson (“Red
Page,” B. 13/7/’01) is wrong in thinking that the
“boyang” is the badge of every man who carries
the pick. The miner and prospector are different
types. The miner is generally a wages-man or
tnbutor; the prospector never works for wages
unless dead-broke. He dresses like any other
bush man, and you rarely see one wearing
“boyangs,” though they often tie their pants
with string, to keep them together. Even amongst
regular underground-men, you will see more with-
out “boyangs” than with them.

Dear Bulletin. —In answer to “Perdu”
(B. 20/7/’01). Let him make me a suitable offer to
prove what I claim for my method, and then I
will be prepared to demonstrate on any country
he may select that I can exterminate every rabbit
on one square mile with one bag of pollard, or
less. “No result, no pay.”—J.W.C.
» • * *
At Foster, Gippsland, religious services
are held in the hollow of a monster tree —diameter
22 feet. The trunk is cut off, and covered with a
paling roof. One hundred people can be accom-
modated inside. The owner uses this hole as a
shed, and has a winnower and cream -separator m
it. The trunk was hollowed out by the bushfire
of 1898.

“Picola” : Grass is lamentably scarce on
N.S.W. South Coast. Nearly all the “cockies”
are hand-feeding their milkers with bran, chaff, or
pollard at ruinous prices, all crops having failed.
Know one cocky who is spending £lO a week in
this way. Added to this he has to pay a rent of
£3OO. Still they must do it, or the cows would
die of starvation. Pitiable to see how the raven-
ous brutes rush the bails in the morning to get at
the stuff. I wouldn’t stand between a cow and
her tucker just now for the refusal of a I ederal
billet.

The Capricornian, referring to a possible
remedy for zamia-poisoning in cattle, calmly
observes that, even if a complete antidote be not

The above are portraits of Mr. J. A. G. Macdonald, who some time bach was blown off the New
Hebrides in a whaleboat, and passed 19 days without food or water-other than a very little
of the latter, caught in a tin-box from passing showers. Of the two kanakas Mr. M. had
with him, one went mad, and the other, when the boat finally drifted on to Espiritu Santo
was promptly killed and eaten by the natives. The white man was being fattened-up for the
same fate, but managing to get a communication smuggled through to the missionary on
Tangoa, the intended victim was rescued in the nick of time by an armed boat-crew under
the missionary's charge. The first photo, is of Mr. Macdonald “ before taking this dread-
ful experience; the second dates from a month after his rescue.

No notice will be taken of communications insufficiently
stamped. Name and address must appear on MS.
No liability is undertaken in any case re contribu-
tions voluntarily submitted, whether sent by post or
handed in ; and in no case will MS. be returned un-
less stamps (of any State) accompany.

“Quad” : Re par. as to bricklayers'
(B. 3/8/’l). No attempt is anticipated to make
14s. a day the standard in Sydney. An advance
of Is. has been demanded, making total lls. per
day of eight hours. Notice of this has been given
to the builders, and will take effect from Sept. i.
As a general rule, in recent years every demand
made for increased wages in this trade has been
conceded, and strikes, as known m other trades,
are of rare occurrence with wielders of the trowel.
The trades-union is fairly strong, very conserva-
tive, and has intimate knowledge of labor condi-
tions. The usual practice when a gang of brick-
layers wants a rise is to notify builder or foreman
on Monday for 6d. or Is. increase, to take effect
next day. If refused-well, away they go to
another job, if they can get it-and they generally
succeed. As to prospects of work, the Trades
Hall knows all about it; and, as it is backed by
the O’Sullivan, who crders union pay m all Uovt.
contracts, up go the rates on the slightest provoca-
tion.

THOSE DESIGNS FOR AN AUSTRALIAN FLAG. \
A lady sent a design comprising a yellow-ribboned outline of the map of Australia on white silk. In the centre of 1
the continent was a portrait of the late Henry Parkes ; in the top corners were portraits of the Duke and Duchess of
York, in the bottom corners portraits of Lord Hopetoun and Mr. Barton. The side spaces held pictures of the shamrock,
the leek, the rose, and thistle, the whole forming a tasteful design, much more suitable for a faney quilt than a Federal''
flag. /
Judging by the “ severe colds ” our statesmen bring back from Melbourne, there must be greater need for bed-
clothes there than flags. The Premier is welcome to the above hint
“ OUR BEAUTIFUL HARBOR.”
The Council of North Sydney propose to improve on Nature as above, Thqy
have decided that sites along the foreshores shall be let for advertising signs.

IN THE SUPREME COURT OF THE
COLONY OF THE CAPE OF GOOD
HOPE, CAPE TOWN,
Friday the 17th May, 1901.
In the matter of the petition of
ALEXANDER MAODONALD
married to MARION SAUNDERS on the 22nd
December, 1890.
Upon the motion of Mr. Benjamin and upon reading the
abovenamed petition and petitioner’s affidavit in support,
It is ordered that he be at liberty to sue by Edictal
Citation the said Marion Saunders for a decree of nullify
of Marriage that Citation be returnable on the 12th
October, 1901, with leave to serve the intendit and notice
of trial in the suit with the Citation, and that personal
service be effected failing 1 which that Citation, Intendit
and notice of trial be published once in the Gazette and
once in the Sydney Bulletin.
By Order of the Court,
J. H. GATELY, Asst. R 'gistrar.
Edward the Seventh, by the Grace of God, of the United
Kingdom of Great Britain and Ireland, King, Defender
of the Faith :
To
MARION MACDONALD,
otherwise known as Marion Saunders, lately of
Cape Town, and presently believed to reside at
93 Wooliooinooloo-street, Sydney, in New South
Whereas ALEXANDER MACDONALD, residing at
Touws River in the Cape Colony, hereinafter styled the
Plaintiff, did, on the 17th day of May, 1901, petition the
Honourable the Supreme Court of the Colony of the Cape
of Good Hope, for leave to sue you, the said MARION
MACDONALD, hereinafter styled the Defendant, for a
decree of nullity of Marriage, and that failing for a decree
of divorce by reason of malicious desertion.
Now, therefore, the said Court having taken the prayer
of the said ALEXANDER MACDONALD into considera-
tion and granted the same, we do therefore command
you, the said MARION MACDONALD, that you cause an
appearance to be entered in the said Court on or before
the 12th day of October, 1901, to answer the said Plaintiff
in an action wherein he claims :
1. (a) A decree of nullity of Marriage
(b) Forfeiture of the Benefits of community of pro-
perty
(c) Alternative relief
(d) Cost of suit.

wrongfully and unlawfully desert him, the said
Plaintilf

perty
(c) Alternative relief
(d) Cost of suit.

Colony of the Cape of Good Hope, at Cape Town, this 1 1 th
day of May, 1901, in the Ist year of our reign.

DECLARATION.
1. Plaintiff resides at Touws River in the Cape Colony,
and defendant formerly resided at Cape Town, but is at
present of unknown address.
2. On or about the 22nd December, 1899, Defendant
went through a form of Marriage with plaintiff before the
Resident Magistrate of Cape Town.
3. There have been no children issue therefrom.
4. At the said date there was a valid marriage subsisting
between Defendant and one Charles Saunders.
Wherefore Plaintiff prays
(a) Decree of nullity of Marriage
(b) Forfeiture of benefits conferred on marriage
(c) Such other and further relief as may seem meet
(d) Costs of suit. . . , ■ tt
And by way of an alternative claim if this Honour-
able Court should find that there was no such marriage
subsisting at the aforesaid date as alleged in paragraph
4 he says : " ,
(5) He craves leave to refer to the allegations in para-
graphs 1, 2 and 3. _ „ , ,
(6) On or about the 16th April, 1900, Defendant wrong-
fully and unlawfully deserted Plaintiff and has refused to
return and cohabit with him.
Wherefore he prays :
la) Order calling upon Defendant to return to and
cohabit with him, and on failure of compliance
therewith
(b) Decree of divorce a vinculo matrimonii
(c) Forfeiture of Benefits conferred on Marriage
(d) Alternative relief
(e) Costs of suit.

When Your Soul Hangs Out
o’ Curl.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
There’s a change comes o’er the scene; things
look pinchbeck-like and mean,
And the ballet-girls and alley-girls no comfort
bring your heart;.
Beer doth lose its pleasant ‘taste; and one’s best
love runsYo waist;
Revels won’t and revels don’t any ravishment im-
part.
When your pipe will draw no more, and your hat
falls on the floor,
You feel yourself reveal yourself a most infernal
churl;
Oh, the birds cease twittering sweetly—things
seem “ busted up ” completely —
Life is numb and life is dumb when your soul
hangs out o’ curl.
For your old pal seems a foe ; you forget to twist
your ‘ ‘ mo ” ;
Bliss is rancid that you fancied —even Sin is dull
and flat;
Kisses lose their honey-stings ; out of tune your
choir-girl sings;
You can’t sit a horse, and hit a duck-egg every
time you bat.
Razor’s got a rotten edge ; collar’s like a granite
ledge ;
You’re growling and you’re scowling with your
fingers all a-twirl ;
You can’t do a stroke o’ work ; skies seem all a
beastly murk;
And your skull is very dull when your eoul hangs
out o’ ourl.
Days have sheol-tales to tell, or they’re as wet as
any well;
The weather’s like a feather, blowing forty ways
at once; v
If a man should say to you: “ See that lovely
cloudlet blue! ”
Ten to one and money on that he will get a fierce
response—
“ Hang the sky and blow the clouds—like a lot of
fantod shrouds ! ”
O the pleasant, green, putrescent malediction that
you hurl !
Send the scenery to Mars ! How oreation subtly
jars!
Boots won’t fit you, troubles hit you, when your
soul hangs out o’ curl.
There’s a fungus on the cheese, and your bed can’t
bring you ease ;
Oh, life is—and a wife is—a dash-asterisky curse !
If a writer, you can’t write, though you sit up all
the night
Sadly sighing while you’re trying to improve your
dismal verse.
Pessimistic do you feel, as you eat your lonely
meal,
Sodden eye turned awry at the faded waitress-
girl :
“ What’s the good of being here, when I can’t en-
joy my beer ? ”
That’s the query makes you eerie when your soul
ha.igs out o’ curl.
Praise the pewter ! man o’ mine ; soon a scarlet
rose shall twine
Round the head and round the bed that seem both
so deadly dull ;
Like a creeper shall it creep, sweetly to your heart
asleep,
Till you wake and till you take a delicious gulp—
and cull
All the blossoms in the bowers just refreshed by
glory-showers
That are coursing and are forcing the blessed beer
to purl :
If a writer, you can write; if a lover, hug her
tight;
And you’re merry—oh, yes, very—when your soul
gets back in curl !
V.
G.M.H.
A picture of Thursday Island :
Thursday Island is a queer little shop. It is a piebald
human camp. Breeds of all sorts mingle freely without
priestly aid, and recognise each other as fellow creatures.
To classify the originals would not be a difficult matter,
but to define the nationality of their descendants !
Sassiety whites, of course, keep to themselves—l
allude to benedicts. The rest are of Bohemian tastes.
Curious to note how many young men have recently dis-
covered that it would be advantageous to their future if
they could master the Japanese language by the aid of
female coaches. Others are of opinion that a knowledge
of the Ceylon tongue taught by half-bred Cingalese
women would serve their interests best. Some again—
conservative as to the original inhabitants of their
adopted country—believe that it is advisable to acquire
an idea of the habits and nature of the aborigines—and
such knowledge can only be gained from dusky ladies
who are always at home to visitors.
Yes, it is a funny shop. But turn the picture. Strong,
lusty, young female emigrants from Great Britain land at
Thursday Island from the British India Co.’s steamers.
They find places quickly, but not for long. Chinamen,
Cingalese, Manila natives, aliens of all sorts, soon dis-
cover resting-places for themselves upon British bosoms.
Some go to church—some don’t trouble the parson ; but
the mixed breed goes on all the same.
It’s a happy-go-lucky crowd that lives on Thursday
Island. On the wharves when a steamer comes in,
whites, blacks, half-breeds, all colors, swarm together to
the wharfinger asking for a job. They work together all
rio-ht, and don’t seem to mind each other’s company a
bit as long as they are at it; but afterwards, when the
whites and colored men separate to their different board-
ing-houses, the air is thickened with the sweet refine-
ment of language usually indulged in when the white
hoodlum speaks to his colored mate.

POLITICAL
POINTS
The war is still costing John Bull a million-
and-a-quarter a week. And each week John gets
for his money a handful of lean kine and a few
ragged Boers.
Sydney morning dailies get their Federal
reports from Melbourne morning dailies. No
special staff is needed. Merely gallery critics and
commentators.
A beautiful figure of speech recently
cabled is that Russia is occupying Manchuria by
a process of “ geographical gravitation.” In other
words, Russia is a heavy article, and is dropping
down the map as heavy articles will.
EDWARD’S TITLE.
We’U find him a titular wheeze,
His Highness is easily pleased.
Since Britannia is Queen of the Seas,
Let Edward be King of the Seized!
A Yankee who lately visited Townsville
tells The Bulletin he saw in that town people of
all colours except green. If he went a bit further
North he would no doubt have met green citizens.
They must be green to so calmly endure the incur-
sions of the colored trash.
It is notified to the Boer leaders that, if
they don’t give up all hope of - their country’s
independence before 15th September, they are
liable to life-long banishment from Africa. Evi-
dently Freedom is going to shriek a second time
when Christian De Wet falls.
NOT NEGOTIABLE.
SHE: “ What a magni-
ficent carriage, Mr.
Stay the, the poet, has!”
HE: “ Mine gootness, Rebecca, you bring
my heart into my mout. I'fe been
drying to get fife pounts from him for
six months. Vere does he geep it?"
Govt.-Whip Bailes is sponsor for a Bill in
Vic. Assembly to abolish “tied” hotels, present
and future. But it has no more hope than Pea-
cock’s Convention scheme of dodging past the 48
Rich Landlords, a probable majority of whom are
partial landlords of pubs, by virtue of brewery-
shares. ~
Queensland’s exports show a decline of
£2,360,(>00 on previous year’s. Gold shows a de-
crease of £115,000, partly due to drought, partly
to the exhaustion of the tailings supply. Sugar
shows a decrease of £500,000 and tallow £160,000,
while the decrease on the export of wool amounts
actually to £1,200,000. Altogether the outlook is
not rosy, although the Treasurer poetically
asseverates that “the cloud which overshadows
the State is not so dense that we cannot see the
sunshine beyond ”
Apropos several things. There is an
allegation (surely the outcome of mere sectarian
suspicion !) that a letter addressed to a Sydney
clergyman during theConingham divorce-case was
opened and read in N.S.W. General Post Office,
and that the information thus gained was used at
the trial.
What enormous powers are placed in the
hands of the persons who from time to time
occupy the position of N.S.W. Minister for
Lands ! If a Minister were dishonest he could
make a cwt. of big bank-notes during even a short
reign by accepting presents from people in quest
of lease-renewals.
S. Law (Labor member) moved a resolu-
tion attempting to bind N.S.W. Government to
make no further Upper House appointments,
because the country was supposed to favor a
reduction in Assembly membership. All the
other Labor members voted against Law’s wooden-
headed proposal, and so did nearly everyone else.
Just imagine the effect of passing Law
M.P.’s justly-defeated motion in N.S.W. As-
sembly to forbid the See Government making any
additions to the Leg. Council. The only means at
the Ministry’s disposal for forcing the coming
Arbitration Bill into law is its power of swamping
the Upper Chamber with sufficient democratic
new blood.
Fortitude Yalley (Q.), bye-election, arising
out of Higgs’ transfer to Federal Senate, returned
previous member McMaster. Labor, in the per-
son of Charles Seymour, a not-too-palatable
pioneer, fought all the papers, parties, and sects
available, and went down by 222 votes. The
Valley laborites vow to have the seat again at the
general election.
Labor-leader Price (S.A.) was placed on
the horns of a dilemma in re his plea for a popular
Convention to formulate a scheme of Parliamen-
tary reductions. “Why,” asked an opponent,
“if you consider that the people are sure to gain
their will in this way, did yon stump the country
in antagonism to the Commonwealth Bill framed
by the Federal Convention?”
If N.S.W is to receive a new Constitu-
tion as promised by Messrs. See and Wise, one
issue put to the people should be as to whether or
not Crown-granted titles should be allowed to
citizens of the State. When Wentworth, Cowper,
and their crowd went in for a new Constitution
in 1853 they put peerage titles in the forefront of
their measure, but public opinion knocked the
item out. The same public opinion can now, if so
disposed, exercise itself as to whether or not the
present practice of sending handles across the sea
shall be longer tolerated.
London papers comment on the Indian
census for 1901 not being up to the mark. The
natural increase of the decade ought to have been
nineteen millions, but, alas, it was only seven
millions ! And as the other twelve millions have
escaped both the devil of the English sweater and
the deep sea of famine, they may consider them-
selves lucky not to be where they aren’t.
The size of some Q. State electorates is
startling. The Gregory covers 84,387 sq. miles
Bulloo 70,773 sq. miles, and the Carpentaria
70,700. They are represented in the Federal
House by Flinders M‘Donald. He could lose
England in his little lot. “Bill” Hamilton,
State member for Gregory, says hundreds of his
constituents have never seen an M.P.; and yet
they are discontented.
Rostrum : lie Victorian Government’s
suppression of Reynolds’s Newspaper. I get
my copy by post from London every week. Is it
only the bulky Customs Joss that is an ass and a
hypocrite ? Anyway, what sort of a people is it
that endures this dictation by Mr. Nobody, from
Nowhere, as to what it shall read? Denounce
English follies as you please, England is in many
ways a freer country than this.

THE NATIONAL CURSE.
ORATOR: “ What, gentlemen, is the curse of this
fair country? Aye, what, may I ask”
VOICIE PROM CROWD: “ Blanky, of course, you
old idiot !”
The W.A. Govt, buys its girl for £22, which is
paid out of loans. Then it makes an income out
of her in direct or indirect taxation, and that isn’t
applied to pay off the £22, but goes into revenue.
The N.S.W. Labor party is willing to give
£5OOO salary to the new State Governor. The
Premier’s organ, the Australian Star, stops at
£3OOO. Funny, to find See outbidding the Arthur
Griffith crowd. The Labor party is willing to
allow him a reasonable sum for entertaining;
the Star nothing. Apropos, the late Thomas
MTI wraith, when Premier, knocked off the enter-
tainment allowances of the Queensland Governor.
Kitchener has grown so weary of the
manufacture of bogus “atrocities by Boers’’that
he has given orders that all cablegrams con-
taining accounts of alleged Boer atrocities shall
be detained till the stories contained therein are
verified. As a criticism of the lying newspaper
atrocity-monger this is about the roughest thing
up to date. If the same system had been adopted
five years earlier there would probably have been
no war at all.
Of the six N.S.W. Federal Senators not
one voted for the proposed new clause in the
Postal Bill refusing 1 to subsidise mailboats employ-
ing Lascars or other colored seamen. No other
State of the Commonwealth was so disgracefully
misrepresented. There were nine votes for white
labor (Victoria, 1; Tasmania, 1; Queensland, 2;
8.A., 2 ; Westralia, 3); and 17 votes for Lascar
labor (W.A., 1;. Queensland, 2; Tasmania and
S.A., 3 each ; Victoria and N.S.W., 4 each).
A Bulletin correspondent :
The causes of the delays in the issue of leases, Crown
grants, &c., by Vic. Lands Dept. First, the enormous
amount of work cast upon contract-surveyors (pre-
viously nearly starved out) by the splitting-up provisions
"f the Land Act, 1898. Some surveyors have on hand as
many as 600 orders which, in rough country, will take
over a year to clear up. Second, the red-tape provisions
of said Act—you can't blow your nose without reference
to the Board of Land and Works and the necessity of
obtaining the tardy signature of the Governor-in-
Oouncil. Fancy a private firm hampered by these awful
clogs on movement in any direction. Third, ever-
increasing political patronage and interference.
The explanations of two N.S.W. gx-
Postmasters-General and an Attorney-General
and other N.S.W. and Vic. officials have at last
begun to make it understood in these two States
that the alleged “letter-opening clauses” in the
Federal Postal Bill only establish much the same
practice that N.S.W. and almost every other
State has been existing under all along. The
trouble is that not one man in 100 has the least
idea what kind of system he lives under, and
when he sees himself in the glass for the first time
he gets excited, and thinks it is somebody else,
and says, “Eject that shocking-looking object off
the premises ! ”

The most humorous thing about recent
proceedings in the Senate is little Zeal’s attempt
to outdo the most devoted Labor men in his
extreme democracy. Sargood’s candidature for
the Presidency was the first thing to cut Zeal off
from his old Tory pals, for he made a certainty of
the billet, and now blames Sargood's own aspira-
tions for the election of Baker. Zeal,
owns the brains of the Council trio, such
as they are, saw that the ability of the
Senate generally was such that said trio
was going to be badly side-tracked, and
thought it best to whoop for the top dog
with violent revolutionary interjections.
Simon Fraser, not being competent to>
realise his own incompetence, persists in
his old Leg. Council attitude, and in
three weeks attained the proud position
of butt of the Chamber. His rising is
always greeted with laughter, and his
labored efforts to be impressive never
fail to give openings to old man
M ‘Gregor, the comic S.A. Laborite. Sar-
good stands midway between Zeal and Fraser, and ;
tries vainly to maintain his prestige by speaking
seldom and by being mildly Liberal in his-
views; but nobody takes him seriously,
and he evidently begins to realise that his
chance of ever being in a Federal Govt, is
vain. Vindictive democrats, who suffered
by the action of the old Peg. Council, now
spend happy hours in the Senate gallery
gloating over the discomfiture of the old-
time gods of Reaction.
Jawbone Neild told the Federal Senate
the other day that he hadn’t heard any “ direful
tales of oppression” in N.S.W., and urged the
Senate not to hurry any attempt at Factory Legis-
lation. Truly nobody has so large a mass of
cotton wool in his ears as the man who doesn’t
want to hear. If Jawbone Neild had read the
evidence published within the last few weeks in
the Sydney papers he would have heard enough
tales about oppression, sweating, long hours,
miserable wages, and utter wretchedness in
N.S.W. to make his blood boil—if he could leave
off thinking about his beautiful military millinery
long enough to boil.
Cabled that “ the consensus of Cape
opinion favors the Boer marauding bands being
declared outlaws.” Cape English opinions, of
course— i.e., a minority opinion. And naturally
enough, when Naboth refused to let Ahab seize
the inheritance of his fathers, Ahab plainly
favoured the policy of outlawing Naboth and
putting an end to the disgusting practice of a man.
sticking to his own vineyard. But suppose the
Boer Naboth outlaws Ahab : what will happen te
the much more numerous bands of English
marauders ? What would have happened to the
fat Yeomanry who fell asleep regularly after
dinner ? Two can play at the outlawry game.
A bit of newspaper gossip ;
The Age support of the Peacock Govt, is being pur-
chased in a peculiar manner. Peacock himself doesn’t
care a fig for the Age support. It would take very little
to induce him to throw up the political sponge. There is-
the possibility of a great fortune in his business of legal
manager. He has a reputation in the English market,
and can handle hundreds of thousands of pounds any
day. Already he is practically an independent man. It
is said in newspaper circles that when one of Syme’a-
minions told him that if he wanted the Age support he
would have to supply them with “ first news ” of every-
thing that was going, Peacock replied : “Go to h .
I’ll please myself about that.” Other members of the
Ministry are not so independent. They sneak news to
the Age office, in the hope that S.vme will keep his
hounds off. One somewhat-necessitous Minister leaves
advance copies of his Bills in—well, peculiar places, for
Age men.
This is more or less how newspaper-scribes-
are talking in their private letters from Mel-
bourne :
There are strong indications of an early break-up of the
Barton Ministry. The trouble is coming from within
rather than from without. Barton and O’Connor are the
only two Ministers who pull in the same direction. Every
other member of Cabinet is doing just what he dern well
pleases. Turner is in despair of being able to keep-
Barton within reasonable bounds in the matter of ex-
penditure. He has informed his chief that, i« future, he
will take no responsibility at all in the matter of spending
money ; Barton must give his consent in writing on.
every one of Turner’s accounts before they are paid. Lyne-
and Deakin, who have been “feeding fat an ancient
grudge ” against each other for years, are openly at
loggerheads. When Barton goes on the Federal Bench—
he will be Chief Justiee (or possibly he may
be High Commissioner) which will be a fair reward for
his services in the Commonwealth movement —Deakin
wants to be Prime Minister. It has been the custom in
Victoria for the Attorney-General to rank next after the
Prime Minister. Lyne objects to this procedure, ancb
says it has been the practice in N.S. Wales for the
Colonial Secretary to receive the mantle of a departed
chief. Result is that Barton has had to order a round
Cabinet table so as to appear to favor neither aspirant.
[All Australian Cabinet meetings, so far as The.
Bulletin knows, are held at round tables.] Kingston
makes no secret to his friends of his utter dis-
gust at Barton’s leadership in the House. After one-
of the recent sittings, when Barton had blundered badly,
Kingston remark, d to one of the members, “ Did you
ever see such damnable leadership in all your life ! ” He-
never speaks on any subject outside of his own depart-
ment ; and, indeed, Ministers generally leave the debate
on any question to be carried on by Barton as far as they
are concerned. Not a voice is ever raised from the
Treasury benches to assist the chief on a ticklish point.
Forrest doesn’t care a dump about anything except his-
transcontinental-railway. Along with Lyne he believes,
in his inmost soul, that Federation is a mistake, and that
in ten years the people of this country will be shouting as.
loudly for a reversion to the old state of things as they
shouted for Federation.

VITADATIO
Is Doing.
Wonderful Cure of Sciatica, Lead
Poisoning and Rheumatism.
BEDRIDDEN FOR THREE MONTHS,
NOW THOROUGHLY CURED.
Cured with Eight Bottles of
Vitadatio.
Mr. W. Webber,
Proprietor VITADATIO.
Dear Sir, —For upwards of one year I have owed you a
debt of gratitude which no words of mine, however far-
reaching in their effects, can ever repay. Many times
when I have seen you in the streets of Launceston my
conscience has always condemned me for not telling you
the facts of my case ; for, after all, I always think
“ Honor to whom honor is due,” is a motto to which
every public benefactor has a just claim. Scores of
people in this city know that I have been the victim of
great suffering for the past eight years from LEAD
POISONING, RHEUMATISM, and SCIATICA. I tried
all patent medicines and liniments I could think of.
At last the Sciatica got the mastery of me, and I could
not turn in my bed for upwards of three months. Anyone
who knows the torturing pains of Sciatica can better
imagine than I can describe what I suffered those eight
long years, and especially the three months I was bed-
ridden.
I tried the skill of several of our local doctors, and then
went through a course of treatment in our City Hospital,
but got very little better. In a state of desperation and
almost hopeless despair, I went at great expense to
Sydney, in the hope that some relief from pain might be
obtained by treatment in that city. I was treated by
several of the Sydney doctors, and without in any way
reflecting upon the' skill of any member of the medical
profession, all my hopes were doomed to despair, and I
returned to Launceston no better than when I left.
Many times during those years I was urged by my
friends to use VITADATIO, and now I frankly admit
there was an amount of prejudice lurking in my mind
against it, because of it being a local preparation. But
the most astounding part of the whole affair is that eight
bottles of your VITADATIO, purchased from one of our
local chemists, effected the great cure in my case, of
which I am proud to boast. I have never been laid up a
day from work since the cure was effected. The hale and
strong may be slow to believe the facts of my case, and it
is a very small matter to me whether they do or not; my
hope is that none of them will ever have a similar ex-
perience, but I do hope that any fellow-sufferer who may
read this will be induced to try the virtues of VITA-
DATIO, and thus prove it to be a conqueror of pain, and
a positive boon to suffering humanity. Of course, I can
only speak as I find, and, personally, it is my opinion,
based on experience, that the invention of VITADATIO
may rightly be classed as one of the most valuable dis-
coveries of the nineteenth century. I cannot but speak
well of it.
You have my full consent to use this account of my
case in any way you think fit. —Yours very truly,
ANDREW MILLER.
51 Canning-street Launceston,
August 30.
For further particulars,
8. A. PALMER,
184 Pitt Street, Sydney „
