The new Sydney “Naval and Military Club”
marks another step forward by the professional
warrior-caste in Australia. Naturally, the. new
club will concentrate influence and pull strings ;
and the purely citizen-soldier is likely to find
himself in a colder place than ever. Militarism
reckons that it is seated firmly on the neck of
Australia now; and the workers are likely to
sweat for it as SiNBAD sweated for his Old Man of
the Sea. Serve the workers right; they allow
themselves to be bamboozled by the Jingo press
into electing Jingo politicians to Parliament, and
when the democratic ass suffers himself to be nose-
ringed and led along to conscription for British
wars, he is merely asserting his ass-prerogative.
*
In another column Antiuric, a new
specific for rheumatism, is advertised. The price
is reasonable, and if it is only half as good as its
proprietors claim they can prove it to be, a large
sale should ensue.
The Bulletin is glad to see that N.S.W.
Supreme Court coincides with its view of the
G-oulburn case, in which James M'Cormack, a
dairyman, was said to have been overpaid £lO by
a bank-teller, when cashing a cheque. This
M‘Cormack denied; it was simply the tellers
word against the dairyman’s ; yet the Goulburn
magistrate fined M'Cormack £2O and costs, lhis
decision the Supreme Court has now upset.
The stranding of the P. and O. liner
Sobraon on Tung Ying Island recalls the fact that
the name is considered unlucky among sailors.
The present Sydney training-ship Sobraon was
on the sailors’ black-list. On one of her passages
to Australia there happened on board an ex-
traordinary number of deaths, a fire and a mutiny.
The Queensland Government declares thac
it will send its seventh contingent to Africa
despite the Federal Government’s prohibition,
and Philp of the Half-Million Deficit says, “This
is entirely a State matter and has nothing to do
with the Federal authorities.” Which, considering
that Queensland of its own free will accepted a
constitution by which all military affairs are
placed exclusively under Federal control, is a
truly magnificent slab of arrogance. All the
same it is possible that Philp reckoned all along
on advertising his Loyalty, and being saved from
the cash consequences by Federal interference.
With a shortage of over £500,000 in one year even
Philp is hardly likely to be craving for more
military expenditure, especially now when the
enthusiasm is so dead that the despatch of ten
contingents would hardly fetch one additional
vote.
If the Government puts its soul into the
work this session N.S.W. should have. B. It.
Wise’s excellent, workable, and democratic Com-
Sulsory Arbitration Act in force by next year.
,ut it must not be forgotten that what Australia
wants now is Federal legislation in these matters,
much more than State legislation. The Federal
Parliament has announced by resolution that it is
ready to undertake the whole work of Labor
Legislation if the State Parliaments will only em-
power it to do so. If either N.S.W. or Victoria
will lead the way with an Act declaring that, so far
as concerns its territory, it hands over the whole
business of Labor Legislation to the Federal Par-
liament, the surrender to take effect as soon as
the other populous State and one smaller State
does likewise, and the State to retain all its powers
until then, it will set the ball rolling. Some State
must lead the way if any good is to be effected,
and it can’t do so too soon.

Each and every official utterance re the
farm-burning policy in the Transvaal is to the
effeot that looting is absolutely prevented, and
houses burnt only as a military necessity. A
N.S.W. trooper, in a letter published in New-
castle Herald says:—
I got 101b. tobacco, one Kruger sovereign, and some
silver pieces. After we loot the houses, if we find any
arms or ammunition, we burn them down.
By this trooper’s showing looting is the regular
practice, whether there be arms in the houses or
not.
The question as to why King Edward sold
his late mother’s sherry by auction when he might
have graciously, royally and charitably divided it
among the London hospitals doesn’t seem to have
been answered. In the light of H.M.’s unex-
pected fondness for pomp and circumstance in
ceremonial, the knocking-down of those dozens of
sherries to the highest bidder was a strangely in-
consistent and undignified transaction. It set the
snobocracy snorting and the mob jeering. And
the Crown can’t afford to invite ridicule.
“ Talbingo ” discourses philosophically on
women:—
“ E.D.’s ” “ Words about Women ” (May 25) are not
merely pleasant satire, but hard, three-cornered facts.
But, by way of reply—Woman is, and always will be, what
men demand of her. Thus, she will go to the stake for
Truth’s sake, and tell a lie about her age, because men
demand she should be a heroine, and throw her aside as a
worn-out chattel as soon as. the first bloom of youth is
passed. Me the selection of a husband and a hat: she
considers more the unimportant frivolities of life than
matters of deep import, because 99 per cent, of men
prefer their wife to be a squaw instead of a companion.
Every man has a harem, and no two individuals therein
must be similar. First, his mother must be a dear old
fool, blind to all his faults and ever ready to help him in
all his scrapes. Second, his sister must be attractive and
good, a girl of whom he can be proud, and who
will help him think he is too good for his wife.
Third, he demands his wife to be spotless. She
must be a noble mother and a good advt. for the
choice of her husband; also a peg on which he
can hang all his shortcomings, but she must suffer
unmurmuringly and with gratitude all delinquencies of
her lord and master. Fourth, he requires his mistress to
be dashing, voluptuous, and notorious for her amours.
Fifth, his “ chum ” must be a girl with plenty in her,
self-willed, vigorous, and refreshing. Some of each of
these styles of femininity are necessary to man, and it is
to the attempt to supply the demand in one person that
the instability, whimsicality, and variability of the female
character are due.
Woman’s aggravatingness is owing to the fact that a
man holds cheaply that which is his and howls for that
which he cannot get. Thus he crushes remorselessly
the heart of the little household angel who, on his daily
return, meets him with smiling, never-failing constancy,
while he goes off his head with love for her who imbues
him with a presentiment that he may come home any day
to find her flown to the bosom of another. Man should not
complain when woman heaps trouble on him, as it is
merely the case of the too-venturesome inventor when
some of his machinery “ busts up.” Woman is as she is,
vain and weak, and such she will be until men, out of
their pjvn nobility, recognising her a creature of heart
and brain, despite her physical inferiority, demand some-
thing more of her than a toy to be cast aside when the
mechanism becomes familiar —a machine to be replaced
on showing the least signs of wear and tear.
The fame qf E. N. Braddon, of Tasmania,
reached the land of Twain a short time ago. But
he wasn’t heard of as a statesman or humorist
(both of which he is), but as a champion axe-
wielder. True, his renowned Blot became known
in Yankee-land ; still, it was chiefly as a woodman
he was heard of over there. This is a letter he
received from a N.Y. firm of manufacturers :
Mr. Blot Braddon, Tasmania, near Brisbane, Queens-
land, Australia. Dear Sir. —Having heard of you as a
champion wood-chopper, we desire to call your attention
to the merit of our axes. The skilled choppers of this
country pronounce our axes to be by far the best that
are made, and we think if you give them a trial you will
say the same. We especially recommend our black, flint-
edge, tempered axes, and if you inquire at your iron-
monger’s for them, and try one, we are satisfied that you
will never use any other brand of axes. In case you do
like and use the axes, we shall be greatly pleased to hear
from you.
The ex-Premier was at the time taking a great
personal interest in an inter-State chopping match
that was to come off in his constituency of West
Devon, Tasmania, and somehow the news got
abroad that he cut down trees personally.

The Pistol and the Bottle.
The man who has once driven a burglar out of
his house with a pistol is likely to keep the
weapon handy by for use in the future.
On a similar principle Mrs. Elizabeth Langmaid
is never without a bottle of Mother Seigel’s
Syrup where she can lay hands on it any day.
About four years ago she was taken bad with
what was called “a complication of complaints.”
The doctors said she had an abscess on one of her
lungs, and also indigestion and heart troubles.
And, seeing how she looked and felt, we should
have believed him without a moment’s hesitation.
“You can get an idea,” she says, “how bad I
was when I tell you I lay helpless in bed nearly
nine months.”
(That does give us the idea and no mistake.
Save for the hope of recovery—which seldom
quite perishes in the mind—l would as lief be
dead, and so have the trouble over and done
with.)
“Finally,” Mrs. Langmaid goes on to say,
“ when I got out of bed, all of me that could
waste away was gone. I was just a skeleton
covered by a skin. In truth they wrapped me in
wadding—for appearance and for such comfort
and warmth as the protection might give me.
“ Whatever my complaint was I always had a
dreadful pain in my sides and under the shoulder-
blades ; but the medicines I took had no more
effect on it than so much sweetened water would
have had.
“While in this miserable condition, I remem-
bered how different friends of mine had spoken
of the virtues of Seigel’s Syrup for many kinds
of ailments that nothing else seemed able to help.
“ Anyway I was sure it would be no mistake to
try it, and so I got a bottle from Campbell-& Co.’s
store in this town. Up to that time I always had
a great feeling of weariness and drowsiness after
eating, and could not shake it off.
“But to my delight I soon discovered that a
dose of the Syrup dispelled this almost im-
mediately, and by the time I had finished the
first bottle I was greatly improved.
“As you will suppose, I persevered in taking
the remedy until by degrees I got strong again.
Gradually, too, I picked up my lost flesh, and
recovered my former good health.
“Ever since then I keep a bottle of Seigel’s
Syrup in the house, and take a dose whenever I
feel out of sorts in any way.
“ You may publish this if you like, and I shall
always be glad to hear of Seigel’s Syrup doing
for others what it did for me.” Elizabeth
Langmaid, Market St., Muswellbrook. N.S.W.,
Sept. 26th, 1899.*

“Piles and Their Cure.”—The pamphlet
by Albert Sharp, entitled “ Piles and the Way to
Cure Them,” without operation, sent free, on
application to the Sydney Depot, 257 George-
street.*
Now this is a business par. I want you,
my dear Arini, to provide Atcherley and Dawson’s
Globe Tea for your next “At Home.” You can
get a canister of 241bs. for £1 Bs., the former price
of which was £2 2s. A. and D. are able to let
you save 7d. a pound because they’ve done away
with the army of canvassers they used to employ,
and the giving of credit. Send a P.O. or cheque,
and if you don’t like the tea you can return it, and
A. and D. will return you your money.
The two individuals who are charged with
attempting to stick up the bank at Henty
(N.S.W.) and ineffectually shooting the manager
(the manager proved to be much the more prompt
shooter of the two) appear to have been both reason-
ably well off. They had much portable property, as
well as a savings’-bank account apiece. Also they
had six books of Deadwood Dick literature, and a
large quantity of stamps. Whether it was reading
flashy literature or stamp-collecting which brought
about their troubles isn’t clear—probably the for-
mer. Anyhow, both the one who is in the hospi-
tal and the one who is in gaol talk in something
approaching the lofty strain which the large-
hatted cowboy in the sixpenny howl uses to his
Indian captors when he is waiting to be officially
scalped by the tribe. A law absolutely prohibit-
ing the sale of books with a picture of a large-
hatted person with a gun on the cover might be
w:orth trying.
A bookie who died in Maoriland lately waB
buried immediately after breakfast in order that the
fraternity might have an uninterrupted business day—the
occasion of a race-meet!
Yell by H. Cuthbert, of Victoria’s House
of 48 Rich Landlords, about the proposed popu-
larly-elected Convention to revise Vic. Constitu-
tion :
This House is not going to give up its privileges to men
elected on the One Man One Vote principle, or yield up
the trust reposed in members by their constituents, who
COMPRISE MEN OF THRIFT AND INTELLIGENCE THROUGHOUT
THE COUNTRY.
What an illogical old man ! If the Council’s con-
stituents are men of such intelligence, why can’t
the Council trust them to elect any one of their
intelligent selves that they please to be M’s.L.C. ?
Under the present Constitution, out of the Coun-
cil’s 129,000 intelligent electors, about 127,000
intelligent men are declared, by the property-
qualification for members, wholly unfit to sit in
the Council. If these men are intelligent, then it
is a gross insult to shut them out. If they aren’t
intelligent, then the Council is a House of Fools
elected by Fools.
A specimen of the reforms which ex-Lands
Minister M‘Coll, the man who always loomed so
large in the eyes of his messengers, effected in
Vic. Lands Department has just come to light.
Finding that many letters written to the depart-
ment were insufficiently stamped, M‘Coll ordered
that the surcharges should be collected from the
senders. For this purpose a special clerk was
employed. His duty to the State was most use-
ful. Whenever a letter arrived on which there
was 2d. to pay he paid the postman, and then
despatched a letter to the sender demanding re-
payment of the 2d. Sometimes sender paid the
money, more often he didn’t. The amount paid
by the department in surcharges totalled about
£3O a year, and apart altogether from cost of
stationery and postage on letters demanding re-
payments, the clerk’s salary cost the country £l2O
more than the sum recovered.

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Time (8.9) registered for Y.A.T.C. G.N. Chase
(about 4m.), Saturday, is speediest since the distance was
extended (in ’B7) from 2jm. Previous best was Larrikin’s
8. Hi, in ’96, with 10.9 up. Latest winner, Arcadia,
bumped 10.7. Official information is that first 3m. were
covered in 6.13 J, the last mile in 1.55 J, last 6fur. inl.26i,
and finishing Jm. in 57£.
* ", * » •
V.A.T.C. G.N. course embraces 24 fences.
Bethnal (13.4) went out at 9-2 against after being heavily
supported for days before. The third fence settled Ric-
carton, but neither he nor his rider was hurt. The
eighth brought Oroua (A . Williams up) a cropper, both
unscathed; Carbinier and Rackstraw blundered badly at
the 11th, when clever jockeyship of Dickenson and Withey
alone saved a fall. At the fatal 13th (with posts and rails
at the back) The Sheik sprawled without serious results.
The 18th dished Carbinier and the favorite, and the next
one brought Rackstraw flop. Nilus failed at the 21st,
where the chapter of accidents ended. Rackstraw’s rider
was thought to be dead till examination revealed nothing
more harmful than shock. Williamson (Nilus’s pilot) had
his collar-bone broken. With full 5 fur. still to negotiate
the field had dwindled to nine. Thence on Arcadia
(going well and willing) won in a canter by eight lengths
from Sir Arthur, who finished two lengths ahead of the
much-distressed second favorite —Freedom.
* * * •
Backers of Arcadia for the G.N. Hurdle at
Flemington, in which the gelding ran badly, must
have been mortified to see him run away with the
Steeple. The comparatively easy Caulfield jumps
give the hurdle-racers a good chance in the 4-mile
event, but, strange to say, it has almost invariably
fallen to a ’chaser of the strong and steady class. Arcadia’s
was 9sec. faster than the time of Chelsea and Domino, the
two previous winners.
• * * *
“Ancient” : First V.A.T.O. Steeple (2|m.),
run in ’B2, went to Left Bower, with Corrigan up. Prize
only 140sovs., with a 7sovs. sweep. In ’B5, when Gran-
ville won, of nine that lined up only four saw the tussle
out. In ’B7 the journey was lengthened to 4m., and
prize-money swelled to £5OO. Blue Mountain—trained
and ridden by Jim Scobie—scooped it. Next year, when
the race was worth a “ thou.,” S. Miller, with Eagley and
Mernder as named, supplied first and second. Long-dead
Tommy Corrigan scored his third success with Sir Wilfred
in ’9O. Two years later the greatest thing ever done in a
lepping event occurred. Miller’s marvel, Redleap, started
at 6 to 4, and won all the way from 10 others with the
back-breaker of 13.12 in the saddle. Daimio, in ’95, got
close to Redleap’s feat. He humped 13.3 to the front
with a field of 14 behind him. When Britomarte won in
’97 9of 11 starters came down. Domino carried 13.5 past
the post first in ’99.
* • * •
In 20 V.A.T.C. ’Chases only six favorites have
won. Hottest was .Martin Loughlin’s Game, at “ evens,”
15 years ago. Britomarte was the only winning mare.
* * » •
Save last year’s (17), Saturday’s was the biggest
field in 10 years. Eighteen turned out in ’9l. V.A.T.O.
’Chase has been on the cards 19 years, and—bar Gran-
ville’s 10.6, Eaglet’s 9.10, Kestrel’s 9.7, and Britomarte’s
9.s—Arcadia’s 10.7 is the lightest weight borne to the
front at the right end.
* * * *
Throughout Caulfield, Saturday, not a single
favorite got home, and only Lock (Oma Plate), and
Kawana (St. Aubin Welter), of punter-supported nags
scored to any extent. Arcadia, winner of the big lepping
event, has never been over a ’Chase course in public
before, hence sixteens and occasional twenties were ob-
tainable against him up to flag-fall. The big knock of the
day was M.L. prad Record Reign’s failure in Malakoff
Stakes. Heavily backed down to 10 to 9 (“ English
odds ”) he wasn’t in it at the finish with W.P. (Commotion-
Phantasma) who literally cleared out and passed the
judge four lengths to the good. Recent form was so
much against W.P.’s chance of annexing such a high-class
race in such fine fashion that while twenties were freely
offered any price could have been had for the asking.
Record Reign is booked for India, and ran in Kour
Sahib Patiala’s name.
* * * «
Stakes won at Caulfield G.N. meet totted up
£3720. Mr. J. Farrell, with Arcadia, scooped £1000;
then followed Mr. Row, £895—£850 of which came
through Repeater’s success in hurdles.
**m * *
First acceptances for Caulfield and Melbourne
Cups were made Monday. Fifty-seven have left the
former and 46 the latter since the weights were issued.
There are 110 still in Melbourne Cup. General entries for
A.J.O. Spring meet tot up 686, as against 677 last year.
Of the 117 originally in Epsom Handicap 45 still remain.
The Metrop has 3 more (48)—just half the number dealt
with by the han^icapper.

The age of chivalry is not dead. At Moorefield
(Sydney) course, on Saturday last, F. Kuhn, who was
riding Jeannette in the Stewards’ Mile, seized the bridle
of the unruly Pretence, and led her up to the barrier. As
Jeannette would probably have won had Pretence been
left, it would be interesting to get the opinion of Jean-
nette’s owner and backers, on this new phase of modern
racecourse politeness.
* • ! * *
P.M.G. Drake’s proposed interception of mail
matter to “ Tattersall ” and other sweep-promoters is glar-
ingly dishonest in the fact that he doesn’t propose to
suppress the gambling telegram, or the ordinary turf
commission letter, which, in most cases, includes money
for Ikey Mo. Many thousands of such letters and tele-
grams are delivered by the States’ postal departments
every year. Indeed, at Cup-time one bookmaking firm
receives hundreds of orders per day, bj’ wire and post.
The racecourses of Australia are furnished by the local
Government with telegraph offices for the convenience of
gamblers. And the section of the community which
deplores the rapid increase in the number of race-meet-
ings whereat these telegraph offices rake in the unhal-
lowed silver of punters and “ books,” is far greater than the
snuffiebustious section which objects to “ Tatt.’s” sweep
on a limited number of events. Does Drake purpose a
refusal of postal' department connivance at any sort of
gambling, or does he merely seek to crush the compara-
tively harmless sweep?
* * * *
“A race-meeting with the ’k’rect card in mourn-
ing ” (writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent) “ does
not conduce to a hilarious gathering, and Ascot pretended
to take its pleasure sadly. Society decked itself in black
and white only, and, following the American gang, un-
patriotically won by English horses’ defeats. In the four
days over £45,000 in stakes were battled for by 257 horses
in the 28 races, and the Yank, W. 0. Whitney, scooped
top with £6OOO odd. Lucky Bob Sievier did well,
netting nearly £3OOO in stakes alone with two horses.
Whitney ran 13 horses, 10 of which brought him prize
money. Very picturesque course—there were only two
classes evident—the ‘ swaggerest ’ and the ‘ very anyhow ’
people—no middle class, just Ist and 3rd. Saw the lath-
like Lester Reiff bring home.'four winners on the last day,
and, despite the hot sun, had he been riding a hammock
in an ice-chest he could not have looked cooler and more
blas6ly indifferent. He is the calmest, cleverest jockey I
ever saw, and a nice gentleman for a poker-party.”
“Among the flatites at Ascot t’other day,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ I found
old Jem Mace making dollar wagers with his patrons, and
we communed. He of the one-time-rubber-neck that
rendered for him a head-guard almost unnecessary, has
just turned 70, weighs over 15 st., and looks remarkably
healthy, though, of course, not fit. He is now managing
a pub. and boxing saloon on,a high-class scale in ‘ Brum.’
for which he gets (he said) a pound a day and his 1 keep.’
A very shrewd young person is Jem. Spoke kindly of old
Australian days, of his Melbourne pub., the V.R.C., —later
the Criterion—Bourke Street, and of Larry Foley, to whose
boxing and fortunes J.M. seemed to feel he had con-
tributed a foundation brick or two. He has now no inten-
tion of ‘ Austy ’ —ing it again. He has written his life and
experiences, and hopes to find a publisher for it shortly.
His punching work has deformed his hands, the back of
the palms being steeply arched to the wrist as if the
metacarpus had been permanently knocked bow-shape
from continual punching. A fairly modest, well-spoken,
interesting old chap I found the ‘ ex-champion of
England.”’

Cabled that “ Havilah ” White (N.S.W.) is
sending English stallion, Louis XIII. to Sidney. Said
he cost 3000 gs. when a yearling, but through an accident
was never trained. His dam, Elgentyne, gave the world
Briarroot, winner of the IOOOgs. Mabiile (grandam of
Louis XIII.) was the dam of Mazurka, and full sister to
Cremorne, winner of the Derby.

Reported financial wreck of R. S. Sievier
(“ Sutton ”) can’t be true. He still owns Sceptre, Laven-
gro, and Duke of Westminster, which would bring him
£40,000 any moment he liked to sell.
* * * *
Apropos Otto Cribb’s death : Although the
coroner’s jury returned a verdict of “accidental death” the
Crown is proceeding with the prosecution of the officials,
seconds and surviving principal.
♦ * * *
The late Peter Jackson’s historic fight with
Farnan, one of the last of the old bare-knuckle prize-ring
goes, occurred in a deserted quarry at Yarraville (Melb.).
The place was a natural amphitheatre, and spectators
were grouped about the rising rocks, with the ring staked
out on a patch of green. Farnan was a S. Melb. black-
smith, and, though the darkey was a comparative
youngster at the time, he gave the man of iron a severe
gruelling. One of the secrets of Peter Jackson’s Success
was his abnormal reach. With outspread arms a well-
formed man is supposed to measure between his finger-
tips as much as his height. Most men, however, are less
in spread of arms, but Peter Jackson, 6ft. lin. high,
-measured no less than 7ft. 6in. from one finger to the
other.
• « ' * *
’Ome heavyweight bruising contest in June
ended in Yankee middleweight O’Brien still retaining the
doubtful distinction from the aspiring Harry Newmier.
’Twas a good thumping affair. Both stood toe to toe
mixing things at lightning gait, fighting in holds and
rolling on the floor together: Yank’s remarkable con-
dition and greater physical force won in the sixth round.
Newmier, a barge laborer, is seven years older than
O’Brien, and was once walloped by Tas. Albury Clifford,
now of Sydney. There’s a fortune for an Englishman who
smashes O’Brien ; and he needn’t be a Peter Jackson
either.
* * • •
“ Old Timer ” : The Passing of the Australian
pugs, who lately bossed America is almost complete.
Only Dan Creedon now remains in the game. Fitz-
simmons has “ chucked ” it for good. Jim Hall is slowly
dying of consumption. “Gentleman” George Dawson
long ago retired to be all-round instructor of the wealthy
Chicago Athletic Club. Joe Goddard is quite dead athleti-
cally, though he tries to make believe he isn’t. Griffo
spends his time between pubs!’and police-stations. ’Tis
stated that while one-time 6ft. heavyweight, Jim
Hall, has wasted to the 8.12 class, Griffo’s load of tissue
would force him among the big fellows if he ever fought
again, which isn’t at all likely. Both Griffo and Hall
have frittered thousands in their time ; now neither has a
bean, though the latter still has friends. Pugilistic his-
tory bristles with like cases. With the exception of
Larry Foley, all Australia’s best fighters have finished in
poverty.
* * * *
Anent Irishman P. O’Connor’s recent remark-
able “ lep ” of 24ft. 9in. Same man has cleared 24ft. more
times than any other athlete in public, and may yet clear
the 25ft. Fry, Prinstein, Newburn and Leahy got over
23ft. at the same meeting. This latest world’s record
causes the apocryphal long-jumping feat of old-time
Phaulus, of Cretona, and Chienis, the Spartan, to become
less of an exaggeration in one’s eyes. First-named is
credited with lepping 55ft., and last-named 52ft. Possi-
bly both had poles or some other like aids !
* « * •
World’s broad jump record-holder, Peter
O’Connor, is 6ft. and scales 1J.4. Thin, wiry and muscular,
he is as hard as iron all over, and carries barely an ounce
of superfluous flesh His style of jumping is complete.
He runs, gathers up like a ball, and; rising high in the
air as if shot from a catapult, soars along and descends
gracefully and easily.
* * * *
Metallician Oxenham (120) got away with the
honors and spoils attached to Sydney Tatt’s. billiard
tournament. His opponent in the final was D. Melville
(70). From 2 to 1 before the opening, odds on “Oxy”
went up to eights. Biggest breaks throughout were :
Oxenham, 19 and 17; Melville, 17 and 16. Oxenham
ultimately finished 41 to the good, and whacked the first"
money (£5O) among the attendants.
• » • *
Amateur billiard supremacy of N.S.W. at
Sydney Sports Club is not remarkable for breaks nor
general excitement. Most competitors have adopted some
crack-brained worn deplume and so destroyed the per-
sonal interest. Best break to time of writing was Rum-
ball’s 80 (Monday night), which more than doubles any of
his other totals. Previously “Bill” Lyons’ 67 and 65
compiled against “ Te Moana” (Mcßride) were the top
figures. The winner of the Belfield-“ Pyramids ” match
(Tuesday) “ Mai,” Lyons, and Rumball were to have de-
cided the semi-finals Wednesday and Thursday evenings.
The final (1000 up) is fixed for this Friday and Saturday.
On form evidenced so far, Lyons’ chance looks rosiest, as
he shapes better and is a more consistent scorer than the
others.
* * * *
At semi-finals of Sydney first grade Rugby,
Saturday, 'Varsity fairly laid over South Sydney by 22 to
nil, and Glebe put Newtown down 13 to 3. ’Varsity
shaped excellently to the applause of a six thousand
audience. Same form would render the premiership a
good thing for ’Varsity, but the team is notoriously un-
reliable.
*****
The recent beating of Vic. by S.A at the
former’s own peculiar code of football was fair and square
—and the first on record, for any previous victories have
been over an “ alternative ” team on the Monday. In the
opinion of many, the game is being spoilt by excessive
handball, the knocking-on (which is allowed) being per-
sistent, and often hardly distinguishable from a throw
(which is not).
*•* . *
Latest anent Albatrott at ’Ome is that he’s
giving fielders a heap of leather-hunting. At “Lords,”
early last month, he compiled 18 (2, 4,4, 4,4) in one over.
A lot of nonsense was talked about Ranji.
“ never getting out ” if he came to Australia, but it might
come near being literally true of W. G. Quaife, one of
Maclaren’s latest recruits. He is a little man with infinite
patience—too much of it for three-day matches—but just
the thing when time does not matter. The latest aver-
ages show him on top with 867 for 14 innings (three not-
outs) ; average, 78, or 10 ahead of Fry, against Ranji.’s
40 and Maclaren’s 22! His last three innings were
centuries. Three years ago Quaife played from July 27
to August 10 without getting out. When all the rest of
Warwickshire were got rid of he just carried his bat till
the next match. His scores were 60, 117, 157, 61, 24, and
52, all not-out, except the last—47l runs before his
wicket fell.
• * « *
London Strand Mag. relates some curious
cricket incidents. Writer of the article was not aware of
the following, which happened on Melb. ground when
Stephenson’s first All England XI. met the Vic. players in
’62 : One of the Englishmen had his middle stump knocked
clean out by a ball; the hot sun having melted the var-
nish the bails were glued together, and did not fall. The
batsman was not out. During same match an English-
man, running fast in the long-field to catch a ball with
the sun in his eyes, missed the leather and caught a
swallow, which he placed inside his flannel-shirt for safety
till the game was over.
» » * *
M.L. pigeon shooting supremacy was decided
at Petone, t’other day. Twenty-one cracks competed.
G. H. Chavannes grassed 19 out of 21 and won, with D.
Fraser and R. J. Whittle, a dead-heat, second. Last
year the premiership went to J. Peat, who killed 20 to
Ghavannes’ 19, and Peat would not have been there but
for the suasion of Ghavannes who wanted a chum. This
time Peat’s bag was only 11.
« * * *
Australian pigeon-shots, Donald Mackintosh
and A. W. Eales have been making money at Home at
Clubs’ weekly competitions. At one big affair Eales
scooped in about £175 in one afternoon, but it is the con-
tinual winning of small sweeps that keeps their pockets
bulging.
• * * *
Once famous sculler, James A. Messenger, of
Teddington (Eng.), is dead at 75. He was English
champion from ’54 to ’57, and there were giants in those
days. The old man was “ head barge master and water-
man to the Queen ” for 40 years. Charles -and Harry
Messenger, of Double Bay, Sydney, are his sons,, and that
latest skilled oarsman, Charlie Messenger secundus, is a
grandson. Three generations of crack scullers !
* * » *
Considered likely that the Cycle Boards Bill
will pass M.L. Parliament. The tracks to be laid down on
roads will cost about £5O a mile, and it is estimated that
50 miles per year will be made when the Bill becomes law.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.—How do our legislators expect
us to keep the close-season for game when some of them-
selves are the worst offenders ? I have myself seen an ex-
N.S.W. Minister of the Crown out all day Sunday after
quail, when heavy penalties were attached to the game.
And he’s not the only one, by a long way.—Piccola.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin.—lt costs an owner £25, includ-
ing jockey’s fee, to run for V.R.O. Grand National
Steeple, the stake of which is worth £l3OO. For the
V.A.T.O. G.N. Steeple of £lOOO it costs £35. Now, what-
ever opinions some of us may hold concerning the
methods of V.A.T.O. office-management, it is certain that
the club is financially strong, and, being only an agent
for the owners, the coin collected ought certainly to be
distributed among winners. A uniform scale of charges
is urgently needed. Even Maoriland lags behind, for it
costs an M.L. owner anything from 1 to 5 p.c. in nomina-
tion and acceptance fees. Auckland and Canterbury
clubs are liberal to owners, but even they differ in their
tariffs. The clubs that study owners study their own in-
terests, and so profit goes with liberality and uniformity.
—B.M.
* * * *
“ Tattersall ” Adams once flew at much smaller
game. About 30 odd years ago he raced an old farmer,
named Cronin, for £2 a-side, on Wollongong racecourse.
Each rode his own nag. “ Tatt.” won, hands-down.

In the search for arguments against the
hated Factories Act, the Vic. Tory stoops very
low. The closing of a certain country tannery
owing to the outrageous and impossible rate of
wages fixed under the Act, or through its tyran-
nous operation in other ways, was a strong argu-
ment with the Fat party recently. And when it
was explained that this couldn’t be because the
Act didn’t apply in that district , Enabling of the
Jaw, speaking in the House of 48 Rich Landlords,
very nearly called Chief Inspector of Factories
Ord a direct Ananias. Enabling and the Argus
both quoted as evidence an alleged Government
Gazette in which the Act was extended to the
district in question. And it now appears there
is no such Gazette notice—only a notice autho-
rising the owner of that particular tannery to vote
in the selection of a Wages Board, with a view to
the possible extension of the Act to his district —
an extension which never cams off. But the
tanner who was crushed by an Act which was
never applied in his district at all is doing
good service as an argument against the Act all
the same.
The present Sydney pneumonia-scare is
largely due to the undue prominence given by the
press to the malady’s prevalence—and more
especially by the Sunday press. Lodge-doctors
generally find themselves busier on Mondays and
Tuesdays than on other days. The average lodge-
patient, who pays 4Jd. a week to his doctor for
attendance on his family of 10 children, has time
on Sundays to investigate his own and his
family’s symptoms, and he compares them with
those enumerated in the sensational interview with
one of the “leading” city medical men in the
Sunday print. Consequently, on Monday, Tommy
and Billy and Molly and Polly are all trotted off
to the doctor’s to see if they haven’t got “ pew-
monia.” Instead of calming the public during an
epidemic, the sensation-loving press does all it can
to scare it, and thereby drives it into the use of
so-called preventives for “warding-off ” disease—
that is, whisky. Although the press advertises
the virtues of whisky, it doesn’t mention the fact
that the whisky-pneumonia case is the hardest to
pull through alive.

The Westralian big gold-producers: Great Fin-
gall, 3928 tons for 63610 z. This monthly output will be
much increased, if not doubled, when the larger equip-
ment is completed. Within the next few weeks the lode
will be struck in its lowest level, and, if as good and as
big there as in several levels above, a substantial rise will
take place in these shares. Present price, £8 15s. It is
quite possible, as in many another mine in Westralia,
that the lowest level will be the poorest, but, as there
has been a gradual improvement with depth in this mine,
a big change for the worse is not likely to take place in
100 ft.
* * * *
Peak Hill (W.A.): Of this mine a local corres-
pondent says that no developments have taken place in
the main shaft, and the reef, Of which much was said
when struck, proved to be only a bulge, and has cut out.
June yield, 15300 z., tonnage not stated, but estimated to
have been 2400 tons. Directors growling about expenses ;
50 miners “sacked”; manager expects to run the 40-
head mill from the open cut, which is opening out fairly
well. Last month 100 bags of specimens were taken from
the rubbly wash —another indication that what is locally
known as the “ Patch ”is an alluvial deposit. There are
300,000 shares in this co., all but a few thousands of which
have been issued. Within the last three years they have
been up to £lO and down to £3. The mine has never paid
more than 4s. a year in divs., and has paid nothing this
year. It still has a market value of upwards of a million*
which is one of those things no Australian mining mail
can understand. * * *
London and Globe : Cabled that at last even
the directors of this swindling concern are pleading for
compulsory liquidation, and when it is granted writer can
picture the squirm of certain persons who run London
financial newspapers that have been born in corruption
and fostered in boodle.
• ♦ * •
Sons of Gwalia: 10,019 tons, 60340z5., and this
has long been going on without a div. But the co. has
added greatly to its plant, has done much development
work, and taken in additional leases so as to make sure to
have the underlay of the lode to a workable depth.
Lake View Consols : 9258 tons, 14,2450 z., valued
at £54,000 ; announced that the co. has decided to give
the money-value every month. Better still if it would
also give the total working cost, so the public would
know the profit made on this enormous production, the
second largest on the field. Shares, £7 I2s. 6d. less than
a third of what they have been, and less than half the
price they were when the monthly yields were not half
the size! The “rigging” of Lake View shares by the
London and Globe 00. has completely shattered public
confidence in mine and management.
* * *
Golden Horsehoe: 9044 tons, 15,4090 z. The
biggest gold and profit-producer of the State, and although
little more than IOOOoz. greater than last month’s yield
from Lake View, there is a marked difference in the mar-
ket valuation of the two mines. Golden Horseshoes are
over £ll in London, and as there are over 50,000 more
shares than in the Lake View, it will be seen that the
market-value of one mine is nearly double that of the
other.

12,2140 z. ; shares, at 18s., are much lower than when the
monthly yield was much less.

According to a recent cable :
Mr. H. H. Asquith, M.P., Home Secretary in the last
Liberal Government, addressing a meeting of the Essex
Liberal Federation, affirmed that the Liberal Imperialists
neither repented nor recanted their view that the war in
South Africa was forced upon Great Britain without
adequate reason and entirely against her will. . . . The
war in South Africa was most humanely conducted. . . .
He hoped in the future to see in South Africa a free and
federated dominion on the model of Canada and Aus-
tralia,
It is Worth while comparing the above with the
following expressions made use of by Lord Howe
wheti Britain Was prosecuting the war against
thei colonies of America over a century ago :
1 kno w too well England’s abounding pride and deficient
Wisdom. Her fondness for conquest, her lust for
dominion, and her thirst for a gainful monopoly will join
to hide her true interests from her eyes, and continually
goad her ori in ruinous distant expeditions both of lives
and treasures.
They both amount to the same thing, anyhow.
Editor Bulletin.—l wish, through your
widely circulated paper, to warn any Australians
who may think of going to the Phillipine Islands ;
as I have met several stranded here (Hongkong)
who came over with that intention. The islands
are still under martial law, and the Americans
are determined that no one but Americans shall
settle there. Also, all the old oppressive Spanish
laws are being upheld by the Yankee authorities,
and the place is worse for a British subject than
before the war. Even anybody wishing simply to
visit the islands has to get a permit from the
American Consul at Singapore or Hongkong with
whom he must also deposit his return - fare.
British steamers at any Phillipine port have to
have six soldiers billeted on them, and to find
these in food and liquor. In fact all the old
Spanish times are still in full force. Surely,
enough said !— B. Fitzalan Howard.
Judge Backhouse, in his report on the
Victorian minimum-wage system, states, no
doubt quite truly, that it tends to crowd out the
inferior workman. It i 3 possible to make special
provision for the aged workman, but the inferior
workman, under the new order of things, is likely
to have a bad time. When the employer has to
pay good wages he naturally wants good work.
This may be roafh on the inferior workman, but
it is certainly good for the country at large. The
country of slummocky, half-trained artisans never
amounts to much. It is always dependent on
other countries for a huge proportion of its pro-
ducts. If it builds up an export trade its products
are always selling at a low figure in the world’s
marine store amid the world’s general contempt.
The inferior workman (who is mostly inferior
because he doesn’t take the trouble to be any-
thing else) brings the country’s goods into such
discredit that even those turned out by the
superior workman (having the national discredit
on them) don’t fetch their fair value. If anyone
can invent a system whereby the inferior workman
can be crowded out, the tribe will, in time, cease
to exist. The new generation will, under compul-
sion, cease to be inferior. If no such system is in-
vented he will increase and multiply. The ten-
dency of the average man is to be as inferior as he
can be while yet making a living.

A certairi Southern Bohemian club
adopted the plan of caricaturing undesirables out
t>f the show because satire was the only thing that
could pierce the hide of one brilliant but erratic
member strongly suspected of dossing there. At
least, after several surprise visits by the committee
at an unearthly hour, he was found snoring sweetly
while his shirt, which he had washed out in the
bath and ironed with a tin pannikin, aired nicely
before the kerosene lamp-stove. As a financier
that member had points about him. He took an
overwhelming interest in the commissariat, and
whenever he found victuals even slightly reduced
he bought all sorts of nice things, and collected
the price thereof from the secretary next day per
medium of a note to the effect that “ I have this
day purchased for the use and delectation of the
members, the following goods, as per schedule
attached.” Then followed the schedule and the
bill for the amount. The secretary, the mildest
man that ever dodged a creditor, paid up and
angrily requested the Brilliant One to leave the
commissariat alone for the future. But the 8.0.
went,on serenely, buying just whatever he listed
and making wonderful excuses for his interfer-
ence (it seemed that he always interfered when to
have done otherwise would have jeopardised
the honor and credit of the club) till the swarm of
caricatures stung him so that he kept right away
from the institution. Then something happened
that drove the club to temporary suicide. One
pleasant morning two members who had been out
all night on press duty decided to go there and
have breakfast. It was hot weather and they
were wearing tennis shoes, or some sort of foot
gear that made little or no noise. When they
reached the door they saw an artistic member
sitting in a wicker chair, peacefully washing his
socks in the washing-up bowl, while on the gas
stove his week’s supply of handkerchiefs bubbled
away merrily in the tea-kettle. It was a pleasant,
idyllic sight, but it roused the club to fury, and
caused it to dissolve and form itself into a new club
which knew not those members who boiled hand-
kerchiefs in the tea-kettle. Shocks never come
singly, and it was about then that the bill for all
the goods “for the use and delectation of the
members” came home, with demand for imme-
diate payment. But the club still survives and
lias a good time on the whole.
At a recent Melbourne meeting the
Christian Social Union it was decided that, in
view of the violently conflicting evidence about
the Kanaka-question, “it was right to look to
the leaders of the Anglican Church for guidance,
and two members were accordingly deputed to
appeal to the Anglican bishops in Queensland
and Tasmania for light upon the subject. Now,
what advice will those bishops give? Why, the
same advice as is given by the Argus and the
Brisbane Courier— that we should take the nigger
to our bosom. What advice does Toorak give ?
What advice is given by the Sydney Points?
Why, Respectable advice. And that is what the
Anglican Church, as a church, will always give.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr R assmussen’s advt. on page
21 of this issue *

We have only to look at the fine buildings that occupy

The instrument-room was, as usual, one of particular
curiosity. Laymen always have a penchant for e>eing
and examining the polished steel articles "»th which so
many extraordinary acts are performed upon the human
body. Thi collection in this room is in every way up-to-
date. Whenever a new instrument comes to the front,
and its worth is proved beyond question, it is added to
this rare assortment. Another room full of absorbing in-
terest is that devoted to the galvanic batteries, of which
there are several magnificent kinds. The noted Game
batteries, the finest French makes, are here in perfection,
irrespective of their high cost. If any article or mechani-
cal and electric contrivance is found necessary in main-
taining the fame of this institution, the price is never
allowed to stand in the way. ~ •_» ~ „
The correspondence department is a veritaoie e\e-
opener. Its sacredness is a bar to a stranger’s admission,
but a full view of the work going on is had through the
glass doors. Half a-dozen typists are seen driving their
machine-writers at electric speed. To the principals only
are known the names of the persons throughout Australia
who are being treated as skilfully in their homes about
the metropolis and away in the interior as if they were
present in personal consultation at the institution.
The packing-rooms are likewise shut off from curious
and prying glances, and the w-ork of one department is a
mystery to the others. No better system for holding
secret the nature of sufferers’ troubles, for safe-guarding
their interests, and giving due heed to the feehngs and
sensitiveness of patients, could be imagined. Packages
are not plastered with advertising labels, they are put up
in such a way that only the names and addresses are in
What a practice is here revealed and what a consola-
tion must it be to these eminent specialists to know that
they are permanently curing and restoring to health and
happiness thousands of people w'hose lives were a burden
to them. The young, the middle-aged, and the healthy
are treated with the same amount of success. The pro-
digious nature of the work accomplished by these
specialists necessitates —not the stereotyped morning and
evening two hours’ consultation ticket, but their constant
attention at the institution from early morning till late
into the night. “I can assure you,” said Dr. Wallace,

Therefore it requires no argument to convince the
most pronounced of optimists that the field in any one of
the great and honored professions of the day is far too
vast for the thorough comprehension of the most per-
fectly brain-endowed man, or body of naturally-gifted
men. Yet in respect of the most delicate and difficult of
all professions— that of prescribing for the ills and
diseases of mankind—we find doctors, scattered over the
globe, off-handedly and without careful, minute, and
searching examination and diagnosis—without any know-
ledge of the past lives of patients—flippantly dashing off
prescriptions for every class of complaint, as if a cure was
as simple as falling over the proverbial log.
Thanks to the march ot civilisation, culture, and edu-
cation, people are getting* to know that modern medical
men who are worth consulting confine their practice to a
certain class of cases. Realising the utter hopelessness of
mastering and curing everything under the sun, and find-
ing that “gullibles” are not so plentiful as they used to
he, the wise and progressive men of medicine devote
themselves to one or other class of cases, arid work out
the remedies for them to the best of their skill and
ability.
What better and more striking illustration of the suc-
cess of Specialists in Skin Diseases, Heart, Kidney, Liver
Affections, Rheumatics, Sciatica, Nervous Diseases, could
the people of this magnificent continent, Australia, have
than the enormous practice of the famous practitioners of
the Electro-Medical Institute. Their skill, attainments,
position, and renowned reputation stamp them as the un-
doubted masters of the classes of diseases and complaints
which have been the study of their professional lives.
When we speak of electricity we think of Edison, why !
Because he dwarfs other men in this branch of science.
In the same wav, when we talk .of specialists, we at once
associate the names of the Freeman and Wallace Insti-
tute. So it is in every branch of scientific medicine;
some one or two men stand head and shoulders above
their fellows. .
It is the aim, object, and devotion of certain doctors to
he pre-eminent in some defined class of disease rather
than to tinker with a host of complaints about which
they can have but a hazy notion, and superficial know-
ledge. To use a hackneyed but expressive phrase, they
are^ 1 Jacks of all trades and masters of none.” Never
since the time of Rieord, the noted French Specialist,
have any men conducted so gigantic and successful a
practice as that contacted by the Freeman-Wallace
Electro Medical and SuificaJ Institute.
of hard work than any other medical men ; we do it in
the best interests of sufferers. We don’t keep them
waiting longer than we can help, and we suffer many in-

Nearly 50 years of experience has taught
Callaghan and Son that a boot in which the
leather has been truly cut (so that the strain is
evenly distributed) and then firmly sewn will last
twice as long as the carelessly-made boot. The
sterling qualities of The Callaghan Boot, and the
fact that it ever holds its shape has made the firm
immensely popular. The firm is now working up
a big country trade, having instituted the satisfy-
ing system whereby boots can be exchanged if
not suitable, or money returned to customers if
they are not pleased with the boots when they
receive them.*

Concerning Sheep Dip.—A good dip kills all
insects and their eggs, and thus gives to the sheep free-
dom from irritation, and consequent peace to feed and
rest. An immediate improvement in the state of the
skin, and in general health, is the first benefit, conducing
to a proper supply of yolk and a vigorous growth of wool.
The other great gain from a suitable dip is that a tip is
formed on the staple that, whilst not only protecting it
from wasting effects of the hot sun, binds the fibres
together and prevents dust and other matter from getting
into the wool. No farmer can hope to keep his head
above water if he neglects to use a good dip and goes in
for fattening ticks, etc., at the expense of wool and
mutton. Which is the best dip ? The Encyclopaedia
Britannica (latest edition) while condemning arsenical and
mercurial salves and washes says :—“ McDougall’s Dip has
been used for very many years and we can testify to its
efficacy and safety.”*
Having glutted its soul with the details of 1356
country towns, and read all about the 24,000 pastoralists,
and the names of 30,000 people who live outside Sydney
and its suburbs, Tub Bulletin feels justified in saying
that “ Hall’s Directory and Gazetteer of N.S. W.” is a useful
and valuable work. It is a mine of information
about every country town, and to such as go down to the
great wilderness on that mysterious mission known as
“ business ” it is full of information where they may find
victims. The only objectionable feature about the well-
bound production is that an enterprising firm of under-
takers grabbed the top of every page for advertising
pages. The writer has a bad cough and hates under-
takers.*

The publication of crowds of symptoms, testimonials and puffs of one’s skill and attainments savours too much of self-praise ; so the public
should look with a doubtful eye upon all those who “ doth protest too much.”
Dr Watson does not pretend to say that “ One Pound is sufficient to send," as stated in certain advertisements, for he considers that the
public are quite satisfied to pay a fair price for fair and legitimate treatment.

Stated that Mrs. Langtry, who has let her own London
theatre for a few months, will make a provincial tour
under engagement to a Mr. Mouillot, instead of running
the show as her own spec. If it is true that the sanguine
Mouillot has contracted to pay the full-blown Lily a
.alary of £2OO a week, she must be a wonderful woman.
About 50 years of age, no longer beautiful, even in her
stage war-paint, and not even a great actress. Yet she
can get £2OO a week as a “ star 1 ”
Re the “ definite ” decision of Melba to come along next
year. Two years ago writer saw letters from the can-
tatrice to some friends in which an assurance just as
definite was given, with the pathetically homesick adden-
dum : “ Get this in the newspapers if you can.”
‘ ‘ The most paralysing thing at Stafford House big charity
festival of snobbery (writes Tiie Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent) was Arthur Roberts’s pantomime of a girl
undressing in a bathing-machine. Quadruple-chinned
and nut-cracker-faced duchesses, countesses, &c., laughed
most plebeianly ; and Arthur, as usual, got right on to
the knuckle. It was a cruel shock to the snobs at three
guineas a head, who said, ‘ ’Ow ’orrid it was of the haris-
toeracy.’ ”
Kyrle Bellew expects great things of his Palmer River
leases (which include the old Ida). His camp is most
luxurious —tables, chairs, bed, and shower (the shower’s
all right). The canvas walls are covered with good pic-
tures, and a thick carpet adorns the floor.
London Referee was lately sending round the hat for
Alma Stanley, who “sorely needs help.” And yet Alma
Stanley for years commanded a salary of £3O, £4O, and
£5O a week. In ’97 she was starring in Australia with
Harry Paulton, and towards the end of their not too suc-
cessful tour she made a hit as the heroine of the old man’s
clever comedy, “ Niobe.”

, grew so turbulent in the d.c. that he had to be removed
by force, much to the detriment of the personal appear-

“ J.X.” : There was an Italian cyclist named Dani, and
the treadle-pushers of Melbourne seem to have got hold of
the idea that tenor Dani is identical with the other fel-
low, and that his voice.was first “discovered” at a
cyclists’ smoke night. Good voices in Italy don’t go un-
suspected by the owners until a casual discoverer comes
along. Boys dream of an operatic career before they as-
pire to triumphs on the track.
The drollest item in Wilson Barrett’s talks to the inter-
viewers is the statement that Williamson happened to
.catch him in a disengaged moment and persuaded him to
fill ;up the extraordinary gap in his “bookings” with
another Australian tour.
Maud Wheeler, writing from London to Sydney Truth :
—“ .. . Presently comes Ellen Terry, a tall, graceful
Woman, whose determined jaw and wide mouth could
never have been beautiful. The slightness of youth is
gone, but a little flesh is not unbecoming. She looked
about thirty, and wore a wig and diamond combs in her
hair. Her voice, to begin at the start, was. harsh and
broken, but as she went on it became mellow and ringing,
and the irresistible power of the actress came out. She
is powerful and pathetic and strong, with the very
essence of art. A very weary old woman, poor and for-
lorn in her old age, after a long life of hard work—yet,
what an actress !• And she has power to thrill, to make
you believe she was, indeed, that auburn-haired Henrietta
Maria whom poor, weak, wavering indolent Charles loved
so dearly. . . . Irving? Well, what of him? A man
comes on the stage with two pretty, golden-haired
children, clad in white. He wears a dress of
sombre black, his fair hair flows over the
point lace collar on his shoulders ; on his _ long,
white, elegant hands diamonds sparkle ; from his ears
droop pear-shaped pearls ; about his neck is a blue ribbon
with a star hanging from it; over one shoulder a purple
cloak. All is perfect. The man speaks, it is a mumble ;
he has no voice, or he is too tired to exert it. When he
does let it go it’s a hard voice with a strong, nasal in-
flection, with no power, with no music in it—a sort of
toneless voice, the sad voice of a very old man on whom
the weight of his long life is beginning to tell. Irving
is 72 ; and he, apparently, has not the physical strength
to act. The armor bows him almost to the earth in the
battle-field scene. The gesticulation was good, the make-
up perfect, but Irving seems done for. He doesn’t act;
he merely goes through the show very quietly, never
troubling himself to assume the character. The effect is
disappointing. One expects to see a great actor playing
a fine part* arid instead sees a man who mumbles away
without opening his mouth or troubling about the effect
he is producing in any way, except by being on the stage.
Irving did not pay his audience the compliment of any
effort; so that it was the most disappointing show possible
to witness.” At this rate, Irving must be about due for a
visit to Australia.
Actor-manager Robert Henry has been ordered by
Queensland Supreme Court to inhabit His Majesty’s com-
modious and well-ventilated gaol till further notice. The
first trouble arose through that agile person using
“ What Happened to Jones ” without the owner’s permis-
sion. The Supreme Court ordered him to leave off. This
was very moderate on the part of the Supreme Court ; if
defendant, instead of using Arnold’s comedy in Towns-
ville, had been found using Arnold’s money there without
Arnold’s authority, it would probably have been called
plain, ordinary stealing, and the result would have been
six months or more. Then defendant changed the title
of the play to “ What Became of Parker,” and produced
it under that thin disguise. The Chief-Justice said it was
a clumsy device, and sent R. Henry to gaol indefinitely
for contempt. Which is one of the circuitous ways of
the law. The actor-manager doesn’t “goup ” for unlaw-
fully using Arnold’s play to the latter’s loss, but
for insulting the pomp of the court by not leaving off
when ordered.
The names of the Italian opera people are not a severe
ordeal for the uninitiated this time. Benedetto, Dani,
Cannonieri, Cattadori, Tondini, Loiribardi, and Sapio can
be pronounced at Australian sight, so to speak. Bassich
and Vaccari (Bas-sish and Vack-cari) are correctly guessed
at, as a rule ; also Marco-mi-ni is an easy one. Most
people know that the g is dropped out of Travaglini, and
that the double z is pronounced as tz in Bozzoli, whereas
the one z in conductor Hazon’s name has the sound of an s.
Mick Simmons’ C. 8.8. briar pipes are like
good artists—they draw well.

Of a recent outburst of the Kloet volcano*
in Java (from the Northern Territory paper):
The outburst was attended by great loss of life. Tor-
rents of lava and hot mud flowed amid showers of ashes
and stones. Many natives and a few Europeans perished.
On an estate called Alas Kedarung, the manager, amid a
shower of stones, fled from a torrent of lava in a carriage
with his wife, two children, and a nurse. The lava over-
took them. The children, the nurse, the syce, and the
horses perished. The parents jumped into a clump of
bamboos and saved themselves. The lava also consumed
the superintendent of the estate and about 25 coolies.
Many coffee estates in the neighborhood were destroyed.
The country around was strewn with corpses.
Premier Peacock’s Bill for the reform of
Vic. Constitution is quite tame, after all, and cal-
culated to eat vegetables out of the hand. It
provides: (1) That both Assembly and Leg.
Council must first agree to the establishment of a
popularly-elected Convention; (2) The Conven-
tion is to draft a new Constitution ; (3) Both
Houses of Parliament are to consider it and sug-
gest amendments ; (4) The Convention is to con-
sider the amendments and finish its Bill, which
will then be submitted to a popular vote ; (5) Even
if the people endorse it by a 10 to 1 majority it
must be accepted by both Assembly and Council
before it becomes law. And the Tory party
bellows mournfully that this is a dark and unholy
scheme for rending from the 48 Rich Landlords
their just authority. Unless Peacock is far more
ferocious than his Bill the prospect of anything
b“'"" done is very small.

ENERAL DEBILITY.
Two Years’ Sufferings.
-A- IFHHEILTID I3sT USTIEIEID.
The Case of
Mr. ROBERT GRAHAM.
{Reported for “ The Star.”)
The increased facilities for recreation
Afforded to shopkeepers and their em-
ploy es under the provisions of the Early
Closing Bill are often appreciated by people
who do not come directly within the
operation of the Act. It gives “ outsiders ”
an opportunity of meeting folks e joying a
half-holiday in the middle of the week who
are not at liberty at any other time, except
after business hours. Knowing that Mr.
Robert Graham, of No. 11 Wattle-street,
Chippendale, was always disengaged on
Wednesday afternoons, our reporter
thought it an opportune time to obtain an
interview in relation to his indisposition,
and was rewarded by finding the gentleman
mentioned sitting on his verandah.
*‘l have called to ask a few question
about your illness.” began the scribe.
“ You are quite welcome to ask as many
questions as you like, and I will freely
answer them. ”
“ What was your complaint, and how
long were you ill ? ”
“ I suffered from debility, in conjunction
with indigestion, for fully two years.
I had been engaged in sewer work for
some time, and the bad air played havoc
with my constitution. My health had been
very indifferent for a long time before I
paid much attention to it, and then it was
compulsory, for the disease had worked so
slowly and so stealthily that I did not
give in till it had mastered me completely.
I thought that the unhealthy feelings would
§o away when the sewer work was finished ;
ut I was greatly mistaken. I could not
go on working through the violent pains in
my head and chest, which afterwards ex-
tended all over my body, and 1 got very
feeble in the )e s. My appetite became
fickle, and gradually died away altogether.
Anything I did succeed in getting down had
a nauseous taste, and p oved quite unpalat-
able. I could not sleep well at night. 'I
went to bed feeling tired and weary, and
after sleeping an hour or so I had to put
in the remainder of the night wide awake
When it was time to get up I had to drag
myself out of bed with much reluctance, not
having enough energy to dress. I could
nob look at my breakfast, and would start
the day with only a cup of tea on my
stomach. My strength gradually caved in,
as I could not take any food to sustain it,
and I lost all animation and vitality. Water
was my principal drink, being always
thirsty, My Btomaeh was too much upset
to allow of my taking anything else, so you
may imagine how debilitated I was. There
was not an hour of the day or night that I
could say I was free from a depressing
languidness that made me melancholy be-
yond description. A sign of my weakness
was the way I used to perspire without any
exertion. Before I was taken ill I was as
hard as nails, and could do a good day’s
work without raising a sweat. In other
ways the debility made itself manifest,
notably by the symptoms of extreme
nervousness from which I suffered. I
used to think I had not got •in J nerves at
all, but now the least thing ?. ouid startle
me, and set me all of a tremble. I fought
hard against my affliction, and often forced
myself to eat when I had not the Bjightesb
inclination for food. The result was usually
very disagreeable, my enfeebled digestion
constantly turning on me. I experienced
dull, achjng pains between the shoulders
shortly after these forced repasts, and it
was not long before the food came up agaiaj
The rgtchihg Involved in the
made ipy headaches, from which I was
never free, iuerease in violence. My
tongue was coated with a thick, slimy sub-
stance, which made my breath disagreeable.
The fellows with whom I worked used to
remark how awfully miserable I looked,
and in truth I was frightfully thin and
pale.”
“ What medicines did you take during
your illness ? ” (*.
“I think I must have taken every
medicine under the sun. At all events I
visited the hospital for six weeks, and
brought back all sorts of physio and pills,
but they did not benefit me in the Slightest
degree. I did not get any relief from pain,
neither did they promote an appetite. Ab
the end of six weeks I gave the hospital
best, and consulted a doctor. He advised
me to take Clements Tonic. I felt rather
doubtful about his advice. After having
taken so many mixtures before. I became
very incredulous about medicine'', and so I
sought the advice of another doctor, who
also recommended me to take Clements
Tonic. Withbut more delay I procured a
bottle, and the«effect was most remarkable.
I had believed that my nervousness could
not be cured by any medicine, but to my
unspeakable delight I felt my nerves more
restful in three or four days after vne intro-
duction of Clements Tonic. Sound refresh-
ing sleep came back quite naturally, and
my terrific headaches were amongst the first
of my tormenting symptoms to be subdued.
The bodily pains left me one by one, and
my strength returned as my appetite got
better, thus enabling me to eat the food
that my worn-out frame so much needed.
There was an entire absence of pain in the
stomach after eating, and I was never after-
wards in doubt about my meals being
properly digested and doing their work
thoroughly. Clements Tonic restored my
bowels to healthy action, and my tongue
lost its obnoxious roughness and became
clean and sweet again. The excessive
perspiration no longer remained to remind
me of my weakness, as I became stronger
and stouter every day. I took quite a
different view of life under my altered con-
ditions, and the feelings of melancholy
troubl ed me no more. W hen I had taken four
bottles of Clements Tonic I was quite strong
again, my nerves were as sound as a bell,
and my digestion was all that I could wish it
to be.” 4
“Can I report these particulars in the
papers.”
“You may with pleasure. I owe my
restoration to health solely to Clements
Tonic, and if any people should happen to
read about my case who are suffering in the
same way they may be glad of my experience.
Publish how and when you like.”

UNEMPLOYED YEOMANRY.
(To the Editor of “London Daily Telegraph.”
Sir, —I should like to thank Col. Lucas for his spirited
appeal to employers which appeared in your issue of the
12th inst. Far from exaggerating the case for the re-
turned Yeoman, he has, if anything, understated it.
Take my own case as an example, and I am only one
among hundreds perhaps more deserving or more neces-
sitous than myself. I have been home some six weeks,
and have made every effort to obtain a clerkship in the
city, but am always met with the same rebuff. When I
say that I have been away on active service for more than
a year, I am told that they couldn’t think of engaging
me, despite my qualifications, the supposition being that
during my absence I must have got out of touch with
city affairs.
Just before I went away I witnessed the general exhi-
bition of “patriotism” caused by the relief of Lady-
smith, and heard men shouting that
“ He’s out on active service,
Wiping something off a slate,”
but I never imagined that, after wiping the slate, and
bearing the brunt of a hard campaign, we should be cold-
shouldered when we returned, and refused employment
upon such paltry grounds by the very traders whose
interests we sacrificed so much to protect. There are
hundreds of men who can substantiate my complaint, but
if you will spare a little space for the insertion of this
letter perhaps city employers may be brought to recon-
sider the course they are adopting.
I am, Sir, faithfully yours, YEOMAN.
June 14.
Moral: Fighting for Cohen pays no dividends.
Except to Cohen.
General Bailer, talking at the Colonial
Club, London, recently, said something good for
Colonials. Here’s his tailpiece :
He hoped they would understand in what he had
endeavored to say that he meant that the worst thing in
the world that could happen to any country was what
was known as the Jingo feeling.
“ Castro’s Last Sacrament and Other
Stories,” by Albert Dorrington (“Alba Dorion ”)
is the best book of short stories yet produced in
Australia. The S. Daily Telegraph recognises
the author’s “absolute genius.” The S.M. Herald
says the volume “ contains dramatic pictures
which the best of our artists in words might well
envy.” A handsome cloth-bound volume, 350 pp.,
post-free from Bulletin office for 4s. 6d.
This is a dangerous deceitful world for any
widow in comfortable circumstances who goes
travelling around with only a marriageable
daughter to look after her. A recent cable
reported the arrest in London of two men from
Melbourne on a charge of swindling Mrs. and
Miss Renwick, also from Melbourne. The evi-
dence seems to show that the spielers either
followed the susceptible relict and her offspring to
England, or discovered them there by accident,
and did a “mash.” Probably they tossed-up to
decide which of them should tell the sweetest,
sweetest story ever told to the young ’un, whilst
his mate was winning the affections of mamma.
Anyhow, they took the two confiding creatures to
Paris under promise of a double marriage, got
£BOO from them, and fled withoi t troubling to
carry out the contract. Then the elder victim
reluctantly concluded that the irresistible Aus-
tralian gentlemen had been fraudulent wooers.
There are limits to the amorous credulousness of
even the Widow O’Brien that Johnny Sheridan
created.

?&e PIANOLA
“It is astonishing to see
this little device at work
executing the masterpieces
of pianoforte literature
■with a dexterity, clearness,
and velocity which no
pla> er, however great, can
approach. Every one who
wishes to hear absolutely
faultless, free from _ any
k ind of nervousness, piano-
playing should buy a Pi-
anola. It Is perfection
I. J. Paderewski.
Has arrested the attention of the
musical world. Its claims have been
investigated and verified by the great
pianists themselves.
This verification of its claims makes them interesting
worthy of attention.
[HE PIANOLA is an instrument by means of which any one
can play the Piano. .
This is accomplished by simply rolling the Pianola m
front of any piano, allowing the felt-covered fingers to rest
upon the keys of the piano, then inserting a roll of perforated paper
into the sockets, and operating the pedals. Playing-directions appear
upon the roll as the music unwinds.
Thus the Pianola provides a substitute for the human fingers and
a correct technique for any piece.
The artistic effects and vivifying personality are given to the ren-
dition by the player, who controls the expression. It is entirely subject
to his will. He may vary the touch from the most delicate pianissimo
to the “ lion's touch of Rubinstein.” #
This human element is the distinctive feature of the Pianola, and
removes it from the ranks of purely mechanical musical instruments. It
taxes the credulity of all who have not heard it, but is substantiated by
the fact that the Pianola
is the only Piano-Player
endorsed by musicians
of note.
