The Mere Truth.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“You have had a great success, Vaunec,” said
the Englishman, in his staid, elderly voice; “a
succ&s fou, one might say.”
He looked up at the young man who was standing
beside a deeply-cushioned invalid’s chair, in which
lay a woman of between 40 and 50, thin and
fragile, but delicately sweet in tint as the silks
that swathed her.
“I? A great success?” questioned Max
Vauneo. He moved, so that he stood behind the
chair, out of its ocoupant’s sight, and then he
shook his head, meaningly, at the other man.
“ A great success ? ” he repeated ; “I don’t know
what you mean, Tresham.”
“Ah?” said the other, carelessly; “it must
have been some false rumour —perhaps the shadow
of a coming truth. We all expect a great success
from you, some of these days; don’t we, Miss
Cecil ? ” he appealed to the woman in the chair.
“ No ; Aunt Mary has ceased to hope anything
from me,” broke in Vauneo, before she could
reply. “ She knows just how little to expect; it
has kept her from being disappointed, so far.*
“ Yes, I’ve managed not to be disappointed in
him,” Mary Cecil said, glancing up at him with
proud, loving eyes.
“Seriously,” said Max, “it wouldn’t do to ex-
pect a great deal from me. I’m lazy, to begin
with.”
“Oh ! I didn’t expect you to write this great
work,’* said the other man, with a light emphasis
that made Max Vaunec move uncomfortably.
“I’m afraid I must be going, Aunt Mary, he
said to the invalid. “I’ll come back after dinner.
Mr Tresham is dining here, isn’t he ? And he s
an old friend who must have all kinds of versions
of old times to conjure up to you. So you won t
b6 ‘‘Neff'but COiflo back early, Max,” said the
weak, sweet voice. I want you to talk to Mr.
Troshftin.
“I’ll try,” he said. Then turning to Tresham
he added, “You could find those business ad-
dresses you wanted in my room—shall I show
you whore, in case I cannot get back early .
“ Thanks,” said Tresham, rising. They passed
together out of the light, flower-scented room into
fee dim, cool hall. ,
“ I see you haven’t told Miss Cecil about your
novel,” said the elder man, out there. “Thats
foolish ; she is sure to hear of it.”
“I don’t think so,” said Max, quickly. She
hardly ever sees visitors, except the few 1 bring
home; and I’ve warned them. And she never
goes out. Really, only a few people in Melbourne
know that I wrote ‘The Mere Truth.’ You are
an exception.” „ ~ rl , ,
“It’s a clever book, Vaunec, said Tresham.
“ I read it on the way out. Clever—but just a
little too-you know; eh? I donjt wonder you
don’t wish to see it in her hands. He nodded
towards the closed door.
A little flu o1 * crept up under Max Vaunec s
dark skin. , , ... , . ,
“ Yes, one reels that,” he agreed, with a boyish
eagerness. He was very young, for all his suc-
cessful novel. “ Yes, I can’t face her knowledge
of the thoughts, the things, that make up one s
experience. It isn’t so much the change it might
make in her attitude towards me; though that
mightn’t be easy to bear; but I shrink even more
from letting her associate in thought with—with
all that she is so innocent of, and that I had
to put into that book. You know the feeling,
don’t you ? She is sweet as she is, don t you think
so ’ She has lived her secluded life here ever since
I can remember her, in this corner, without ever
coming into contact with the soil of the world ;
and one doesn’t want her to now.”
“I understand,” the other man said, thought-
fullv He was much older than the young author,
whom he looked at reflectively for a moment.
' “ Don’t you think it’s a pity?” he continued—and
then broke off. ‘ ‘ But I’ll see you again, Vaunec.
I’ll go back now or she will wonder what we are
A few minutes *ater he was back in the sweet-
scented, dainty room, and Mary Cecil was looking
eagerly at him from her beautiful sunken eyes.
“ What do you think of my nephew ?’’ she was
asking. ‘ ‘ Isn’t a sick old maid lucky to have had
such a boy to bring up ? , <t T
»He seems a fine fellow,” agreed the man. I
hope you haven’t spoilt him.”
‘‘No- how could I? He has been to our best
school, to our University ; I’™ only *£* hl s “
vacations, and intervals like that. But those
times have made life bearable ; and now that he s
a man he makes his headquarters here with me,
of his own free will, and I see a great deal of him.
I think I am almost happier than I ve ever been
b6 “°Ever before ! ” echoed the man. He took a,
sudden resolve, and drew nearer, leaning forward
t °“Sii l clciV’ I he e said, “I come to Australia
almost wholly to bring a message to you.
“Yes?” she said, with a kind of breathless
miestion." There died from her eyes an unrestful
glitter, an excited avoidance of something she had
feared. It was no use avoiding it; it was here.
Her face changed from forced cheerfulness to
Ba ST« e ’' P Sme i °in Trestan', low tone,; “my
brother,’ who loved you, is dead. He wrote to
you before he died. He gaye me this letter and
this packet to be given to you with my own
hands. He never forgot those weeks when he and
you believed yourselves man and wife ; he never
forgot for an instant what was to him the torture
of losing you. He knew that the resolve which
brought you out here to your sister and her hus-
band was a wise one ; but he never ceased to feel
its cruelty. He lost his balance with the loss of
you. 1 It ruined him.” He paused a moment; the
thin outline beneath the silk draperies lay like
that of a corpse upon the long chair. His wife,
he went on, “is still alive. We found out how
shoTad tricked us into believing her dead, for her
own ends. He never saw her. You know he was
blameless in intention—to you ?
“I know,” she said.
“ That is all,” said the man.
“Thank you,” she answered. Then, with an
forcing herself to speak, she added, 1
fe “ed Sling like this.' Yet I tried not to
think of it—not to remember who you were. I
have always endeavoured to forget. It was
toother life, all that. I am different, I am
another creature, now. You can see-since my
accident—almost since I readied Australia, that is
have been -this! ” She let her eyes rest on her
own still limbs; laid along the chair.
The man bowed his head silently.
“And lying here,” she went on I have come
to hate the world and its ways. I want to forget
it. I shrink from it. The few people I ever see
seem to feel that, and they help to keep this
corner sacred, as it were. Nothing ugly comes
here to disturb, to repel; and. sometimes 1 f oo i
that to those few who come it is a kind of refuge
—as it is to me ; and I tell myself that my recoil
from those passionate days and my desperate care
to keep my secret, have been good and right; for
to those young men who are my fnends ana
Max’s, I am an influence for good. Perhaps it
they knew all about me I couldn’t be. Of course,
they must live, they must go through the tire.
But to keep them caring for something qui ot >
something unassociated with the soil of life tha
would be good.” . .
She stopped, shaken with the outpouring oi
long-pent thoughts.
“ Don’t you think I am right ? ” she asked, with
wistful pleading. “ I deceive Max, whom 1 love.
But isn’t it better so ? He thinks me an angel—
ignorant of all the wrongs of this world. Isnt it
better to keep this ideal in him ? It would kill
me to have that belief of his destroyed. Don t
you think I am right ? ” n
He hesitated a second before he said “Yes.
“You hesitated,” she cried. “Why? What
wrong can there be ? ”
“No wrong,” he replied, slowly, only some-
times deception breeds deception.”
She smiled a sad little smile. “ I know what
you mean,” she said. “ But there is no wrong in
that.” She drew from below her heaped-up
cushions a book, and held it towards him.
“It’s fine,” she said, “and true.„ One loves—
and fears—a man for work like this.”
It was Max’s book, “ The Mere Truth.
A.E.L.
The Jews, all over Australia, are beginning
an agitation for a reduction of their burial-fees.
These seem to claim adjustment, in fairness.
Christians bury three in a grave, but it is sternly
obligatory on a Jew only to have one, even if it
be a still-born child, whose grave costs £2 10s. m
many places. Then again, the Jews think they
ought not to be charged extra for Sunday burials,
as they cannot bury on Saturday.
At Kilmore (Vic.), recently, an old-age
pensioner was haled before the local aggregate
Pontius Pilate for having “insufficient visible
means of support ” —in other words, it was pro-
posed to gaol him on the ground that Government
didn’t give him enough to live on. The ordinary
J.P. would have enthusiastically awarded him
three months, but the P.M. chanced to be present,
and he ruled that the Law had decided the pension
to be sufficient, and the Law couldn’t call itsell
a liar without compromising its dignity.

of which is a further proof of the hollowness of

Gurr, the pious linen-draper who poses
Victoria’s Minister for Education, has at last got
himself into a tight place. His religiosity, or his
desire to please its professors, led him into pro-
mising a deputation that he would introduce a
Bill dealing with religious instruction in State
schools. This was in direct opposition to Cabinet
instructions. Religious instruction was one of the
first matters discussed by Peacock and co. when
they set to work seriously on their measures lor
the session, and it was decided without much
hesitation to leave the subject severely alone.
Peacock warned Gurr, wHen he saw that a crowd
of religious deputations was going to wait on
him of the decision of the Cabinet. l\otwith-
standing this reminder, Gurr blundered into a
hole The incident has caused much friction in
the Cabinet, and Gurr was given to understand by
Peacock that he would have to fall out unless he
exercised more control over his silly tongue. Ihe
only reason that Gurr is kept m the Cabinet is
because he carries David Syme’s stamp of ap-
proval.
Part of the sudden newspaper outburst
against the Vic. minimum-wage law is logical
enough, for when border tariffs are abohsned, the
high-wage Vic. manufacturer will be at a grievous
disadvantage unless the other States likewise
abolish sweating. But part of it is just greed
and sudden personal shock. A few months ago a
Printers’-wages Board was appointed. Ihe Board
has not yet completed its work, but it has got far
enough to touch the pockets of Victorian news-
paper proprietors. In the first place, the Board
determined to raise the price of machine-setting
from 3d. to 3|d. a thousand. Then there was a
suggestion that the “readers” on the big papers,
who are being shockingly paid at present, should
have their wages raised from 15 to 20 per cent.
As soon as these things became known there was
a move in the newspapers to rake up everything
calculated to create a bad impression against the
Act. Hundreds of reasons were found for the
suspension of the measure, in the vain hope that
it would never be enforced again. To a certain
extent the newspaper howlers have gained their
point The Peacock Government, under pres-
sure weakly consented to pass an order-m-council
empowering the Board to consider time an

NFLUENZ A is not generally understood by the greater part of those
who suffer from it. It is, in reality, an aggravated form of cold,
supplemented by a general weakness of the whole system. Ordinary
colds, it is well known, are caused by the congestion of the blood,
which, as a rule, is most prominent in the head or throat. Influenza
can be traced to the same cause, and irregular or insufficient liver
action is, in most cases, responsible for both. When the liver,
through any derangement of the digestive organism, ceases to act in
a proper manner, a certain amount of bile finds its way into the
blood, and, at the same time, the blood created from food consumed
is of a decidedly poor quality. In this manner the blood becomes
congested, the circulation sluggish, and a cold is the result. The
circulation of the blood now being much lower than usual, the entire
system is affected. The nervous organism becomes unstrung, the appetite poor, and the rest
disturbed. The body is extremely susceptible to chills, and the slightest draught causes a
violent attaok of coughing or sneezing. Many so-called cures for influenza fail to get at the
root of the trouble, and only afford temporary relief. A medicine that will keep the liver in
thorough and regular working order, and the digestion right, will, without doubt, prove the
most effective preventative and cure for influenza and colds. This is what Bile* Beans for
Biliousness will do, and no better remedy can be found for these ailments. Bile Beans have
proved m hundreds of cases to be the most effective known remedy in dealing with influenza,
colds, and all ailments arising from such complaints. Obtainable from all Chemists and
Storekeepers, price 13Jd. per Box, or from the Australian Depot, Bile Beans Manufac-
turing Co., 39 Pitt-st., Sydney.

The Cattle-Boats.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Four weeks from Monte Video,
And sights that fe w men sees—
A -prayin’’ that the clouds will blow
A healthy, spankin' breeze ;
With glass a-showin’, down below ,
A himdred odd degrees.
When God made out His shippin’ notes
And sent this world to sea,
He must have missed the cattle-boats
And cattle-men like me.
He meant all farms to be ashore,
Not sailin’ full and by,
With chokin’ bullocks sweatin’ gora
And layin’ down to die.
He didn’t authorise that hells
Should wander on His seas,
A-liftin’ to the swingin’ swells—
Such reekin’ hells as these,
That squatter out and tumble in
To be the shippers’ gain,
With cattle-keepers spoutin’ sin,
And cattle mad with pain.
We ships ’em at the River Plate,
And from the States they come,
With bleedin’ horns and starin’ hate—
Thank God, the brutes are dumb !
We rig up win’s’ls so’s to try
And purify the air ;
So if they go and drop and die,
That isn’t our affair.
The stokers sometimes feel that God
Is workin’ wonders near,
A-watchin’ by a fractured rod
That’s fighting Death and Fear.
But hoistin’ out the dead and maimed
And dodgin’ every roll,
A man might doubt, nor be ashamed,
If he has got a soul.
The pirates old that used to float,
A-steerin’ by the stars,
Would know a stinkin’ cattle-boat
Before they raised her spars.
By carcases and brimstone smell
And other smells they’d know
They’d struck a border state of Hell,
And they would let her go;
A-driftin’ with a silent screw
Or rollin’ big degrees,
With chokin’ scuppers all a-spue
A poisonin’ the seas.
The sharks they slink around her flanks—
The sharks are very wise ;
And oh ! they love the cattle-tanks
And every beast that dies.
We ain’t in Heaven’s shippin’ notes,
And God don’t surely know
That such dam’ things as cattle-boats
Are tradin’ to and fro—
A-plungin’ till their stock is piled
In heaps, all blood and hair,
And men are killed, to put it mild,
For facin’ Death too fair.
The coal-ships most are bound for Hell,
Where coal is rulin’ high;
The liner rings her dinner-bell
And swaggers stately by,
With passengers a-suckin’ hard
At pipes and strong cigars :
They seem to know a cattle-yard—
Perhaps it’s by our spars.
Pass rovmd the chain ! Now easy ! Oh,
What cheerful tasks are these /—
A-liftin' dead 'uns from below
And prayin' for a breeze.
God didn't mean that Hell should go
A-howlin' on His seas.
M.li.
QUILP N.
A Little Letter from London.
London, June, 1901.
My Dear Friends,—
London, London, London, London, London!
And June too, and the very houses out in
flower. Even the old brown London houses—and
such yellow sunshine and blue sky and clear, orisp
goldness in the days that I wonder London doesn’t
start a libel-action against Australia for taking
away its character.
Well, and London is like this then, is it?. Bern-
hardt and Rejane to be had for a shilling. Calve
and Eames and Melba, Paderewski and Kubolik,
and Ysaye and Buboni, Sargent and Solomon and
Shannon all round the corner, all in the next
street, or the next again, instead of a world away.
And Ireland and Scotland and France so close that
you are there in a few hours.
That’s what paralyses an Australian, the near-
ness of Paris, of Scotland, Ireland, Switzerland.
At present lam mad on Rejane. Nobody knows
how to do anything but the French. Last night
I saw this great actress play Sapho. She has just
come over, bringing her entire company from the
Vaudeville Theatre, Paris. London is wild over
her, seeing in her Bernhardt’s only successor.
Poor Bernhardt! Rejane stole away her husband,
and now that her husband is dead, she is stealing
her laurels—which are much less easy to steal.
Rejane’s Sapho is so acted and so staged that
you do not look at, but live in, the scenes por-
trayed. Simplicity and consummate naturalness
remove all suspicion of acting. They come, talk,
eat, weep, quarrel, come and go, these Frenchmen
and girls, unconscious that the blind is up, and
thousands of eyes are peering in. Rejane comes
creeping into the first act; in Jean’s room—in a
heavy, black, glittering frock, falling off her
shoulders, but covered for the moment with
a heavier and still more glittering black
cloak, whose hood is drawn above the head
and tied with a stiff bow that sticks out over th«
auburn hair and white face like the horns of a
devil. How she moves, this womar ! Her figure
is inordinately Parisian. At the back it curves in
from neck to waist ; then goes rolling out in a
great round that continues till the hips are in-
cluded and passed. This abnormal curve comes
off and on. It is the fashion just now to keep it
t ! ee 1* , on a ny of the Regent-street
modwtes. In front her figure bears evidence of
the straight corset that doesn’t let the waist press
in at the waist. 1
thin, white, scraggy face, a big, red
mouth, heavy-lidded brown eyes, and the triumph
wl, f - and dre and cllarm over beauty—this is
what Rejane gives you.
When she kisses Jean her white face goes shades
Her acting doesn’t stop at the kiss j she
acts through it and after it, coming back from a
kiss a changed woman, with another face, another
expression. The play isn’t shocking in the least.
It s so utterly French, and is acted in such a
French homely way, that it could not offend any-
one hkely to be there. Rejane is great in two
ü bh ® persuades you beyond all shadow of
doubt that whatever she does could not be done
any other way ; and she leaves you to discover her
ail r~. b now her for days and days afterwards.
It is only to-day that I see that Sapho was corn-
mon. I remember now how Rejane was never
very elegant ” with her hands and fingers ; how
she got her dress twisted about her when she
knelt on the sofa ; how she laughed and sang. At
the time I vaguely felt there was something about
Rejane that I did not like.
She leaves you to discover her, just as you are
leftt° discover women you meet in “ real life.”
Mrs. Pat. Campbell models herself on Rejane.
1 saw her in “Maziana,” a Spanish play, with
litheradge doing lover. Mrs. Pat. is thin, dark,
Bweet, and affected. She has a habit of sniffing,
and a laugh smothered at the back of her nose,
with the lips kept shut, is a feature of her acting
that irritates. Beryl Faber also laughed that way
I noticed. Poor Mrs. Pat. worked hard, and was
magnificently frocked, but her house was almost
empty. I am sorry to say that our own Tith.
can fbe regarded as a draw. He seems sadly mis-
placed. His method, too, is glaringly opposed to
Mrs. Pat’s. She drops her speeches off as if she
He delivers his in a reciting voice.
When he recites his love to her, and she lets
fall little naturally-uttered words, the incon-
gruity irritates one out of all sympathy with them
both. It is a pity to see Tith. so misfitted. Can
you imagine him masquerading as a Spanish lover
whom everyone calls Dan-y-ell ? All the same,
out of the whole co. he looks the real man—looks
like daffodils and Australia, a nice, quiet good
look.
Mrs. Pat’s frocks were marked with the new
feature of all good frocks, short wide sash tied at
the back in a big fat bow. Beryl Faher looked
charmingly pretty and cool in empire frocks, and
big flat hats. Another member of the Spanish
menage has also been in Sydney, Miss Italia
Conti. She brought in Mrs. Pat’s cloak once,
and shut the door once, as far as I can remember.
A play that society is making much of is
Esmond’s comedy, “ The Wilderness,” with
George Alexander playing lover to dainty Eva
Moore. I confess George Alexander makes me
tired. I hate his love-making. Perhaps I look at
it from a man’s point of view, for all women adore
him, and the only word against him comes from
men. He is sq sentimental, so Family HERALD-ish.
He sits in an arm chair and Eva Moore comes and
sits on the arm of it. In the play Eva is George’s
wife, and they are still dla honeymoon. She draws
his head over towards her—and he gets into an
extraordinary attitude. It may be natural, hut one
doesn’t like to see it on the; stage. He crouches
sideways till she is leaning round him and he is
looking up back at her, and. her head is on his side
and his is on hers. That sounds complicated, hut
he looks quite happy in this silly pose with Eva
looking for grey hairs. The Drawing Room is
written all over this popular actor, considered by
many The Ideal Lover.
“ The Wilderness,” by the author of “ One Sum-
mer’s Day,” that pretty slight thing the Broughs
played a couple years ago. It marks the present
English craze. Bernard Shaw says everything
has to be labelled in England, so I feel quite
justified in putting a label on what England likes
best just now in Art, Literature, Drama.
For want of a better word I shall make use of
the epithet “ idyllic.” You can call it wishy-
washy, if you prefer.
Where do I see it ?
In the pictures at the Academy, in the plays at
the theatre, in the hats in the park, in the books
on the bookstalls, in the stories in the magazines.
What about the hats? Well, there are straw-
berries and cherries, and black daisies with white
hearts, and strings of chiffon and streamers of
lace or chiffon hanging yards down the back.
And they are wearing here a hat that nobody
would patronise in Sydney, the frilly pink, blue
or white muslin. And all the hat ribbons are
spotted, black spotted in whi ce, or white spotted
in blaok, put on in a low boW. And the hats are
wide and crinkled round the brim, ever so idyllic.
But perhaps the most “ idyllic ” thing I have seen
here was Ethel Haydon singing at the Palaoe. The
silliest song in the whole wide world, with a refrain
“Cuckoo, cuckoo.” She looked pretty in a
black jet frock with long sleeves and low bodice,
but was merely one of the performers. She sang
her little song in a little voice, and repeated the
last verse, and was gone, leaving no impression at
all behind her.
I could rattle on and on, but if I don’t stop now
I shall be telling you something about the King
that might end in my being beheaded for high
treason—for even the King is an Idyl these days.
—Good-bye and farewell,
L.M.
Query : Suppose the authority of the
Inter-State Commission is limited to preventing
unfair competition and differential rates on rail-
ways, what is to prevent Victoria, instead of
attracting western N.S.W. wool to Melbourne by
giving specially-low railway charges on such wool
when carted to its railway termini on the Murray,
turning common carrier and bullock-driver ? In
other words—suppose that it charged the western
N.S.W. squatter the usual wool rates on its rail-
way line, but carried his wool to the Vic. railway
line in the Vic. Government’s gratis bullock-dray.
N.S.W. couldn’t shut out the bullock-dray of a
friendly State; the Inter-State Commission would
have no power to interfere with mere road-traffic,
and the concession would probably cost Victoria
no more than the present differential railway
rate does.
When any friendless unfortunate is picked
up dying in the Melbourne streets the first
impulse is to get him along to the big Melb. Hos-
pital as soon as possible, but it happens with
dreadful regularity that the hospital is full, and
the door is figuratively slammed in the face of the
poor patient. Yet another recent instance
when a deserted old man with one foot in the
grave was wheeled along by a policeman and
couldn’t be taken in, because the institution was
even more overcrowded than usual. The Alfred
Hospital, a couple of miles further on, was
strongly recommended to the poor old fellow as a
good place to shuffle off in, but ere he could be
carted so far, his other foot had got over the
margin, and he was just wheeled round to be put
m the morgue instead. Gruesome, but that’s how
they have managed these things in Melbourne for
many years.
